Validation/verification 
body 
Organization and contact name with email address and phone number  
History of CCB status This is the first CCB Validation of the project. 
Gold Level criteria GL Climate Benefits  
North Kalimantan faces escalating climate risks driven by rising 
temperatures, increasingly erratic rainfall, extended dry seasons, and 
sea-level rise. In the absence of the project, continued peat drainage 
and mangrove degradation would intensify fires, flooding, saline 
intrusion, and livelihood instability. By maintaining high peat water 
tables, restoring mangrove ecosystems, and promoting community-led 
forest stewardship, the project delivers measurable ecosystem-based 
adaptation benefits. These actions enhance hydrological resilience, 
reduce fire and coastal hazards, and strengthen community and 
biodiversity adaptation capacity. Through these outcomes, the project 
meets the CCB Climate Gold Level criteria by addressing key regional 
climate stressors while improving the adaptive capacity and long-term 
well-being of local communities and ecosystems. 
 
GL Biodiversity Benefits 
The project will meet the Gold Level criteria for exceptional biodiversity 
benefits as it hosts 28 globally threatened species, such as the 
Proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus), Irrawaddy dolphin (Orcaella 
brevirostris) and Storm's stork (Ciconia stormi). Through forest 
protection and restoration of degraded peatland and mangroves, the 
project will enhance habitat quality, strengthen biodiversity 
conservation, and reduce threats to these species. 
Expected verification 
schedule  
February 2027 
Prepared by Fairatmos

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
Outcome or impact estimated by the end of project lifetime Section reference 
1) 6,755 ha of peatland area will be protected from land use 
conversion and restored (excluding the Community Partnership Area 
area). Forest patrol activities and fire prevention will be executed to 
minimize threats from the surrounding region.  
2.1.17, 3.2.2 
2) 524.58 ha of mangrove ecosystems are expected to be protected 
and restored primarily through rehabilitation efforts throughout the 
grouped project’s lifetime.  
2.1.17, 3.2.2 
3) Structured and transparent community support will enable 
households to access sustainable livelihood and public service 
improvements. 
2.1.17 
4) Critical habitats for globally threatened species will be safeguarded 
ensuring the protection of trigger species such as the Proboscis 
Monkey and Storm’s Stork. 
2.1.17

1.2 Standardized Benefit Metrics 
4
 Improved forest management (IFM) - Activities that change forest management practices and increase carbon stock on forest 
lands managed for wood products such as saw timber, pulpwood, and fuelwood (VCS Program Definitions) 
3
 Afforestation, reforestation and revegetation (ARR) - Activities that increase carbon stocks in woody biomass (and in some cases 
soils) by establishing, increasing and/or restoring vegetative cover through the planting, sowing and/or human-assisted natural 
regeneration of woody vegetation (VCS Program Definitions) 
2
 Reduced emissions from deforestation and forest degradation (REDD) - Activities that reduce GHG emissions by slowing or 
stopping conversion of forests to non-forest land and/or reduce the degradation of forest land where forest biomass is lost (VCS 
Program Definitions) 
1
 Land with woody vegetation that meets an internationally accepted definition (e.g., UNFCCC, FAO, or IPCC) of what constitutes a 
forest, which includes threshold parameters, such as minimum forest area, tree height and level of crown cover, and may include 
mature, secondary, degraded and wetland forests (VCS Program Definitions)

8
  Based on the percentage of people who expressed interest in participating in livelihood activities during community consultations, 
this figure was extrapolated to represent the wider population of the project zone communities. 
7
 Livelihoods are the capabilities, assets (including material and social resources) and activities required for a means of living 
(Krantz, Lasse, 2001. The Sustainable Livelihood Approach to Poverty Reduction. SIDA). Livelihood benefits may include benefits 
reported in the Employment metrics of this table.
 
6
 Full time equivalency is calculated as the total number of hours worked (by full-time, part-time, temporary and/or seasonal staff) 
divided by the average number of hours worked in full-time jobs within the country, region or economic territory (adapted from the 
UN System of National Accounts (1993) paragraphs 17.14[15.102];[17.28])

 Employed in project activities means people directly working on project activities in return for compensation (financial or otherwise),

work.

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14; CCB, G1.2) 
The Nunukan Mangrove & Peatland Project (NMPP) aims to protect and restore the vast peatland, 
mangrove, and swamp forests from the threat of conversion into timber logging for large-scale pulp 
and paper production, palm oil plantation, and aquaculture, as the main deforestation drivers in North 
Kalimantan and broader Kalimantan Island. It is a grouped project with a total area of 15,591 hectares 
within Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan, Indonesia, separated into two different concessions with 
distinctive ecosystems in each, i.e. BHK I and BHK II. The 1st project instance is 10,980 ha, out of 
which the crediting area is 8,362 ha, and it’s located in both BHK I and II (as can be seen in Figure 9). 
The 2nd instance, i.e. the project’s expansion area, will cover mangrove (forest cover) areas that are 
found in BHK I (approximately 2,866 ha). The project also aims to strengthen local communities, over 
24,427 individuals that reside within 4 communities in the project zone, through inclusive participation, 
improved public services, and the development of sustainable livelihoods, ensuring economic 
resilience and social well-being. At the same time, it will safeguard biodiversity by conserving High 
Conservation Value (HCV) areas, restoring degraded habitats, and protecting threatened species, 
while proactively enhancing ecosystem resilience to climate change; the vast area of NMPP is home 
to over 28 RTE (Rare, Threatened, and Endangered) species, including rich bird biodiversity due to 
its proximity to the Sebuku-Sebangau Important Bird Area (IBA). 
NMPP (both BHK I and II concessions) falls under the management of PT Bumi Hijau Konservasi (PT 
BHK), a company engaged in Nature-based Solutions (NbS). PT BHK aims to contribute to 
environmental conservation and ecological balance through a strong commitment to environmental 
management, community engagement, and research & innovation. PT BHK has a principal permit for 
the two concession areas, and is currently finalizing its Forestry Utilization Business License (PBPH) 
with the Indonesian Ministry of Forestry (Kemenhut), which covers two land units: PT BHK I, covering 
4,424.31 hectares predominantly consisting of mangrove forests, and PT BHK II, covering 11,164.29 
hectares, primarily composed of peatland forests. Both units are in Nunukan Regency, North 
Kalimantan Province. 
The PBPH license will provide land tenure security for up to 90 years
13
. This project aims to pursue 
certification under Verra VM0007 for Emission Reductions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation 
(REDD), including the Avoiding Planned Deforestation Module, as well as VM0007 for Wetland 
Restoration and Conservation (WRC), specifically focused on peatland conservation. Additionally, it 
seeks certification under VM0047 for Afforestation, Reforestation, and Revegetation (ARR) and 
VM0033 for Tidal Wetland (Mangrove) Restoration. By preventing forest conversion under the 
13
  PP No. 23 Tahun 2021. (n.d.). Database Peraturan | JDIH BPK. https://peraturan.bpk.go.id/Details/161853/pp-no-23-tahun-2021

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Industrial Plantation/Hutan Tanaman Industri (HTI) permit and ensuring the protection status of the 
forest, along with the restoration of peatland and mangrove areas in its place, this project will make a 
significant contribution to emission reductions and removals, with a total of up to 14 million tons of 
CO2-equivalent during its 40 years lifetime, or 355,276 tCO2-e annually. The project is not located 
within the jurisdiction of a REDD+ programme.

2.1.4 Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.1, 3.6, 3.8, 3.18, 4.1; CCB Program Rules, 4.2.4, 4.6.4) 
According on the criteria outlined in Appendix 1 Eligible AFOLU Project Categories in the VCS 
Standard version 4.7, the proposed project area is eligible for both VCS and CCB programs due to 
the following reasons: 
1. The project fully complies with both the general requirements outlined in Section 3.1 
and the AFOLU-specific criteria detailed in Section 3.2 of the VCS Standard Version 
4.7. 
2. The project intends to stop and reduce the deforestation of the area as it is threatened 
for conversion of forest into Industrial Timber Plantation where the natural forest system 
is clear-cut and replaced by a different system that has lower carbon stock that aligns 
with the eligibility of REDD – Avoiding Planned Deforestation (APD) eligibility based on 
Section 3.2.1 and A1.5 and A1.8 of VCS Standard Version 4.7. The project baseline 
indicates that ~11,164 hectares area would have been converted into an Industrial 
14
 See Appendix 1 of the VCS Standard
 
21 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Timber Concession. This baseline is in full compliance with the methodological 
requirements outlined in Section 3.2.1 of the VCS Standard, Version 4.7.  
3. The project intends to increase vegetative cover on areas that have been degraded into 
non-forest areas (e.g. grasslands, bare lands, and wood shrubs) through planting and 
human-assisted natural regeneration, thus aligns with the eligibility of ARR project 
activity with a size of ~934 hectares based on Section 3.2.1 and A1.1 of VCS Standard 
Version 4.7. 
4. The project aims to enhance net GHG removals through wetland restoration and reduce 
emissions by preventing wetland degradation. It combines AFOLU activities with 
wetland restoration and conservation, qualifying as a WRC combined project comprising 
CIW–APWD (~6,149 ha) and ARR–RWE (~143 ha). The project aligns with the eligibility 
criteria under Section 3.2.1, A1.13–A1.16 of the VCS Standard, Version 4.7. 
5. The project has a lifetime of 40 years, with the option for renewal not exceeding 100 
years of total project lifetime, as permitted under subsection 3.9.3 of the VCS Standard, 
Version 4.7. 
6. The period between the project start date and its pipeline listing process complies with 
the three-year requirement & also the period between the project start date and its 
validation complies with the five-year requirement. These requirements are specified in 
subsection 3.8.2 and 3.8.4 of the VCS Standard, Version 4.7. 
7. The project has applied a current, valid, and approved methodology to calculate its 
greenhouse gas (GHG) reductions, ensuring a conservative approach as required by 
subsections 3.1.1 and 3.1.2 of the VCS Standard, Version 4.7. 
8. The project has successfully demonstrated its compliance with the additionality 
requirements outlined in Section 3.14 of the VCS Standard, Version 4.7. 
2.1.5 Transfer Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.23) 
The project is not registered under other GHG programs, as such transfer eligibility conditions are not 
applicable for this project.

2.1.9 Project Ownership (VCS, 3.2, 3.7, 3.10; CCB, G5.8) 
PT Bumi Hijau Konservasi (BHK) is a limited liability company headquartered in Jakarta, Indonesia 
whose business is mainly in the utilization of environmental services, a type of forest utilization 
business permit (Perizinan Berusaha Pemanfaatan Hutan/PBPH) permissible in the country. The 
company is one of the four subsidiaries of Green Legacy Conservation (GLC) Group, all striving to, as 
quoted from the mission statement, “foster a harmonious relationship between economic 
33 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
development and environmental preservation by implementing innovative and sustainable practices 
across our operations.” 
PT BHK is in the final process of obtaining legal rights to manage the project area. It has gone 
through several stages of application. In January 2023, the company submitted a letter titled “Request 
for Recommendations and Technical Considerations for the Management of Business Licensing for 
Utilization of Production Forests (PBPH) related to Multi-Business Forestry Sector and Ecosystem 
Restoration, covering a total area of approximately ±18,545 hectares,” numbered 005/BHK/I/2023. In 
response, the Investment and One-Stop Integrated Service Office (Dinas Penanaman Modal dan 
Pelayanan Terpadu Satu Pintu/DPMPTSP) of North Kalimantan Province issued the 
“Recommendation for Business Licensing Application for PBPH on behalf of PT BHK” on February 
2023, numbered 757/1/R.PBPH-HP/DPMPTSP.III/II/2023. The Indonesia Investment Coordinating 
Board (Badan Koordinasi Penanaman Modal/BKPM) followed suit by issuing the “Approval of 
Business Licensing Commitment for Forest Utilization (PBPH) in Production Forest on behalf of PT 
Bumi Hijau Konservasi in Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan Province,” one for each of the area 
(BHK I & II) on August 2023, numbered 16082311116503004 and 18082311116503006. The project 
has been granted an AMDAL exemption (B.633/E/PLA.6.1/10/2025) for BHK II and has secured 
Environmental Management Effort and Environmental Monitoring Effort approvals (UKL-UPL) for both 
BHK I and II from the Ministry of Environment numbered 1238 year 2025 and 2145 year 2025. 
Currently, the company is still waiting for the issuance of a Ministerial Decree (Surat Keputusan/SK) 
that will authorize the management rights of the project area to PT BHK. 
In terms of regulation, the Government of Indonesia (GoI) has legislated Law No. 11/2020 on Job 
Creation; in which the first reference to and the mandate to transition to the Multi-Forest Businesses 
(Multi Usaha Kehutanan/MUK) concept was found. This concept enables logging activities (timber 
harvesting and utilization) to operate in tandem with the utilization of non-timber forest products 
(NTFPs) and environmental services. The law was followed and elaborated further by Government 
Regulation No. 23/2021 on Forestry Management and the Ministry of Environment and Forestry 
(MoEF) regulation No. 8/2021 which discern the technicalities of MUK, utilization of ecosystem 
services (including carbon sequestration/reduction), and related activities. To address carbon trading 
and project development in particular, the Indonesia government has issued Presidential Regulation 
No. 110/2025 on the Implementation of Carbon Economic Value Instruments and National 
Greenhouse Gas Emission Control.

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
Topography 
Based on the analysis of Digital Elevation Model (DEM) data from the Shuttle Radar Topography 
Mission (SRTM) with a 30-meter resolution, the project area is dominated by gently undulating terrain, 
with the elevation range of -53 meters (water bodies) to 222 meters above sea level (m ASL). The 
topography map is presented in Figure 1 below. 
 
Figure 1. Topography Class Map in the Project Zone
15
 
 
The slope classes from the spatial analysis are divided into five categories: flat (0-8%), sloppy 
(8-15%), slightly steep (15-25%), steep (25-45%), and very steep (>45%) (Arsyad, 1989). About 
92.61% of the study area falls within the flat slope class, covering an area of 105,024.34 hectares 
(Figure 2). The very steep slope class covers an area of 46.28 hectares (0.04%). More detailed slope 
classes are described in Table 2. 
15
The project name shown on this and subsequent maps reflects an earlier version of the project title. All future maps and related 
materials will be updated to display the correct and final project name: “Nunukan Mangrove & Peatland Project”.

Soil 
Based on 1987 RePPProT (Regional Physical Planning Program for Transmigration) data, six soil 
types are distributed across the landscape. The majority of the soil in the project area is dominated by 
Dystric Histosols, covering 61.76% of the project area (9,628.48 ha) and 32.60% of the landscape 
(37,983.81 ha). Additionally, Humic Gleysols are also dominant, covering 29.89% of the project area 
(4,659.84 ha) and 19.90% of the landscape (23,187.04 ha). Meanwhile, Orthic Acrisols occupy only 
2.92% of the project area (455.35 ha) and 13.40% of the landscape (15,607.93 ha). 
About 5.43% of the project area (846.93 ha) and 16.19% of the landscape (18,858.51 ha) lack soil 
data, thus classified as "No Data." Thionic Fluvisols are found in 4.45% of the landscape (5,188.85 
ha) but are not identified within the project area. Orthic Luvisols and Cambic Arenosols are barely 
identified, covering only a very small area (16.16 ha and 84.95 in the landscape). Table 4 below 
summarizes the distribution of soil types in the project zone, which is displayed in Figure 4 
afterwards.

Climate 
The classification of climate types is used to group an area into the same climate type. A commonly 
used climate classification system is the Schmidt–Ferguson classification. Schmidt–Ferguson utilizes 
a comparison value (Q) between the average number of dry months (퐶퐻푏푘) and the average number 
of wet months (퐶퐻푏푏). Based on the rainfall data for Nunukan Regency, the average monthly rainfall 
exceeds 100 mm (see Table 5). This indicates that each month can be categorized as a wet month. 
Table 5. Schmidt-Ferguson Climate Classification 
Climate Type Vegetation Criterion 
Very Wet Tropical rainforest 0 < Q < 0.143 
Wet Tropical rainforest 0.143 < Q < 0.333 
Slightly Wet Forests, leaves falling during the dry season 0.333 < Q < 0.600 
Moderate Seasonal forest 0.600 < Q < 1.000 
Slightly Dry Savanna 1.000 < Q < 1.670 
Dry Savanna 1.670 < Q < 3.000 
Very Dry Grassland 3.000 < Q < 7.000 
Extremely Dry Grassland 7.000 < Q 
Source: Lakitan 2002

Based on calculations using the Schmidt & Ferguson method, with the highest average rainfall 
recorded in September (282.23 mm) and the lowest average in February (113.85 mm), the PBPH 
area of PT Bumi Hijau Konservasi falls under the moderately wet climate type, with a Q value ranging 
from 33.3% (0.333) to 60% (0.6). Details of the average monthly rainfall are provided in Table 6 
below. Based on the rainfall distribution pattern, Nunukan is classified as Equatorial Type (Badan

Hydrology 
The project area is located within the Sebuku Watershed, with part of the project zone falling within 
the Sembakung River Basin (Figure 5, Table 7). All rivers flowing within the project area ultimately 
discharge into the Sebuku River to the south. The main rivers traversing the PT BHK concession are 
the Pembeliangan River, Sumbal River, and Sebaung River. The concession area of PT BHK is 
situated in the downstream region of the watershed, making it prone to high surface runoff 
accumulation or flooding. Additionally, flooding may also originate from runoff from the tributaries of 
the Sembakung River, which flow south of PT BHK's concession boundary. The flood risk is further 
exacerbated by land cover conditions, including open land and shrub areas upstream of the Sebuku 
River.

Periodic flooding is a regular occurrence in this area, particularly during the rainy season. Floodwater 
levels can reach 0.5–2 meters, receding only a day after the rain stops. Flooding and inundation are 
caused by flat land conditions, geological depressions and lowlands, river sedimentation, high rainfall, 
tidal river effects, and deforestation in upstream areas. Deeper inundations typically occur in swampy 
lowlands, basins, and floodplains. 
 
Rivers within and surrounding the project area are also influenced by tidal phenomena. The tidal 
current energy in this region is equal to or slightly stronger than the river's current energy. The highest 
tides occur during the dry season, reaching +3 meters, while the lowest tides occur during the rainy 
season, at +0.5 meters. The tidal pattern is "semi-diurnal," meaning there are two high tides and two 
low tides within 24 hours. The hydrological conditions are illustrated in the figure below. 
43

(right)

Regarding the landform, BHK I is dominated by Fluvio-Marine Plains with some Peat Plains in the 
central area. Meanwhile, BHK II is dominated by Peat Plains, bordered by Fluvio-Marine Plains along 
the banks of the Pembeliangan River in the south. To the west, it is bordered by Fluvial Plains 
(flat-lowland), to the north by Fluvio-Volcanic Plains, and to the east by Karst Plains (flat-undulating) 
extending to Karst Mountains (Hardansyah et al., 2016).  
Fluvio-Marine Plains are land areas influenced by tidal seawater. Key characteristics of this region 
include muddy soil, abundant mangrove vegetation, and common land use as fish ponds. The mud in 
these landforms is derived from sediment transported by river flows and deposited by coastal currents 
and sea waves. These plains are generally found in coastal areas with relatively calm waters or small 
waves. 
The diversity of plant species in the study area corresponds to its ecosystems. In the peatland forest, 
there are protected plant species such as red balau/belangeran (Shorea balangeran, VU), meranti 
rawa/meranti daun halus (Shorea teysmanniana, CR), ramin tree (Gonystylus bancanus, VU), and 
pantung/jelutung rawa (Dyera polyphylla, VU). In the mangrove forest ecosystem, there is the rare 
species Camptostemon philippinensis (EN). Meanwhile, in mineral soil forests, protected species 
include dedaru (Cantleya corniculata, VU) and Bornean ironwood/ulin (Eusideroxylon zwageri, 
VU).

2.1.15 Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
Main Settlements 
Based on administrative boundaries, the villages within the project area fall under three districts in 
Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan Province. These districts are Nunukan District, Sebuku District, 
and Tulin Onsoi District. However, there is one hamlet, Sebakis which although administratively part 
of the village of Pembeliangan, is considered a distinct settlement due to its significant geographical 
distance from the village center and the notable disparity in development between the two areas. 
Pembeliangan demonstrates relatively more advanced infrastructure and public services, while 
Sebakis Hamlet remains considerably less developed. This separation, both in terms of physical 
accessibility and socio-economic conditions, has contributed to differences in the needs, priorities, 
and daily challenges faced by the respective communities.  Additionally, Sebakis community 
members currently operate illegal palm oil farms within BHK’s concession boundaries thus 
requiring a special focus from the project in terms of engagement and benefit-sharing. As such, 
any community engagement, development planning, or intervention efforts recognizes  Sebakis 
as a separate and contextually unique entity to ensure that its specific circumstances and 
development gaps are adequately addressed. The list of villages/hamlet surrounding the project area 
is presented in Table 9 below.

consulting with KPH and local village leaders it was determined that they are not affected by or could 
affect the project. For example, the village of Binusan was excluded from the project zone as KPH 
indicated that the communities do not engage in any activities in or close to the project area, and after 
meeting with the village’s leader it was clear that the only activity that could be taking place in the 
project zone is a swallows nest business which was however deemed as not probable as no such 
structures were observed.

Land Use 
According to local representatives, the indigenous people of Nunukan Regency, particularly the 
Dayak tribe, were initially highly dependent on nature. Their daily lives revolved around meeting basic 
needs by fishing in the sea or rivers and clearing land for farming fields and growing fruit. At first, their 
farming and fishing activities were solely for family subsistence. Land clearing for farming was 
typically done near riverbanks to facilitate access to the farmland. At that time, the only means of 
transportation was by boat along the rivers compared to cars and bikes that are also used in more 
recent years. 
Farming on cleared land usually lasted for one to three planting cycles, with each cycle taking a year. 
Thus, these lands were worked for only one to three years. Afterward, the land was either left to rest 
or converted into orchards, timber plantations, or other types of plantations. This shifting cultivation 
system meant that any land previously cleared became the property of the person who cleared it, 
according to local customary law. As a result, indigenous people often own large areas of land, 
sometimes reaching tens of hectares. 
With the introduction of transmigration programs and the establishment of plantation companies 
(mainly oil palm), which utilized significant amounts of land, the practice of shifting cultivation began 
to decline due to diminishing land availability. A rotational farming system, involving farming the same 
land two to three times before moving to new land, started to develop. On the new land, the same 
process occurred: farming for two to three cycles before moving again or returning to previously 
farmed land that was considered fertile once more. Many farmers now plant plantation crops, 
particularly oil palm and various fruit trees, after the first planting cycle. This is because plantation 
crops are seen as more productive, profitable, and easier to maintain. Consequently, it is not 
surprising that many residents now cultivate plantation crops, especially oil palm. 
Over time, land prices have risen sharply as agricultural land becomes scarcer. In the past, acquiring 
land simply involved clearing forest, but now residents must purchase land from others or inherit it 
from their families. Land has also become a form of long-term investment due to its continually 
increasing value. 
Land is an integral part of the community's life, especially for those whose livelihoods depend on 
farming and plantations. Beyond the previously mentioned benefits, land also provides herbal 
medicines, space for livestock and fish farming, building materials, animal feed, a source of income 
(cash), and more. Land is also used for housing, social facilities, education, worship, roads, and other 
purposes. 
Historical Conditions 
Before the 1980s, the residents were primarily indigenous people who practiced shifting cultivation by 
clearing forested land. Land clearing was concentrated around riverbanks, as rivers served as the 
main transportation routes to access new farmland. The primary purpose of this land clearing was to 
meet subsistence family food needs. To open land for farming, residents only needed approval or 
48 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
acknowledgment from the village head (lurah) at that time, and followed local customary regulations. 
Clearing was usually done by burning after initial felling, to expedite the process. Farming followed a 
shifting cultivation system, supplemented by planting fruit trees at the start of farming activities. The 
land was primarily used for growing upland rice, alongside the cultivation of secondary crops 
(intercropping/tumpang sari system) such as:  vegetables, tubers, rhizomes, and fruits. 
By the early 1980s, some residents began planting rubber trees on their farmland. At the time, rubber 
fetched a good price and could be harvested daily, providing a steady source of income. Meanwhile, 
agricultural produce and fishing activities mainly served to meet family needs, particularly for 
residents of Pembeliangan and Semunad villages. 
During this period, oil palm plantations, Industrial Plantations (Hutan Tanaman Industri/HTI), and 
government transmigration programs were introduced. The arrival of companies and transmigrant 
populations from outside Kalimantan led to rapid population growth in the four study villages, 
increasing ethnic diversity. On one hand, these developments improved infrastructure and economic 
conditions for local communities. On the other hand, they reduced the availability of land for 
agriculture and settlement. Shifting cultivation gradually transitioned to rotational farming and wet rice 
cultivation. 
Additionally, with many residents taking jobs in the companies, much of the land remained untended. 
The high price of oil palm and the ease of selling the produce due to nearby companies encouraged 
residents to convert their farmland into oil palm plantations. Many rubber plantations and traditional 
farmlands were thus transformed into oil palm plantations, especially as rubber prices became less 
lucrative. 
By the 2010s until now, development has become more modern, with improved infrastructure, diverse 
livelihoods, and new economic opportunities. These changes are driven by the operations of 
companies within the communities, fostering new businesses, the creation of markets for goods and 
services in several villages, and infrastructure improvements, particularly roads. 
Continuing the trends from the previous period and in the absence of alternatives, several residents 
have taken jobs in oil palm companies, leaving them with limited time to farm their land. The decline 
in rubber prices has further motivated residents to convert much of their land into oil palm plantations. 
Oil palm cultivation is appealing due to its low maintenance requirements, minimal time investment, 
and monthly harvesting schedule. This allows residents to engage in other side businesses, such as 
farming, working as company employees, trading, and more. As a result, it is not surprising that most 
residents now have multiple livelihoods. 
Socio-cultural information 
Ethnicity & Religion 
The ethnic groups in the project zone can be classified into two main categories: indigenous and 
migrant ethnic groups. The indigenous groups in the region are the Dayak Tidung ethnic group 
49 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
(primarily in Nunukan Barat and Pembeliangan Village) and the Dayak Agabag ethnic group (primarily 
in Semunad Village). Meanwhile, the migrant ethnic groups originate from Java (Javanese and 
Sundanese), Buginese, Batak, Flores, Malay, Chinese-Indonesian, Torajan, and others. 
The Dayak Tidung ethnic group and other migrant ethnic groups, such as Javanese, Bugis, 
Sundanese, Malays, and others, are predominantly Muslim. On the other hand, the Dayak Agabag 
ethnic group is primarily Catholic and Protestant. Despite their differing beliefs, these two ethnic 
groups coexist harmoniously in each village. Up until the time of this study, residents reported no 
issues of inter-ethnic or religious conflict (SARA) that could disrupt social harmony in the area.

Basic Needs Fulfillment 
In Indonesia, basic needs can be categorized into three primary kinds: clothing, housing, and food 
and clean water. For clothing, the residents of the villages meet their needs through purchasing. 
Currently, no materials are specifically sourced from nature for clothing. This is partly because such 
materials are becoming increasingly scarce and also because buying clothes is easier and more 
practical, thanks to improved access to affordable goods and services in each village in the project 
zone. 
For housing, most residents obtain building materials and other household needs, including furniture, 
by purchasing them from nearby markets. There are few residents who still gather wood from forests. 
Those who do are usually people from other areas who come to the studied locations specifically to 
collect wood and sell it elsewhere, including as far as Tarakan. The local residents prefer to purchase 
materials due to the ease, practicality, and convenience of accessing goods and services, as well as 
their busy work schedules. 
The majority of food needs, including carbohydrates, vegetables, fruits, and protein, are fulfilled 
through purchases from Java, Nunukan, and Malaysia. Changes in land use from agriculture to 
50 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
plantations and the establishment of companies in the community have caused a shift in professions 
for some residents—from farmers to company employees. Consequently, much of the land in their 
vicinity has been neglected and converted into plantations (mainly oil palm), as plantations are 
perceived to be easier to manage than food farming. As a result, food supplies, including vegetables, 
fruits, and carbohydrates, are now primarily obtained through purchases. 
For protein sources like fish and meat, many residents in the studied villages work as fishermen (in 
rivers or at sea) or engage in livestock farming. Improved access to transportation, markets, 
communication, and other facilities has made it easier for residents to obtain goods and services to 
fulfill their basic needs. 
To meet basic needs for clean water and sanitation, residents in the villages already have access to 
piped water from the local PDAM (Perusahaan Daerah Air Minum/Regional Water Utility Company). 
Additionally, other sources of clean water include well water (especially from drilled wells), rainwater 
collection, rivers (notably for residents of Semunad Village), and bottled or refillable water. 
Livelihoods 
In Nunukan Barat, economic activities are quite diverse due to its location in the capital area of 
Nunukan Regency. Still, most residents (70%) in Nunukan Barat work as fishers, including capture 
and aquaculture fishers. Some also cultivate seaweed. Fishers from this area often fish around 
Sebaung River and nearby areas, not just in the sea. In some cases, certain residents use electric 
currents to catch softshell turtles (labi-labi) for consumption and sale. According to a local fisherman, 
the turtles are typically sold in Tarakan. Some residents in Nunukan Barat also work as wetland rice 
farmers, though the availability of paddy fields is limited. To meet their basic needs, they rely on 
natural resources, cultivate their land, or purchase items from markets or local shops. Other 
livelihoods include trading, working as casual employees, civil servants, laborers, company 
employees, swiftlet nest farming, service providers, and more. 
In Pembeliangan village, the primary livelihoods are oil palm farming and fishing; a specific area in 
Pembeliangan, namely Sebakis, has extensive ongoing smallholder palm oil plant that are currently  
being operated and thus special focus will be given by the project to this sub-area of the village. Other 
livelihoods include swiftlet farming (to harvest the nests), transportation services, trading, laboring, 
livestock farming (subsistence), company employment, and casual work. Fishermen in Pembeliangan 
typically acquire their boats either by making them themselves or purchasing them. Boat prices range 
from Rp1,000,000 to Rp7,000,000 (without an engine), depending on size and quality. Boat makers 
obtain wood from their own plantations, local material shops, or nearby areas, including within the 
project area. 
The study also found that some residents in Pembeliangan Village harvest non-timber forest products 
(NTFPs), particularly honey, which is collected during the honey season. Honey gathering is not a 
primary livelihood due to its seasonal nature. Trees such as lita, mangris, and bengkirai are common 
51 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
sources of honeybee nests. Honey is consumed locally and sold at around Rp150,000 per 600 ml 
bottle. 
In Semunad Village, the primary livelihood is oil palm farming, with each household owning between 
2 to 5 hectares of oil palm plantations. The availability of land has facilitated this extensive farming 
activity. Other livelihoods include employment at oil palm plantations operating in the village. Only 
about 30 residents are fishers, while around 20 residents are hunters. Fishing activities are 
concentrated in the Sapiul, Tulin, and Sebuluh Rivers, while hunting occurs anywhere. There is no 
wet rice farming in Semunad; the rice farming system practiced is upland rice farming, though only a 
small number of residents engage in it. Most residents prefer oil palm cultivation. 
Land Ownership 
The residents recognize land ownership that comes with proofs or certificates such as SHM (Sertifikat 
Hak Milik/Certificate of Ownership) and AJB (Akta Jual Beli/Sale and Purchase Deeds), especially for 
residential properties. In some cases, less robust certificates are still used, such as SKT (Surat 
Keterangan Tanah/Land Statement) and SPPT (Surat Pernyataan Penguasaan Tanah/Land 
Ownership Statement), particularly for plantations and croplands. Yet, FGDs and discussions 
revealed that many villagers do not have any formal proof of land ownership, whether in the form of 
SKT or certificates. For those without documentation, land claims are typically based on mutual 
recognition of natural boundaries and agreements among the farmers/landowners. In the absence of 
formal proof, residents freely open new farmland within or near the project area. They believe that the 
forested areas they clear for farmland or oil palm plantations are inherited from their ancestors or 
parents, making it common for land ownership in the study areas, especially in Pembeliangan and 
Semunad Village, to be extensive. In Semunad Village, residents shared that land ownership in the 
village ranges from 2 hectares to tens of hectares. For plantations alone (mostly oil palm), the 
average ownership is 2 to 5 hectares per household. 
In managing land use by communities or other parties, such as companies, village governments play 
a vital role. Figures involved in resolving disputes, besides the village government with 
decision-making authority, typically include customary/community leaders, village officials (such as 
the village secretary or division heads), and other community figures, as well as the disputing parties. 
These issues are resolved through deliberation (musyawarah) to reach consensus and in a familial 
manner. If the village government cannot resolve the issue, the matter is escalated to higher legal 
authorities.

benefits”, encompasses the whole PT BHK concession area. Hence, the NMPP area is 15,591 ha.

Meanwhile, the NMPP Zone is defined as “the area encompassing the project area in which project 
activities that directly affect land and associated resources, including activities such as those related 
to provision of alternative livelihoods and community development.” In this project, the project zone 
delineation overlaps with the boundaries of biodiversity, hydrological, and social landscapes. 
Following the SBIA, the Project Zone boundary is determined using a combination of 4 criteria: 1) a 5 
km buffer zone, 2) the hydrological boundary (Sebakung-Sebuku Watershed), 3) land system 
classification, and 4) potentially impacted villages for the entire grouped project area (this includes the 
Northern part of Nunukan island as this is where the majority of Nunukan community members reside 
and in contrast why other villages in the island are not included). These considerations account for 
the presence of natural ecosystems; land use surrounding PT BHK; land cover and/or locations that 
could serve as wildlife habitats, particularly areas with connectivity to PT BHK; and livelihood 
activities of the communities. The map also shows the boundaries of the grouped project’s 1

instance as well as the potential expansion areas. The spatial distribution of HCV areas can be seen

2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6; CCB, G1.8) 
The project aims to address the interconnected drivers of forest degradation in the Nunukan region 
through an integrated approach that delivers measurable climate, community, and biodiversity 
53 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
benefits. The area faces ongoing threats from the conversion of industrial plantation, expansion of oil 
palm cultivation, illegal logging, illegal aquaculture ponds, and encroachment, driven by limited local 
livelihood options, weak governance and law enforcement, and a lack of education and awareness of 
ecosystem values. These pressures are compounded by the increasing needs of a growing 
population, a weak land tenurial understanding, and insufficient enforcement of concession 
boundaries. In response, the project has developed a theory of change with activities that focus on 
ecosystem restoration, community-based stewardship and forest conservation, as well as livelihood 
diversification to address root causes, shift behaviors, and create lasting incentives for conservation. 
Through these activities, the project seeks to improve land conditions, enhance carbon sequestration, 
strengthen community resilience, and ensure long-term protection of peat and mangrove ecosystems, 
including the vast biodiversity that they host. Moreover, all the project activities will be implemented 
with the involvement of local leaders, communities, and authorities. 
The problem flow chart of this project can be seen in Figure 10. 
 
Figure 10. Problem flow chart. 
 
54 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 11. Theory of Change 
 
The overarching goal of the project’s Theory of Change (illustrated in Figure 11 above) is to ensure 
the protection of intact forests, the restoration of degraded peatland, mangrove, and non-wetland 
ecosystems, the safeguarding of biodiversity, and the sustained improvement of community 
well-being. This goal is pursued through three integrated strategies comprising nine key activities 
55 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
designed to deliver tangible benefits across Climate, Community, and Biodiversity (CCB) domains. 
These strategies are: 
1. Rehabilitation and restoration of forests, 
2. Community & collaboration-based forest stewardship and biodiversity protection,  
3. Strengthening community well-being and livelihood resilience. 
Each strategy directly responds to the underlying drivers of degradation identified in the problem flow 
diagram, employing both proactive and preventive measures. This dual approach not only tackles 
immediate threats (e.g. replanting deforested areas, patrol system development) but also addresses 
long-term risks through actions like community and other stakeholders engagement, and support for 
sustainable alternative livelihoods. Together, these interventions form a coherent framework aimed at 
building ecological resilience and fostering enduring community stewardship of these critical 
ecosystems. 
More details on the project’s key activities can be seen below: 
1. Rehabilitation and Restoration of Forests  
1.1 Pre-planting land preparation and rehabilitation 
It involves site-specific ecological assessments, removal of invasive species, delineation of planting 
plots, and the overall needed preparation prior to the planting activities. The restoration approaches 
that will be used are tailored to local environmental conditions, to ensure optimal planting design and 
the improvement of local hydrological conditions. These preparatory steps enhance the planting 
success rates and ensure the efficiency of ecosystem rehabilitation.  
CCB benefits:  
1) Improved carbon sequestration capacity through more successful and sustained vegetation 
regrowth,  
2) Local community engagement in short-term jobs (e.g. site preparation, seed collection, and 
nursery work) and building of local ecological knowledge,  
3) Re-establishment of native flora which support habitat recovery for endemic and RTE species.  
1.2 Nursery establishment 
It involves the recruitment and capacity building of local community members to carry out all aspects 
of nursery operations, including seed collection, species identification, quality screening, 
propagation, maintenance, and seedling preparation for planting. Nurseries are designed and 
established to suit site-specific ecological conditions, ensuring the propagation of native species that 
are resilient and suited to local hydrology. Seeds are transported from their natural sources to 
nurseries, and subsequently cultivated in the nursery beds until the seedlings successfully pass the 
acclimatization phase in the nursery, before final transfer to planting locations. The nursery ensures 
a consistent and reliable supply of high-quality planting material, supporting timely and large-scale 
restoration efforts. 
56 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
CCB benefits:  
1) The availability of healthy/well-adapted seedlings helps increase carbon sequestration 
through planting success,  
2) There is job creation and skill development in addition to nurseries potentially evolving into 
long-term, locally run businesses,  
3) The cultivation of native species supports ecosystem recovery and the return of 
habitat-specific fauna. 
1.3 Planting and maintenance  
It encompasses full planting, enrichment planting, and assisted natural regeneration (ANR) and it is 
implemented through the hiring and training of the local community. The tasks that are included in 
this activity are site preparation, seedling placement, direct planting, gap-filling in partially degraded 
areas, and facilitating natural regeneration processes where viable. In parallel, the community is 
engaged in ongoing maintenance tasks such as weeding, pest control, and monitoring of planting 
success. This continuous care allows for early identification of threats (e.g., disease, invasive 
species, water stress) and timely implementation of corrective measures, ensuring optimal 
conditions for plant survival and growth. 
CCB benefits:  
1) The accelerated reforestation of degraded peat and mangrove ecosystems enables for a 
more rapid carbon sequestration and storage,  
2) Sustained employment and skills development in ecological restoration as well as stronger 
local ecological stewardship through direct involvement in forest recovery,  
3) The re-establishment of native vegetation cover and restoration of forest structure improve 
habitat connectivity, support species recolonization, and strengthen the ecological resilience 
of restored areas. 
2. Community & Collaboration-Based Forest Stewardship and Biodiversity Protection  
2.1 Creation of forest patrol and response system  
This is a critical component of the project’s strategy to protect restored and intact peatland, 
mangrove and non-wetland (mineral soil) ecosystems. This activity includes the recruitment and 
training of local community members to form dedicated patrol teams, equipping them with the skills 
and tools necessary to monitor and safeguard the project area. Two strategically located base 
camps will be established to serve as operational hubs, along with the construction of fire towers 
(equipped with fire suppression equipment), installation of forest signage, and establishment of 
patrol posts to enable broad coverage and rapid response. Patrols will be conducted regularly 
following set routes and schedules, with teams responsible for detecting and reporting illegal 
logging, encroachment, poaching, unauthorized land clearing, and early signs of fire risk (firefighting 
training will also be conducted). The project plans to conduct continuous engagement with 
57 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
neighbouring private companies and stakeholders, which is crucial to ensuring collaborative efforts in 
the protection of the involved areas.  
CCB benefits:  
1) The prevention of peatland drainage and burning through early detection and suppression of 
fires and encroachment will ensure there are no greenhouse gas emissions due to their 
degradation,  
2) Job creation, training in forest protection and safety protocols, and the promotion of local 
stewardship in protecting natural resources, as well as enhanced community security and 
cooperation with authorities,  
3) Reduction of habitat loss and illegal wildlife extraction, ensuring the protection of species. 
2.2 Enhancing community & stakeholder understanding of concession boundaries  
This activity focuses on clarifying land use rights and strengthening the understanding of concession 
boundaries among local communities and stakeholders. It begins with an inventory of areas currently 
used by communities and identifying where these may overlap with the project area. Based on this 
mapping, tailored informational sessions will be conducted and accessible educational materials will 
be developed and distributed. These efforts are designed not only to increase awareness of legal 
and project boundaries but also to foster constructive dialogue and understanding around land use, 
access, and stewardship. 
CCB benefits:  
1) Reduced risk of land use conflict leading to unsanctioned clearing of forests, that would result 
in significant emissions,  
2) Improved clarity over land tenure, greater inclusion in land-use planning processes, and 
reduced risk of disputes or enforcement-related tensions, through transparency and mutual 
understanding this activity supports social cohesion and empowers communities,  
3) Minimization of unintentional habitat disturbance. 
2.3 Biodiversity management and safeguarding  
The activity focuses on ensuring the long-term protection and recovery of key species and their 
habitats within the project area. This includes the hiring and training of local community members in 
patrolling including identifying signs of wildlife presence, recording sightings, and recognizing 
potential threats (e.g., poaching, habitat degradation) as well as in appropriately monitoring existing 
flora and fauna (e.g. biodiversity transects, using camera traps, bird count surveys etc.). Regular 
patrols will be conducted with a focus on trigger and indicator species to assess ecological health, 
while biodiversity monitoring protocols will be integrated into the broader forest protection system 
and more specific conservation actions will be decided and realized.  
CCB benefits:  
1) Intact ecosystems maintain natural carbon stocks and enhance the resilience of carbon sinks,  
58 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
2) Development of local ecological knowledge, new employment opportunities in conservation, 
and a growing sense of stewardship in protecting native species and natural heritage,  
3) Sustained protection of threatened flora and fauna and improved understanding of species 
trends over time will contribute to maintaining and enhancing overall ecosystem health, 
functionality, and resilience. 
3. Strengthening Community Well-Being and Livelihood Resilience  
3.1 Alternative livelihood opportunities assessment and support  
This activity aims to identify and support the development of sustainable alternative livelihoods that 
align with both community priorities and the project’s conservation goals. Through participatory 
engagement processes, including focus group discussions and surveys, communities will indicate 
their preferred livelihood options, which will then be assessed by the project team for feasibility, 
sustainability, and market potential. Based on this, Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) will be 
developed to guide the implementation of selected livelihood initiatives which will be tailored to the 
communities’ needs, priorities, and potential. Some indicative alternatives as mentioned during 
community consultations thus far include sustainable aquaculture, seaweed farming, ecotourism, 
mass production of alternative agricultural corps, tropical fruit agriculture, cattle/goat farms, 
agroforestry, non-timber forest product development (e.g. honey, swallows, medicinal plants, rattan). 
Capacity building, training, and the development of market-driven opportunities are some of the 
approaches and means that will be provided to help community members launch and sustain these 
alternatives. 
CCB benefits:  
1) Reduced pressure on peatland and mangrove ecosystems, particularly from land clearing, 
illegal logging, and burning (usually driven by economic need),  
2) Diversification of income sources, greater financial resilience,  
3) Shifting away from land-intensive/destructive practices helps preserve critical habitats and 
reduces human-wildlife conflict. 
3.2  Enhancement of public services with a focus on education 
This activity aims to enhance community well-being and resilience by addressing critical 
infrastructure and public service gaps in the communities, especially by directly addressing barriers 
to education in the project zone. It includes a participatory assessment to identify and prioritize the 
most pressing public service needs, such as access to education, healthcare, clean water, and 
sanitation. Based on these priorities, Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) will be developed for 
targeted interventions that will be vital to each community. Examples of such interventions and 
requests that were collected during initial community consultations include the improvement of 
facilities, vocational training for in demand skills, increasing teachers and providing scholarships to 
ameliorate school attendance.  
CCB benefits:

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

1) Enhanced access to education and healthcare, increased quality of life, and stronger human 
capital,  
2) Strengthened social foundation enables the communities to reduce reliance on extractive 
activities and build a long-term stewardship of surrounding ecosystems. 
3.3 Implementation of technical knowledge sessions on forest management  
This focuses on strengthening local capacity and environmental awareness by developing and 
delivering targeted training sessions and awareness-raising campaigns on the ecological functions 
and value of peat and mangrove forests. Training modules will be designed to be accessible and 
context-specific, covering topics such as forest ecology, carbon storage, water regulation, 
biodiversity, and sustainable resource use. Capacity building will also include practical skills relevant 
to forest management and project implementation, enabling local community members to potentially 
take on higher level roles in project implementation.  
CCB benefits: 
1) Increased local understanding of carbon-rich ecosystems supports the long-term 
maintenance of carbon stocks,  
2) Enhanced environmental literacy, empowerment through knowledge, and the ability for 
communities to participate more actively in project governance and implementation,  
3) Better-informed communities are more likely to support initiatives that sustain habitats and 
wildlife. 
2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
The project protects drained peatlands and degraded mangroves through activities such as 
rehabilitation of ecosystems, planting, assisted natural regeneration, and nursery establishment, while 
also ensuring biodiversity is monitored and safeguarded. In parallel, community development 
programmes are designed to provide community members with both direct employment (e.g. 
patrolling, planting, monitoring) and alternative livelihoods so that destructive activities in the forests 
will be halted. The project will also help ameliorate the well-being of communities through 
ameliorating public services as prioritized by the communities. 
  
SDGs Goals Impacted 
 
Year-round employment (patrols, nursery, restoration crews) and the 
implementation of a benefit-sharing mechanism which will direct funds to 
community programmes, that will promote alternative livelihoods, improve 
public services, and promote capacity building.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

Through public services investments (as part of the Benefit Sharing 
Mechanism) the project will address health related needs as identified and 
prioritized by the communities by improving local health services and 
potentially enhancing Water, Sanitation, and Hygiene (WASH). 
 
The project aims to empower women to participate in project 
implementation and benefit from its BSM by actively participating in its 
design. There is a gender aspect to the grievance mechanism and no 
tolerance policies will be applied in regard to gender-based 
discriminations. 
 
Restored hydrology safeguards freshwater lenses, and community WASH 
investments will deliver safe water and sanitation. Also, by conserving and 
rewetting peatlands, the project helps protect and restore wetland 
ecosystems, contributing to improved water quality and availability in the 
project area. 
 
All project activities are designed to address climate change and its effects 
by reducing deforestation and forest degradation in the project area, 
thereby lowering greenhouse gas emissions. The project also contributes 
to climate action through the restoration of mangrove and peatland 
ecosystems. 
 
Mangrove forests are biodiversity hotspots that provide essential 
ecosystem services. They protect coastlines from floods, storms, and 
erosion, and serve as nurseries for many species, including commercially 
valuable fish. By preserving mangroves as part of the project, the local 
ecological balance will be enhanced.

The project promotes the conservation and restoration of forests and 
wetlands by reducing deforestation and rehabilitating degraded 
ecosystems. It also contributes to the protection of the critical habitat of 26 
species in the project area that are listed as Critically Endangered, 
Endangered, or Vulnerable on the IUCN Red List, with an emphasis on 
safeguarding the Proboscis Monkey and Storm’s Stork. 
 
The project supports Indonesia’s nationally stated sustainable development priorities by contributing 
directly to the FOLU Net Sink 2030 Roadmap through large-scale forest conservation and restoration, 
reducing emissions and enhancing carbon sequestration. Additionally, it will help advance targets 
under Indonesia’s RPJMN 2020–2024 and SDGs Roadmap, especially in improving rural livelihoods, 
public health, and environmental resilience. The sustainable development contributions will be 
monitored and reported through the monitoring reports, using indicators such as training participation, 
area restored, and biodiversity metrics, ensuring alignment with national goals and transparent 
reporting.

2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
The project incorporates a range of integrated measures to ensure the permanence of climate, 
community, and biodiversity (CCB) benefits beyond the official project lifetime. These measures are 
64 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
designed to address both the ecological and socio-economic drivers of ecosystem degradation and to 
establish long-term incentives for sustainable stewardship. 
The project invests in the long-term protection of high-carbon-stock peatlands and mangrove forests 
by establishing protection/patrol areas and implementing restoration activities that increase 
ecosystem resilience. The avoided emissions and enhanced carbon sequestration are supported by 
improved land-use planning, hydrological restoration, and fire risk reduction. Continued carbon 
monitoring and community-based patrols help ensure that forest cover and peat hydrology are 
maintained even after the project period ends. 
To ensure that communities continue to benefit from the project, emphasis is placed on 
capacity-building, developing an understanding and ownership of their natural resources, and 
diversified livelihood strategies. By reducing dependence on destructive practices (e.g., illegal 
logging, unsustainable palm cultivation), and promoting more sustainable income sources the project 
strengthens local economic resilience. These livelihood interventions are designed with community 
participation and ownership, increasing the likelihood that they will persist beyond project funding. 
Additionally, the improvements that will be made to shared public goods are expected to also benefit 
the communities beyond the project's lifetime. 
The restoration and protection of degraded habitats improves habitat continuity and ecological 
function, which in turn supports the long-term survival of flora and fauna, including RTE species of 
peatland and mangrove ecosystems. Biodiversity monitoring and the promotion of community-based 
conservation practices (e.g., patrol teams, environmental education) are key tools to maintain these 
gains. Involving local stakeholders as co-managers of biodiversity assets is expected to help embed 
conservation values into local decision-making systems. 
The project aligns with national and local policies on peatland and coastal management, leveraging 
existing regulatory frameworks to enhance benefit permanence. The project is designed to be 
implemented for at 40 years as per the license granted but said license can be extended by up to 90 
years as per Indonesian regulations. Moreover, collaborations with government authorities, NGOs, 
and community groups help institutionalize project activities and ensure continuity. Lastly, activities 
that will help clear land tenure disputes and the participatory land-use planning are expected to 
prevent future land-use change that could reverse project gains. 
2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The NMPP will derive the majority of its revenues from the sales of carbon credits, which will start to 
accrue after the project has been certified in year 3, leaving at least a two-year period where the 
project will not generate revenue.

2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project 
(VCS, 3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
Conditions Prior to Project Initiation: 
The NMPP area is primarily dominated by mangrove forests in PT BHK I and peatland swamp forests 
in PT BHK II. In PT BHK I, Medium Density Mangrove Forests make up around 50% of the total area. 
In contrast, peatlands are found only in PT BHK II, where they are situated within Medium and 
High-Density Swamp Forests, which cover approximately 70% of the total area. Outside the forested 
regions, both project units feature non-forest areas primarily dominated by Other Woody Vegetation. 
As explained in Section 2.1.14 Physical Parameters
 (CCB, G1.3)
, the climate in the project area is 
moderately wet, with an average monthly rainfall exceeding 100 mm, and the highest rainfall recorded 
in September (282.23 mm). The area experiences consistent wet conditions year-round, with a Q 
value between 33.3% and 60%. Located within the Sebuku Watershed, the project area includes 
rivers such as the Pembeliangan, Sumbal, and Sebaung, all of which flow into the Sebuku River. The 
area is prone to flooding due to its downstream location, flat terrain, high rainfall, and upstream land 
cover conditions. Flooding, often reaching depths of 0.5 to 2 meters, occurs regularly during the rainy 
season. Tidal influences are also significant, with tides ranging from +0.5 meters during the rainy 
season to +3 meters during the dry season. 
The project area is gently undulating, with elevations ranging from -53 meters to 222 meters above 
sea level. The slope is predominantly flat, covering approximately 73% of the area. The area is 
formed by alluvial deposits and other geological features as indicated on the 1995 Indonesian 
Geological Map. Six soil types are identified across the project area based on 1987 RePPProT data. 
The predominant soil type is Dystric Histosols, covering 61.76% of the project area. Humic Gleysols 
make up 29.89%, while Orthic Acrisols cover 2.92%. A portion of the area (5.43%) lacks soil data, 
and Thionic Fluvisols are found in 4.45% of the landscape but absent in the project area. Orthic 
Luvisols and Cambic Arenosols are rare, covering only a tiny fraction of the area. Detailed soil 
distribution is provided in the referenced tables. The project area includes diverse vegetation, with 
high-density swamp forests being the most dominant. These areas feature tidal influences and are 
characterized by muddy soils and mangrove species. The vegetation includes protected species such 
as balau merah (Shorea balangeran), meranti rawa (Rubroshorea teysmanniana) in peatland forests, 
and Camptostemon philippinensis in mangrove forests. 
Without-project Land Use Scenario: 
The project areas are located on state-forest land designated by the Ministry of Forestry as 
Production Forest (HP) and Convertible Production Forest (HPK), which are legally authorized as an 
area to be logged/cleared. Prior to project initiation, the BHK I area had been increasingly affected by 
aquaculture pond development, while the BHK II area faced pressures from both industrial plantation 
66 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
(HTI) business and small-scale illegal oil palm encroachment by local communities. Based on these 
conditions, two possible without-project land-use scenarios were assessed. 
Scenario 1: Common practice  
The BHK I and BHK II project areas in North Kalimantan are located within Production Forests and 
are eligible to be land cleared for Industrial Plantation development. In BHK II, the prevailing land-use 
practice has long been the conversion of natural forests into HTI, particularly within HP and HPK 
zones, with large-scale industrial plantations, supported by market demand, regulatory frameworks, 
and economic incentives. Given these established regional patterns and enabling conditions, HTI 
conversion represents the most plausible without-project land-use scenario for BHK II. On the other 
hand, the BHK I area consists largely of mangrove ecosystems that are unsuitable for HTI 
development, which typically occurs on mineral soils or degraded upland areas. Consequently, HTI 
conversion is not considered a common practice in BHK I. 
Scenario 2: Continuation of pre-project land use without being registered as a VCS AFOLU 
carbon project 
Smallholder oil palm cultivation occurs around the BHK II project area; however, it remains limited in 
scale, and local communities lack the technical and financial capacity to expand encroachment 
throughout the broader BHK II area. Accordingly, continuation of pre-project smallholder expansion is 
not considered the most plausible without-project scenario for BHK II. 
In contrast, within the BHK I project area, the expansion of aquaculture, particularly shrimp and fish 
ponds, is a significant driver of forest and mangrove conversion around the project area. This 
expansion often results in the conversion of forest and mangrove areas, primarily driven by non-local 
business actors. Without VCS AFOLU carbon project registration, continuing aquaculture pond 
development in mangrove areas represent the likely land-use changes. 
The baseline scenario, along with the range of potential land-use scenarios, is further discussed in 
Section 3.1.4 (Baseline Scenario). 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
The most-likely land-use scenario in the project area: (i) continuation of pre-project aquaculture pond 
expansion in the BHK I area; and (ii) industrial plantation (HTI) development–reflecting the regional 
common practice–in the BHK II area. 
For a justification of the land use scenario without the project and other potential land use scenarios, 
please refer to Section 3.1.4 and 3.1.5. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
Findings from SIA and Participatory Rural Appraisals (PRAs) confirm that without the project and its 
carbon revenue, there would be no significant benefits for communities or biodiversity in the region. 
Restoration and conservation activities would not happen due to lack of funding, leading to further 
67 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
encroachment for oil palm plantation and aquaculture ponds, worsening poverty, and loss of 
biodiversity. Key challenges faced by communities in implementing activities, including: 
1. Limited expertise and technical skills. 
2. Poor access to external markets and partnerships. 
3. Lack of start-up capital and microfinancing options. 
The project aims to address these barriers directly or through partnerships. Currently, no alternative 
mechanisms exist to offer similar support to these communities. Additionally, without the project, 
commercial logging and mosaic planting would continue, harming native biodiversity.
 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
The NMPP is not seeking any additional offsets or credits from the community or biodiversity benefits 
generated by the project. 
2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
The stakeholder identification process was mainly conducted during the Social Impact Assessment 
(SIA) that took place in 2024 and there has been continuous engagement ever since. There were 
three primary stages of the identification, which are explained in detail below: 
1. Preparation and Desk Study 
Basic information (both spatial and non-spatial) was collected from publications (study 
reports, journals, books, statistical data, etc.), as well as through communication with 
parties who were believed to have information, knowledge, or experience related to social 
issues at the study location (local social-cultural conditions, community history and 
demographics, history of social conflicts, regional development, government policies and 
plans, etc.). 
2. Field Survey 
Data and information were gathered directly in the field to understand the stakeholders and 
the project’s potential social impacts. The sequence of activities in this stage was as 
follows: 
a. Opening Meeting: This meeting was intended to communicate the purpose of the 
Social Impact Assessment, conduct field scoping, organize the fieldwork team, and 
agree on the daily activity schedule. 
b. Participatory Social Mapping and Stakeholder Mapping: The assessors identified: 1) 
key parties/stakeholders that will be or have already been impacted (either positively 
or negatively) by the presence and operations of the project, or have or will have an 
impact (either positive or negative) on the project; 2) key parties/stakeholders that can 
facilitate/support or potentially hinder the project’s presence and operations; and 3) a 
68 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
depiction of the social-cultural and socio-economic life of the community within and 
around NMPP's area. This activity also involved the community jointly depicting the 
general conditions of their region and specific areas within it that would be impacted 
by the project, either directly or indirectly. 
c. Field Observation: The assessors collected and explored information related to direct 
or primary social impacts in the field, covering three sub-studies: Social-Cultural & 
Community Empowerment; Socio-Economic & Rural Development; and Labor & 
Social Relations. Counterparts from both the project and local communities were 
engaged. This activity also helped clarify and identify secondary data and more deeply 
analyze phenomena occurring directly in the field. 
d. Interviews, conducted in several ways, including: 
● Focused Group Discussion (FGD) 
During the discussions, the assessors gathered information and opinions from 
participants, as well as clarified, confirmed, supplemented, and deepened the 
interim findings from the field in the form of a brainstorming discussion on several 
social issues recorded, both positive and negative. 
● In-depth Interview 
In-depth interviews were conducted to explore the opinions and views of the 
community regarding social impacts that are expected to arise in the present and 
future. These interviews were conducted with key figures (key person interviews) 
to gain deeper insight into issues according to the expertise or authority of each 
key respondent in the village. 
e. Analysis and Field Identification Notes: During this stage, the assessors processed 
and analyzed the data and information obtained from field activities and then compiled 
it into an "identification note" containing interim findings from the field, such as the 
main stakeholders, social issues, predicted social impacts, conclusions, and their 
justifications or arguments, to be submitted to PT BHK’s Management Unit. 
Clarifications were also made regarding interim findings, and any missing 
data/information was completed. 
f. Closing Meeting: In this activity, interim findings—including brief information on 
identified main stakeholders, socioeconomic conditions and issues, and predicted 
social impacts—were presented to PT BHK. The goal of this activity was to ensure 
that the project receives the substance of the identification findings and can follow up 
on important or urgent matters without having to wait for the final report. 
3. Social Impact Analysis 
In this activity, the assessors performed in-depth processing and analysis of field results to 
confirm, clarify, and revise some of the contents based on input from PT BHK.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

across the villages of Nunukan Barat, Pembeliangan, and Semunad. While these villages cover 
extensive areas, they have low population density, resulting in some settlements situated far from the 
administration centers. One settlement that might profoundly impact or be impacted by the project is 
Sebakis, which falls under the jurisdiction of Pembeliangan. The residents of these areas have 
diverse backgrounds and differing aspirations, but their most urgent issues and needs have been 
identified and will be addressed by the project.

viii. Environmental Agency (Dinas Lingkungan Hidup) of Nunukan Regency
