2.3.3 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
Project documents that are not commercially sensitive have been and will be available to the public, 
ensuring that all stakeholders can access them. Hard copies in English and Bahasa Indonesia of the 
full project document (PD), monitoring reports, and the feedback and grievance process will be 
available at project offices in Jakarta and in the field. An executive summary in Bahasa Indonesia will 
be displayed in public areas within the Project Zone, along with a contact email and phone. Although 
internet access is limited in some areas, mobile phones and WhatsApp are widely used by the 
community. A WhatsApp group will be created for key community members (members of paguyuban) 
to receive updates and provide feedback. 
2.3.4 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
The project has established a set of communication channels designed to facilitate effective, 
inclusive, and context-specific information exchange with all relevant stakeholder groups. The 
selection of dissemination methods is aligned with the social, geographic, and technological 
characteristics of each audience, ensuring that communication remains accessible, culturally 
appropriate. More specifically,  
● Local communities and stakeholders: dedicated emails, whatsapp group (for members of 
paguyuban who will in turn spread the information throughout their networks), telephone calls, 
field offices and field officers. 
● Authorities and decision makers: official communications through emails, letters, and phone 
calls. 
● General public: printed materials and publications in whatsapp group. 
For example, key project documents, including the Project Description (PD) and its executive 
summary, will be made available in English and translated into Bahasa Indonesia; printed copies will 
be accessible at field offices and designated public locations, such as the village administrative office, 
while digital versions will be sent to the whatsapp group. 
2.3.5 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
From the outset, the project has prioritized meaningful and culturally appropriate stakeholder 
engagement in alignment with social safeguard standards, including the principles of Free, Prior, and 
Informed Consent (FPIC). Engagement efforts began in 2024 and continued through 2025, with both 
71 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
formal and informal meetings conducted to build trust, understand community priorities, and identify 
potential social, cultural, and economic sensitivities. 
Participatory Rural Appraisals (PRAs) were conducted through FGDs and field observations. Public 
outreach was extensive, and meetings were attended by community members, local officials, and 
administrative stakeholders. During these sessions, the project’s vision, climate and biodiversity 
objectives, and proposed community development activities were clearly communicated. Meetings 
concluded with open dialogue and question-and-answer sessions to allow stakeholders to express 
concerns and provide input. FPIC principles were embedded throughout this process to ensure 
community members could make informed decisions voluntarily and without coercion. 
To supplement group consultations, personal interviews were conducted across villages within the 
Project Zone. These interviews allowed for deeper engagement with vulnerable or less vocal groups 
and helped uncover specific needs and potential barriers to participation or benefit-sharing. 
This inclusive and iterative engagement process forms the foundation of the project’s ongoing 
stakeholder engagement and grievance redress strategy, ensuring that community voices remain 
central as the project scales up implementation. 
2.3.6 Risks from the Project and No Net Harm (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
The risks from the project are presented in detail in Appendix 3: Project Risks Table.   
2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 
The project has been developed through an inclusive, participatory framework grounded in the CCB 
Standards and strong community collaboration. Engagement with local communities began well 
before project implementation, with initial discussions among project partners in 2024. 
The project has implemented both structured and informal consultations across all relevant 
communities. This includes large group meetings, smaller focus group discussions, and one-on-one 
interviews, particularly in areas where community members may have limited access to 
communication tools such as mobile phones. These sessions were held in Bahasa Indonesia and 
organized at locations and times appropriate to the social and cultural context. Invitations were 
distributed through local leaders and village administrative channels. 
Key information, including potential project benefits, risks, and responsibilities, was shared and 
discussed in detail during these sessions. Formal stakeholder meetings were held in 2024 and 2025 
and have been followed by additional outreach and feedback mechanisms.  
To support continued engagement, the project has already established a dedicated office based in 
Nunukan Barat and in addition to that, two field camps will be constructed starting in 2026 that will 
serve as points of contact as well as information centers. The office in Nunukan can be seen in Figure 
12, and it is open to all community members and stakeholders. These establishments will also act as

local communication channels and will have project related documents and summaries (e.g. PDD,

By maintaining an active presence in the project area and embedding communication in both the

effectively and transparently, and ensure equitable participation throughout the project lifecycle.

2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (VCS, 3.18.6,

Information about the validation and verification processes has been shared with stakeholders in a 
high-level and accessible manner since the beginning of project design. These initial, more general 
explanations were provided during early information meetings/FGDs and throughout the FPIC 
process, as described in Section 2.3.4. Building on this, the project will provide more detailed, 
technical explanations and opportunities for discussion in upcoming follow-up sessions as 
stakeholders become more familiar with the project and its procedures. 
2.3.9 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (VCS, 3.18.6; 
CCB, G3.3)
 
As mentioned in the previous Section 2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification 
Process (VCS, 3.18.6, 3.19; CCB, G3.3), information regarding the validation & verification has been 
disseminated to the communities since the beginning which will then be supplemented by handing out 
a printed copy of the PD in English and translated into Bahasa Indonesia. The communities were also 
notified about the start of the public comment period and the procedures for submitting written 
feedback during the period, as well as the complete public consultation process, timelines, and 
requirements outlined by Verra’s VCS and CCB standards. 
Community engagement staff maintain regular engagement with both project-related communities 
and residents in surrounding villages. Ahead of the validation and verification process, project 
personnel will notify stakeholders in advance about the upcoming audit and provide sufficient time for

2.3.10 Stakeholder Consultations (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
The project began stakeholder communications in July 2024. Initial consultations were conducted in 
the frame of a Social and Biodiversity Impact Assessment (SBIA), which served as an introduction to 
the project for local communities in the three in villages identified as either potentially affected by or 
likely to influence the project, namely Nunukan, Semunad, and Pembeliangan. These sessions 
focused primarily on evaluating the "without-project" scenarios and assessing potential impacts—both 
from the project to communities and vice versa. During this phase, a Rapid Rural Appraisal (RRA) 
technique was employed. RRA is a method designed to quickly capture an overview of local social 
conditions while maintaining representativeness of the general characteristics of the study area.  
The next phase of stakeholder consultation commenced in March 2025. This phase placed greater 
emphasis on information dissemination, including details about the project developer and asset 
owner, project goals and activities, the rationale behind the project, its duration, the project area, and 
the presence of High Conservation Value Areas (HCVA). These consultations took place in the same 
three villages mentioned above and there was an additional consultation that was realized in Sebakis 
due to the area’s strong ties with palm oil planting and need to engage directly with local stakeholders 
to address concerns about land use changes and ensure community support for the transition to 
peatland conservation activities. This round of consultations also aimed to seek community 
agreement or approval for the project’s implementation following the principles of FPIC. The project 
developer organized dialogues and Focus Group Discussions (FGDs). These FGDs included 
stakeholders who were considered representative of various groups within the community, such as 
local government leaders and officials, village councils (Badan Permusyawaratan Desa), tribal 
leaders, religious leaders, neighborhood heads (Rukun Tetangga/RT), and other community members 
representing occupational or social groups within the villages. 
The first portion of each FGD was allocated to a project presentation delivered by the project 
developer. The second half consisted of open discussion and a Question and Answer (Q&A) session 
with the participating community members. Throughout this process, the project team received 
numerous pieces of feedback and suggestions, which were acknowledged and taken into account 
during the project design phase. The whole stakeholder consultations process was supported by the 
representatives from Nunukan’s office of Forest Management Unit, the regional branch of the Ministry 
of Forestry of the Republic of Indonesia. Their assistance was especially valuable during the first 
74

practices.

2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
The project will maintain open communication with communities and stakeholders throughout the 
project, ensuring they stay informed about key activities such as restoration, monitoring, and the CCB 
validation process. Community officers stationed on the ground will engage directly with community 
groups through regular meetings and remain responsive to feedback to improve the project’s 
approach, especially following the project’s grievance SOPs. The communication channels mentioned 
in 2.3.12 will also be used for ensuring that community members can actively participate in the 
implementation of the project.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Overall, the project will follow an adaptive management approach, allowing it to refine its methods 
throughout its lifetime based on monitoring, evaluation, new insights, and technologies, all in line with 
the SOPs and monitoring plans. 
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 
Several engagement and consultation meetings with communities and nearby stakeholders have 
been conducted to inform and involve local actors in the design and implementation of the project. To 
ensure that diverse voices were heard, the project employed the Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) 
method. This included participatory surveys, focus group discussions (FGDs), structured interviews 
with both men and women, and field visits with local residents to key areas of cultural and livelihood 
significance. These methods enabled community members to express their priorities, identify risks, 
and co-develop mitigation strategies. More information on these activities is provided in Sections 
2.3.3 and 2.3.4. Invitations were extended through formal letters and digital messaging platforms to 
ensure inclusive participation. The involvement of customary and elected leaders in outreach helped 
to ensure representation and legitimacy throughout the process. Consultations were carried out either 
directly with community members or through their representatives. A comprehensive stakeholder 
mapping exercise was completed to identify all relevant actors and ensure they were appropriately 
engaged. These stakeholders are the primary audience for consultations, socializations, and training 
throughout the project lifecycle. The project follows a structured communication and feedback 
process, using established channels to promote transparency and accessibility. Attendance at 
meetings and training events is and will be consistently documented, with records including 
participant lists, minutes, and supporting materials. This participatory process is instrumental in 
establishing trust, promoting informed consent, and aligning project activities with the long-term 
aspirations and needs of local communities. 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6)
 
The project consults with the communities and stakeholders to incorporate local knowledge into 
project design and implementation. These consultations are inclusive, engaging men, women, and 
marginalized groups in culturally appropriate ways to capture diverse perspectives. Further details on 
our consultation methods are provided in Section 2.3.5 and 2.3.10. 
2.3.14 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (VCS 3.19; CCB, G3.7) 
The project operates in full compliance with Indonesia’s constitution and laws, including those related 
to anti-discrimination, labor, and occupational safety. Examples of such laws are: 
1. 1945 Constitution (Undang-undang Dasar 1945/UUD 1945) article 27 
2. Law Number 40 of 2008 on The Elimination of Racial and Ethnic Discrimination 
3. Law Number 1 of 2023 on the Criminal Law Code

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
All employees receive safety and employment induction, along with an employee handbook. We 
uphold a zero-tolerance policy for discrimination and sexual harassment, ensuring equal opportunity 
regardless of gender, race, religion, ethnicity, or social background. A grievance mechanism is in 
place for staff, community members, and stakeholders to report and resolve any such issues promptly 
and appropriately. Recruitment follows best practices, prioritizing fairness, qualifications, and 
inclusivity.
 
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
The Feedback and Grievance Redress Mechanism (FGRM) upholds the following core principles: 
1. Accessibility: It must be open to all parties, with support for those facing barriers such as 
language, literacy, cost, distance, or fear of retaliation. Special attention should be given to 
vulnerable groups such as women, children, and persons with disabilities. 
2. Predictability: It must have a clear and consistent process with predefined timeframes, 
predictable outcomes, and mechanisms to monitor the implementation of resolutions. 
3. Fairness: Procedures must be perceived as fair and objective, ensuring access to 
information and opportunities for meaningful participation in decision-making. 
4. Compatibility with Rights: The outcomes produced must align with national and 
international standards and must not limit access to other grievance redress mechanisms. 
5. Transparency: The process and its outcomes must be communicated openly and clearly to 
ensure accountability and public trust. 
6. Capacity: Adequate technical, human, and financial resources must be available to 
address issues effectively. 
7. Feedback: It must serve as a channel for communities to provide input to improve the 
quality of project implementation and outcomes. 
8. Legitimacy: It must have a transparent and independent governance structure to ensure a 
fair process free from bias or interference.
 
9. Confidentiality: The identity and personal information of complainants must be protected to 
prevent the risk of retaliation or other negative impacts. Data will be securely stored and 
managed, and access will be limited to authorized parties as needed for grievance 
handling.
 
The FGRM development process and procedure is explained in more detail in Table 16 below: 
Table 16. Development process and procedure of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Mechanism 
Development process The Feedback and Grievance Redress Mechanism (FGRM) was 
developed through an iterative process and involved the 
communities as much as possible at every step. Steps taken to 
develop the mechanism include: 
1. FPIC Consultations with Local Communities 
84 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
During the consultations, the locals shared the most pressing 
issues they are facing and what their priority needs are. Local 
cultural practices and social norms were also studied during 
these sessions. All of these information became invaluable 
inputs for the FGRM SOP draft. 
2. Drafting of FGRM Standard Operating Procedure (SOP) 
Document 
The draft was composed based on the principles laid out 
above. It also aligned with national standards and international 
standards. The steps for addressing grievances and related 
technicalities are tailored with the common practice in 
restoration ecosystem concessions in Indonesia and modified 
according to the local circumstances, such as literacy levels, 
existing community structures, and local customs. Therefore, 
the FGRM will truly be understandable and accessible to all 
members of the community. 
3. Public Consultation with Local Communities 
After finishing the draft, the community officer disseminated it 
to the local communities and discussed it with them to gain 
their feedback. The feedback is later used to revise the draft. 
Later versions of the draft is then consulted again with the 
communities until it got their greenlight and eventually 
finalized. 
Grievance redress 
procedure 
1. Step 1: Receiving Complaints and Providing Initial 
Responses 
There are five grievance channels available for communities or 
relevant stakeholders to submit their complaints. For each 
channel used, the complainant or their representative must fill 
out a grievance handling form to ensure proper follow-up. The 
available channels are: 
1. Phone, SMS, or WhatsApp to the on-duty field staff. 
2. Direct verbal reports to the on-duty field staff. 
3. Grievance form submission into complaint boxes located 
at the NMPP field office, local village hall, or the residence 
of the neighborhood head (RT). Boxes are checked 
weekly by field staff. 
4. Dedicated grievance email address. 
5. WhatsApp group of the stakeholder association, which 
includes representatives from each relevant stakeholder 
group. 
Complaints submitted through informal means—such as 
handwritten notes—are still considered valid but must be 
transferred to the official grievance form and processed 
according to standard procedures. Field staff serve as the first 
point of contact and are responsible for recording complaints in 
85 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
the official grievance log. If the submitted form lacks sufficient 
information, the complainant will be contacted to provide 
additional details. If no further information is received within 
seven days, the complaint will not be processed. Within 24 
hours of receiving a complaint, the complainant will receive an 
acknowledgment letter explaining the grievance process, the 
contact person for follow-up, and an estimated timeline for 
resolution. Throughout the process, the complainant must be 
regularly updated on the status of their case. 
Field staff record complaints and follow up if information is 
incomplete. Complainants receive an acknowledgment within 
24 hours and must be regularly updated. If missing details 
aren’t provided within seven days, the complaint will not 
proceed. 
2. Grievance Categorization and Classification 
Once a complaint is received and acknowledged, it is analyzed 
to determine the appropriate handling method, based on the 
parameters such as i) parties involved, ii) type of grievance 
(grievance, complaint, or concern), iii) nature of the issue, iv) 
estimated resolution time, v) relevance to the project, and vi) 
sensitivity of the issue. 
3. Verification and Investigation 
Field staff will contact the complainant and take necessary 
actions. Simple issues such as questions or suggestions 
should be resolved directly through communication but must 
still be documented. If immediate resolution is unattainable, 
the Community Supervisor, in collaboration with the General 
Manager, is tasked to provide a response within seven working 
days. If unresolved, an additional seven working days will be 
given, and the complainant will be informed of the extension. If 
the complainant remains unsatisfied after all internal 
mechanisms are exhausted, they have the right to pursue legal 
action. 
During the investigation, steps may include meetings with the 
complainant for clarification, field visits, and interviews with 
relevant parties. All steps must be documented in writing. The 
investigation results will be submitted to the internal FGRM 
team for joint discussion to determine the appropriate 
response and timeline for resolution. 
4. Case Resolution 
If the complainant and project team reach a resolution, it is 
documented and signed by both parties. If not, independent 
mediation is offered at the project's expense. If mediation 
succeeds, the agreement is monitored and formally closed; if it 
fails, the case is considered closed and the complainant may 
pursue legal action. All records must be archived. 
86 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
5. Documentation 
After a case is closed, project staff document the resolution 
and ensure all discussions and actions are recorded. 
Complaint forms and logs are securely stored and accessible 
only to project staff. The internal team reviews closed cases to 
identify trends and lessons learned, and a full report is 
published and archived. Complaint details may be shared on 
the project website, but only with the complainant’s explicit 
written and verbal consent. 
2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
G3.8)
 
The feedback and grievance redress procedure will be communicated to the local communities 
through community meetings and the WhatsApp group that have been mentioned in Section 2.3.3 
and 2.3.4 and will be accessible to all villagers. Complainants can submit grievances verbally, in 
writing to the project office, or online through the platform that will be provided by the project team or 
WhatsApp. The grievances and project responses will be documented and reported every six months 
by the project team and, unless confidential, made available for stakeholders to view at the project 
office upon request. 
2.3.17 Worker Training (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.9) 
Building the capacity and empowering local stakeholders/ community members is critical to ensuring 
high-quality implementation of project activities and securing long-term, lasting impacts. Depending 
on the specific project activities that workers will be involved in, they will undergo the appropriate 
training. Some examples can be seen below: 
● Peat monitoring - training in water-table measurement (e.g. dip-wells), peat-depth coring, 
subsidence pole reading, GPS tagging, data entry. 
● Biodiversity monitoring - training in camera-trap deployment and maintenance, point & 
line-transect surveys, species identification (primates, waterbirds etc.), data-sheet 
protocols. 
● Forest patrolling - instruction on patrol methodology, reporting illegal activities, personal 
safety in peat/swamp terrain. 
● Forest-fire prevention & suppression - practical drills on early-warning detection, safe use 
of equipment. 
● Ecosystem rehabilitation - modules on seed/seedling handling, propagule selection, 
correct spacing, post-planting maintenance. 
● Nursery establishment & management - training in germination techniques, watering 
schedules, and record-keeping. 
87 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
● Forest management & environmental awareness - workshops covering basic ecology of 
peat swamps and mangroves, biodiversity values, climate-change mitigation. 
● Alternative livelihood training - tailored sessions on the specific livelihoods that will be 
selected. 
● Data collection & equipment handling - hands-on instruction for GPS use, DBH tapes, 
augers, sample labeling, etc. 
● Health & safety - induction covering first aid, emergency evacuation etc. 
● Waste Management and Pollution Control – Proper waste segregation, hazardous waste 
handling, and pollution prevention in field operations. 
● GIS and Remote Sensing Basics – Spatial data collection, GPS use, and interpretation of 
satellite imagery for project monitoring. 
● Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC) – Understanding and implementing FPIC 
protocols in community decision-making. 
● Stakeholder Engagement and Communication – Building constructive relationships with 
local communities, government, and partners, ensuring their effective engagement with 
the project. 
● Gender and Social Inclusion – Promoting gender equity and inclusion of marginalized 
groups in project activities. 
● Human Rights and Indigenous Peoples’ Rights – Understanding national and international 
frameworks for rights-based project implementation. 
● Grievance Redress, Conflict Resolution, and Negotiation – Establishing fair, transparent 
processes to manage complaints and community disputes, and necessary skills to handle 
complaints and resolutions effectively. 
● Financial Management and Anti-Corruption – Budget control, reporting, and safeguards 
against misuse of funds. 
● Transparency and Ethical Conduct – Promoting accountability, good governance, and 
ethical behavior across all project activities. 
● Reporting and Documentation – Developing clear, accurate, and timely reports for project 
tracking and verification. 
● Data Collection and QA/QC Protocols – Standardizing methods for field data collection, 
verification, and quality assurance. 
The training will be delivered through a mix of "desk-based" sessions, field demonstrations, and 
on-the-job mentoring to build local capacity and ensure long-term project sustainability. To ensure 
local capacity is retained despite staff turnover, the project will implement a train-the-trainer model, 
develop locally adapted training materials and SOPs, and pair new staff with experienced mentors for 
hands-on learning. A database of trained individuals will be maintained to ensure there is a skill 
rotation and active participants/workers will be prioritized for leadership opportunities to encourage 
long-term engagement.

2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
The project is committed to the hiring of community members throughout all levels of implementation. 
The project will provide equal employment opportunities to all communities, recognizing that this is 
both highly important for the implementation, since locals are better acquainted with the landscape, 
as well as for the community members themselves, taking into consideration that this was among the 
most frequent requests during consultations. The workforce will be selected following a recruitment 
process and all candidates will be equally assessed against specific job descriptions. The hiring of 
local people will always be prioritized, with a special focus on encouraging women and disadvantaged 
individuals to apply. It is estimated that the majority of the local workforce currently possesses 
qualifications suited primarily to manual labor. However, the project is seeking to improve education 
and provide capacity building specific to natural resource management to facilitate local employment 
even to higher, more specialized positions in the future.   
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 
Occupational safety measures will be prioritized throughout the project to prevent accidents, injuries, 
and health risks among field staff and community participants. Prior to deployment, all personnel will 
undergo comprehensive safety training and receive appropriate protective equipment covering at 
least the following: 
● Safe navigation in remote and swampy peatland areas. 
● Use of personal protective equipment. 
● Emergency procedures (e.g. first aid). 
● Safe handling of monitoring tools (e.g. camera traps, collecting samples etc.). 
Emergency response protocols will be put in place, including clearly defined evacuation procedures 
and access to communication equipment (e.g., radios or mobile phones). Additionally, a health and 
safety SOP will be developed ensuring that all field workers are protected and informed about 
potential risks and safe practices to mitigate them. Safety monitoring and incident reporting will be 
conducted and reviewed regularly. Lastly, insurance coverage for staff employed in field positions will 
be provided where applicable.

2.4.3 Management Team Experience (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.2) 
PT Bumi Hijau Konservasi engages Fairatmos to support the project as the operator. Fairatmos will 
handle the project design, financing and project management office setup. Meanwhile, PT BHK will 
manage overall project financing, team recruitment, and stakeholder management, including 
government relations.  
Fairatmos brings strong technical and operational expertise in nature-based carbon projects in 
Indonesia. The Fairatmos team has supported the development of large-scale blue carbon and 
peatland restoration initiatives. Using its proprietary digital pre-feasibility tools and the AtmosWatch 
MRV platform, Fairatmos enables accurate, real-time monitoring of carbon stocks, land conditions, 
and restoration outcome. Fairatmos enables accurate, real-time monitoring of carbon stocks, land 
condition, and restoration outcomes.  
Fairatmos has a team of 40+ employees to date (November 2025) that showcased strong in-house 
expertise in carbon project development, forest management, climate technical expertise, remote 
sensing, mangrove expertise, community engagement experts and technology development 
expertise. Fairatmos has also been trusted by over 400+ concession owners across Indonesia, 
Philippines, Malaysia, Cambodia and other countries in South East Asia to assess, develop and 
represent their carbon projects to buyers and investors globally. The organization also works directly 
with local stakeholders to co-design restoration plans, improve livelihoods, and ensure regulatory 
compliance. This experience directly aligns with the objectives of the Nunukan project, ensuring 
effective delivery of climate mitigation, biodiversity conservation, and community development 
outcomes. The key personnel details are shown below and will be complemented by more profiles 
as the hiring process progresses to ensure for a comprehensive project team:

G4.2)
 
To complement the project team’s capacity, we are collaborating with The North Borneo Adventure 
(TNBA), a local NGO based in Nunukan with strong expertise in community engagement, 
ecotourism, and environmental education in North Kalimantan. Their grassroots knowledge and 
established relationships with local stakeholders strengthen the project's ability to implement 
community-based conservation strategies effectively. As the project progresses, we are committed to 
developing additional partnerships with technical experts, local institutions, and NGOs to ensure that

                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
any gaps in knowledge or experience are addressed through collaboration and targeted recruitment

2.4.5 Financial Health of Implementing Organization(s) (CCB, G4.3) 
The project proponents are financially stable companies with capacity to undertake the project. The 
project is currently under due diligence by an investor who is seeking to provide both direct 
investment and support for initial activities through a pre-purchase of credits. A comprehensive 
financial model for the project outlines development and financial management strategies, including 
projected credit sales and annual budget forecasts, which indicate sufficient cash flow from 
investment and contracted sales to maintain operations throughout the full crediting period. This 
financial model is available to the VVB, and project validators may request additional financial 
statements to verify the proponents’ financial health. 
2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.3) 
The project has established and implemented an Anti-Corruption Policy and SOPs to ensure that the 
project team and partners remain free from corruption, including bribery, embezzlement, fraud, 
favoritism, nepotism, extortion, and collusion. The General Manager oversees the policy’s 
implementation and monitoring, with clear steps taken to prevent unethical practices. These 
measures include managing project funds through a joint bank account, ensuring transparent 
financial transactions, conducting internal and external monitoring of project operations, performing 
thorough audits, and fostering a culture of ethics and transparency. All relevant control mechanisms 
and documents will be shared with the VVB. 
2.4.7 Commercially Sensitive Information (VCS, 3.5.2 – 3.5.4; CCB Rules, 3.5.13 – 3.5.14)

1. Contracts and Agreements

2.5.2 Relevant Laws and Regulations Related to Worker’s Rights (VCS, 3.18.2; CCB, 
G3.11)
 
The NMPP is committed to protecting workers' rights and complying with all relevant laws and 
regulations in Indonesia. It ensures that employees are aware of their rights and that these rights are 
consistently upheld. Key labor laws governing workers' rights are listed below, with confirmation that 
the project meets or exceeds each standard. 
1. Law No. 13 of 2003 on Employment — this law oversees labor rights and workplace 
conditions, ensuring fair treatment of workers in the project. It covers employment 
contracts, wages, working hours, and termination processes. 
2. Government Regulation No. 44/2015 on Work Accident Insurance and Death Insurance 
Program 
3. Government Regulation No. 35/2021 on Fixed-Term Employment Agreement, 
Outsourcing, Working Hours and Rest Hours, and Termination of Employment 
4. Government Regulation No. 36/2021 on Wages 
5. Presidential Regulation No. 82/2018 on Health Insurance 
Through these efforts, the NMPP strives to promote a fair, safe, and equitable workplace, fostering 
respect for workers' rights and supporting their well-being. All workers involved in the project will be 
informed of their rights through pre-employment orientation sessions and visible posting of labor 
rights at work sites. They will also access a clear and confidential grievance mechanism to report 
concerns or violations.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
The NMPP recognises and is dedicated to respecting and promoting the rights of Indigenous 
Peoples (IPs), local communities (LCs), and customary rights holders, guided by international laws 
like the UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) and ILO Convention 169. It 
integrates these rights into all aspects of the project through several key commitments: 
1. Ensuring Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC): 
A strong FPIC framework ensures communities are informed about the project and its 
impacts before activities begin. Transparent consultations will allow them to decide their 
involvement freely. 
2. Recognizing Rights: 
The project acknowledges the unique rights, traditions, and governance systems of IPs 
and LCs. It will document their customary rights and ensure their voices are central to 
the project. 
3. Protecting Cultural Heritage and Livelihoods: 
The project will safeguard traditional practices and integrate local ecological knowledge 
into its activities. It also aims to build community capacity and promote sustainable 
livelihoods aligned with their values. 
4. Adhering to Grievance & Redress Mechanisms: 
Clear processes will enable communities to raise concerns about rights violations, 
ensuring issues are resolved promptly and fostering trust. 
5. Monitoring and Reporting: 
Regular evaluations will track adherence to human rights and community engagement, 
with transparent updates on progress. 
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
The NMPP applies a combined approach to defining Indigenous Peoples that aligns with the 
self-identification principle consistent with both the Climate, Community & Biodiversity (CCB) 
Standards and the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP). 
Based on the HCV assessment, no HCV 6 sites were identified. HCV 6 refers to sites, resources, 
habitats and landscapes of global or national cultural, archaeological or historical significance, 
and/or of critical cultural, ecological, economic or religious/sacred importance for the traditional 
cultures of local communities or indigenous peoples. However, in later consultations there were 
mentions of a part of land within BHK II which has cultural importance; the project remains 
committed to the recognition and protection of cultural heritage sites as an integral part of its 
implementation and so further participatory mapping is planned to be conducted to ensure that 
existing HCV 6 sites are fully mapped and incorporated. As part of broader stakeholder engagement 
and participatory land use planning, the project actively includes traditional knowledge and respects 
local customs, ensuring that culturally meaningful landscapes and practices are safeguarded. 
100 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Furthermore, any cultural values that may emerge through ongoing consultations will be 
appropriately documented and protected through adaptive management measures and continuous 
community dialogue. 
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
The assessment area is divided into several land-use categories, namely Permanent Production 
Forest (HP), Convertible Production Forest (HPK), Protection Forest (HL), and Other Land Use 
(APL). Based on this classification, nearly the entire project area falls within the production forest 
category (HP and HPK), covering 15,591 hectares, as shown in Table 17 and Figure 18.

Statutory rights within areas classified as forest in Indonesia are regulated by: 
1. Government Regulation No. 23/2021 on Forestry Management,  
2. The MoEF of Indonesia Regulation No. 8/2021 on Forest Management and the 
Development of Forest Management Plans, as well as the Utilization of Protected and 
Production Forests, 
3. The MoEF Decree No. 13496/MENLHK-PHL/BRPH/HPL.0/12/2023 on Forest Utilization 
Direction Map for Forest Utilization Business Licensing Year 2024. 
The utilization of forest for timber, non-timber products, and environmental services is legally 
permissible in forest areas designated as Permanent Production Forest (HP) and Convertible 
Production Forest (HPK), as stated in Articles 126-127 of the previously mentioned Government 
Regulation. The forestry business license (PBPH) is granted to companies by the Minister of 
Environment and Forestry after fulfilling the requirements outlined in Article 49-107 of the MoEF 
regulation. 
Article 31 of the Government Regulation highlights the criteria for classifying a forest as HPK. Article 
58 of the same regulation then enables the release of HPK from its “forest” status, allowing it to be 
converted to other land uses, such as land parcels to be distributed for land reform policy, National 
Strategic Projects (Proyek Strategis Nasional/PSN), or other land-based business enterprises, such 
as oil palm or rubber plantations. 
102 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Outside of the project area, on land categorized as APL, ownership evidence is recognized by the 
community included Ownership Certificates (SHM) for residential areas, Deeds of Sale and 
Purchase (AJB), and in some cases, Land Certificates (SKT) or Land Ownership Statements (SPPT) 
for agricultural and plantation lands. In Semunad Village, most land ownership is still documented 
with SKTs, especially for farming and plantations. Many residents, however, lack formal proof of land 
ownership entirely. In such cases, land claims rely on informal recognition using natural boundaries 
and mutual agreements among land users. Residents often freely clear farmland within or near the 
project area, believing the land to be inherited from ancestors. This perception contributes to the 
extensive land ownership reported in Pembeliangan and Semunad villages. 
In some areas, customary land rights and ownership are also recognized. The government of 
Nunukan Regency has started inventorying customary lands and addressing issues related to village 
boundary disputes. Through this activity, residents are encouraged to collaboratively map their 
territories, which will then be formally recognized as customary lands in each village. This process 
aims to establish mutual recognition of customary land ownership among villages and resolve 
longstanding boundary disputes in several villages within Nunukan Regency. The customary lands 
proposed by residents generally belong to Dayak Agabag, Longdaye, Tidung, Tahol, Tegalan, and 
other communities. Currently, customary lands have been formally agreed upon in eight 
villages—most of which belong to the Dayak Agabag tribe—using the Customary Law Community 
(Masyarakat Hukum Adat/MHA) framework. Five of them are located in Tulin Onsoi District: Kanung, 
Sayan, Tembalang Salang, Tinampa 1, and Tinampa 2 Village.
 
2.5.6 Recognition of Property Rights (VCS, 3.7, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
The project area is classified as “Permanent Production Forest” and “Convertible Production Forest” 
based on the designation of the Ministry of Forestry (formerly the Ministry of Environment & 
Forestry). No individual land title can legally exist within its boundaries. That being said, communities 
have used parts of the land for various purposes. The most notable is in Sebakis, where hundreds of 
hectares of oil palm plantation have been cultivated within the project area. These areas are being 
excluded from crediting and are designated as community partnership areas. The project team is 
doing everything in its capacity to make sure that all stakeholder rights are legally recognized, 
documented, and enforceable. The project’s land and resource rights, along with those of the local 
people, are clearly defined and respected.

2.5.8 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (VCS, 3.18, 3.19;) 
The Benefit Sharing Mechanism (BSM) is being designed through a participatory and transparent 
consultation process aligned with FPIC principles. Initial consultations were held in all project 
villages, Nunukan Barat, Semunad, Pembeliangan, as well as in Sebakis, conducted in the Bahasa 
Indonesia to ensure full understanding and meaningful participation. Key stakeholders included 
village heads, customary leaders, women’s groups, farmer and fisher groups, local NGOs, and 
relevant district officials. These groups jointly identified their socio-economic priorities and suggested 
livelihood improvements during these village-level meetings and FGDs. Initial agreements indicating 
the areas of interest from the communities were signed, while the project continues to transparently 
discuss the BSM framework with them and to plan the project community development activities in a 
participatory manner.  
In accordance to the communities’ initial identified priorities, as described in the signed agreements, 
the project’s BSM will: 
● Promote fair and equitable sharing of benefits in order to improve communities’ livelihood as 
a long term partner and co-benefit of the project 
● Support community-driven priorities, i.e. community development programs, in Nunukan 
Barat, Semunad, and Pembeliangan villages, including both alternative, sustainable 
livelihoods and improvements to basic infrastructure, as identified by the communities 
themselves, 
● Facilitate the transition of the Sebakis community (within Pembeliangan) away from illegal oil 
palm encroachment through the designation of a Community Partnership Area,  
● Ensure short term benefits that include the direct employment of community members for the 
implementation of project activities, and offer capacity-building and training to build skills as 
required. 
Following the initial consultations and outlining of priorities, the project will continue to work closely 
with the local communities in refining and validating the BSM as well as in planning and 
implementing the project activities, e.g. specific interventions to be developed, the mechanisms to 
distribute the benefits equitably, and the obligations of the project team. 
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
Through mapping and clear boundary setting, the project enables local communities to identify their 
location in relation to the project zone and to make informed land use plans within their own village 
limits while avoiding conflicts with neighboring areas. There is no need for forced displacement or 
disruption of essential community practices. The project fully respects the continuity of local 
livelihoods and cultural traditions. 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
Illegal activities that have been identified include:

To mitigate these risks and prevent them from proliferating, the project will implement a list of 
measures; which include maintaining communication with surrounding oil palm and industrial 
plantation (HTI) companies; establishing a community partnership area for sustainably phasing out 
current oil palm plants; collaborating with local authorities, such as the KPH, village government, and 
district government; and routine patrolling with the communities. The project will monitor these 
activities based on the relevant SOPs that will be created. 
With these in mind, the proponent has ensured that the benefits derived from the project are not 
derived from illegal activities. The NMPP is also dedicated to respecting human rights, ensuring that 
no victims of trafficking, forced labor, or child labor are involved. Strict labor policies and monitoring 
ensure all workers are employed fairly, legally, and with respect for their rights and dignity; in 
accordance with the principles and regulations listed in section 2.5.2 & 2.5.3. Finally, the 
comprehensive grievance mechanism that is in place will allow for immediate action if anything is 
reported by the communities.
 
2.5.11 Ongoing Disputes (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.5) 
The existing disputes around the project zone revolve around village boundary demarcation, 
resulting in unclear boundaries with and within both PT BHK I and II. For instance, the officials of 
Pembeliangan Village claim that their village boundaries extend to Gunung Daeng, including the 
marine guard post in the area. They also assert that Tanjung Pilar marks the boundary with Nunukan 
Barat Village and that the Sebaung River, located in this area, is part of Pembeliangan. However, the 
government of Nunukan Barat Village also claims the area around the Sebaung River based on an 
overlay of the company’s licensed map, Podes maps, and RBI maps. These unresolved inter-village 
boundary issues mean that village maps remain incomplete. This example is not the only village 
boundary issue in the broader area around the project zone.  
To resolve this problem, the project will conduct a participatory mapping together with the residents 
of conflicting villages to verify the exact location of the boundaries, as suggested by the participants 
of public consultation held at the beginning of the project. The proponents will also cooperate with 
relevant authorities, such as the governments of the disputing villages and the districts they fall 
under, as well as the Nunukan Village Community Empowerment Office at the regency level. To 
settle the conflicts, the office is currently engaging with academics from Hasanuddin University 
(Unhas) and applying the Customary/Indigenous Law Community (Masyarakat Hukum Adat/MHA) 
as acknowledged in the regional regulation of Nunukan Regency. By carrying out these measures, 
the proponents have ensured that no project activity will prejudice the outcome of an unresolved 
dispute relevant to the project.

2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 
The project will take steps to proactively minimize and/or eliminate the threat of leakage by ensuring 
that the conditions for applicability of methodology VM0007 and VM0047 are fulfilled guarantees the 
prevention of activity-shifting leakage, while for VM0033 by preventing both activity-shifting leakage 
and market leakage. Ecological leakage under VM0033 does not occur as explained in section 3.2.3. 
The main activities that are taking place within the project area and will be halted are: planned 
deforestation, illegal logging, and illegal aquaculture ponds. 
To proactively mitigate the risks associated with planned deforestation and illegal logging (primarily 
within BHK II), the project will implement a combination of strategic measures: 
● Direct employment: Community members will be prioritized for direct employment 
opportunities throughout the project lifetime, offering stable and sustainable income that 
reduces reliance on destructive activities such as illegal logging or land clearing. 
● Community partnership areas: A portion of the concession has been excluded from the 
project crediting area and designated as a community partnership zone. Community 
members already operating farms there will be supported to gradually transition to 
sustainable agroforestry practices, aligning livelihoods with conservation outcomes and 
reducing the risk of displaced deforestation. 
● Alternative livelihood development: Through structured community development programs, 
the project will identify, assess, and support sustainable livelihood options such as 
agroforestry and non-timber forest products. These activities provide long-term economic 
alternatives that decrease dependence on unsustainable resource extraction. 
● Awareness and capacity building: Continuous awareness campaigns and training sessions 
will strengthen community understanding of the ecological value of forests and build skills for 
alternative livelihoods. This will help reduce the incentive for illegal logging or clearing. 
Concerning the displacement of planned deforestation, although the regulatory oversight for the 
expansion of planned deforestation areas belongs to the Government of Indonesia (Ministry of 
Forestry), the project remains committed to proactively monitoring any significant activities within the 
111 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
project zone to effectively manage leakage risks. Despite not having direct authority or control over 
regulatory decisions, the project proponent will continue to collaborate with stakeholders (e.g. KPH) 
to address and mitigate potential impacts of leakage. 
In BHK I, there are also illegal ponds causing the degradation of mangrove ecosystems. These are 
operated not by local community members, but by external actors originating from villages far from 
the project zone. The project will implement a multi-faceted strategy to manage and the risk of 
leakage: 
● Enforcement and compliance measures: The project will collaborate with local authorities 
and law enforcement to close illegal ponds within the project area and prevent their 
re-establishment. Engagement will also be pursued with storage and distribution facilities 
currently accepting shrimp from illegal ponds, with the aim to establish the products deriving 
from illegal operations as not marketable. By reducing access to markets, incentives for 
relocation are significantly reduced. 
● Stakeholder engagement with external operators: While these operators are not community 
members, the project will engage them in dialogue to communicate the legal and 
environmental risks of continuing operations, and to explore non-destructive alternatives. 
● Community-based monitoring and patrols: The project will initiate patrols to monitor and 
report on potential attempts to re-establish shrimp ponds within or near the project area. 
Georeferenced patrol reports and photographic evidence will serve as verification of 
ongoing control measures. 
● Community development programs & alternative livelihoods: To reduce indirect drivers of 
leakage, the project will support local communities with the provision of alternative, 
sustainable livelihoods (e.g. sustainable aquaculture, ecotourism). Although the current 
pond operators are not local, strengthening community stewardship reduces the likelihood 
of future illegal entry or collaboration with external actors.

No further information is needed at this time.

detailed explanation of this selection will be provided in Section 3.1.2. The primary

3.1.2 Applicability of Methodology (VCS, 3.1) 
This project falls within the Reduced Emissions from Deforestation and Degradation (REDD), 
Afforestation, Reforestation and Revegetation (ARR) and Wetlands Restoration and 
Conservation (WRC) AFOLU project category as defined in the most recent version of the 
VCS Standard v4.7. Therefore, the project is compliant to the following applicability 
conditions of the methodologies that are mentioned in Table 19. as it is crucial for the project

1 VM0007 (REDD+MF) Land in the project area has 
qualified as forest (following the 
definition used by the VCS; in 
addition, see Section 5.1.2) for 
at least the 10 years prior to the 
project start date.  
Mangrove forests are excluded 
from any tree height 
requirement in a forest 
definition, as they consist of 
95% percent mangrove species, 
which often do not reach the 
same height as other tree 
species. Mangroves occupy 
contiguous areas and their 
functioning as a forest is 
independent of tree height. 
The forest area within 
the NMPP area has 
qualified the condition 
as it has been a forest 
based on the UNFCCC 
definition from the year 
2014-2024 based on 
remote sensing analysis 
and field survey 
(Section 2.1.14).

3.1.3.1 Geographical Boundary 
As outlined in Section 2.1.16 Project Zone Map, the project zone is divided into two separate 
geographical concessions—BHK I and BHK II. These two concessions are approximately 35 km 
apart and can be accessed within one day of travel. Each concession has distinct project 
boundaries, as illustrated in Figure 9.

3.1.3.2.3 
 
Sections 3.1.3.2.1 to 3.1.3.2.4 below outline the stratification procedures applied to the units 
identified above. These analyses were conducted after the delineation of the project area was 
completed. Spatial modeling was carried out using spatial resolutions (i.e., raster pixel sizes) 
appropriate to the characteristics of the input datasets and parameter settings and subsequently 
harmonized to a minimum mapping unit of 30 meters. 
3.1.3.2.1. Aboveground biomass (AGB) stratification 
Aboveground biomass (AGB) stratification was conducted in accordance with the requirements of 
VCS Module X-STR. Utilizing remote sensing analysis, supported by ground truthing through field 
surveys, the project area was stratified into 13 distinct land cover classes comprising both forest and 
non-forest strata. These strata were defined according to vegetation types that are indicative of 
varying levels of aboveground carbon stocks. The resulting land cover classification serves as the 
foundation for subsequent field inventory efforts and informs the appropriate emission 
characterization and carbon stock estimation approaches.  
Biomass carbon stock data for several land cover classes within the project area were derived 
through direct field sampling and therefore are categorized as Tier 3 data. However, for other land 
cover classes, carbon stock estimates were based on Tier 2 data sourced from Indonesia's Forest 
Reference Emission Level (FREL)
17
. This approach was adopted because those land cover classes 
were not included in the field sampling design, due to their relatively small area and/or low variability 
in biomass values. 
In accordance with the optimal allocation principles of the Winrock Sample Size Calculator, strata 
with a low contribution to the total area or with low data variance were not allocated sample plots, as 
their inclusion would not significantly improve the overall precision of the carbon stock estimate. 
17
 Government of Indonesia. Indonesia’s Forest Reference Emission Level (FREL) Submission to the UNFCCC. Jakarta: Ministry of 
Environment and Forestry (MoEF), 2020. Available at: https://redd.unfccc.int/submissions.html?country=id 
140 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Consequently, for such strata not covered by field measurements, conservative Tier 2 values from 
the FREL dataset were used.  
For the AGB data collection sampling method in the field is shown in Appendix 4, and further details 
regarding the AGB method have been provided in the SOP. Figures 22 and 23 further illustrate the 
spatial distribution of land cover classes in BHK I and BHK II, respectively. 
 
Figure 22. Land cover classification Map in BHK I of NMPP

Figure 23. Land cover classification Map in BHK II of NMPP 
 
The following section outlines the procedures applied in determining the stratification. 
Satellite Data Acquisition 
Utilizing Landsat 8 and 9 satellite imagery for the 2014 and 2024 analysis periods, the land cover 
classification was executed using the Random Forest machine learning algorithm—incorporating 
visual interpretation as a manual refinement for sampling each land cover class to ensure higher 
quality—with comprehensive methodological details further discussed in Appendix 12. The resulting 
model achieved a sufficiently high and representative accuracy based on a confusion matrix 
assessment, recording an Overall Accuracy of 92.04% and a Kappa coefficient of 0.88. 
3.1.3.2.2. Peatland Stratification 
Following the VCS Module X-STR, the project area was stratified into the following discrete, 
relatively homogenous units:  
1. Differentiation of peatland from non-peatland;  
2. Stratification of the peatland area based on peat thickness and peat depletion time (PDT); 
3. Area of peatland eligible for crediting;  
 
 
142 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Differentiation of peatland from non-peatland
 
GHG emissions from peat soil are assessed using distinct proxies. The NMPP uses the applied 
vegetation cover and subsidence rate (based on the peat thickness) as the primary proxies. Open 
water bodies are identified through vegetation cover analysis. The stratification between peatland 
and non-peatland area was established based on field peat inventory.  
The sampling design was based on several data inputs, such as Peat Hydrological Units (KHG) Map 
published by the Ministry of Forestry, Digital Terrain Model (DTM), Peat Depth Map published by the 
Ministry of Agriculture, Land Cover Map, and Land System Map. These inputs were combined with 
the requirements set out in VMD0016 X-STR Module and Regulation of the Minister of Environment 
and Forestry of the Republic of Indonesia Number 14 of 2017 concerning the Inventory and 
Determination of Peat Ecosystem Functions, particularly Article 14. The maps were used to 
distinguish peat and non-peat areas. Sampling points were distributed according to the grid 
specifications outlined in the Ministerial Regulation, with a spacing of 500 m between sampling 
points. The grid boundary follows the project area boundary that has been buffered by 500 m. The 
resulting sampling points were classified into Priority Sampling Points and Optional Sampling Points, 
which is based on their location relative to the peat area. The Priority Sampling Points are those 
located on top of the peat dome, as indicated by the DTM, and are the main focus for data collection 
on the ground. If data collection on these points are not possible due to terrain or other constraints, 
the Optional Sampling Points are used instead. The peat sampling design map, survey results, and 
interpolation method can be seen in Appendix 5, 6, and 7. 
As shown in Table 22, the peatland area is only present in the BHK II area, covering 50.41% of its 
total area.

Stratification of the Peatland Area in Discrete Units of Relatively Homogenous Emission 
Characteristics 
In accordance with the requirements of Module X-STR, the project area was stratified into discrete 
units characterized by relatively homogenous emission characteristics. This stratification serves to 
differentiate the peatland areas based on their emission profiles, thereby ensuring consistent and 
transparent accounting of GHG emissions. Module X-STR identifies three categories of peatland 
area: (i) ditches and open water bodies, (ii) burnt peat, and (iii) peatland not classified as open water 
or burnt. For this project, the burnt peat category was excluded from consideration, as fire-related 
areas are not included in the accounting. 
 
Stratification was applied consistently under both the baseline and project scenarios. The detailed 
quantitative results for each stratum are presented in Table 39 for baseline scenario, and Table 53 
for project scenario. For the baseline scenario, areas categorized as “ditch/open water” are projected 
to remain as canal systems. Meanwhile, areas classified as “peatland (not open water, not burnt)” in 
the baseline scenario are further subdivided according to post-conversion land uses, including 
plantation areas, infrastructure, conservation, and community partnership areas. 
 
144 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
In the project scenario, the same approach is applied to ensure comparability. The peatland stratum 
not categorized as ditch or burnt is projected to be allocated for ARR planting areas, conservation 
and protection areas, and infrastructure associated with project implementation. These 
correspondences ensure that emissions are consistently estimated between baseline and project 
conditions. 
 
The allocation of these strata after conversion is summarized in Table 23.

When using a conservative (high)

project crediting period exceeds the

therefore distinguishes

accordingly to ensure

are clearly identified. 
The project proponent must 
demonstrate that the resolution used 
in mapping results in a conservative 
assessment (i.e., it tends to 
overestimate strata that will be 
depleted), for example, by assessing 
variation in peat depth near the critical 
depth through multiple corings at close 
distance or by assuming a default 
conservative error (e.g., of 10 cm in 
moss or sedge peat or 50 cm in 
(tropical) wood peat with coarse 
woody remnants). Peat strata that will 
be depleted can be further stratified 
according to their peat depletion time. 
Areas where peat will not be depleted 
need not be further stratified. 
During the survey, the 
project proponent 
conducted multiple soil 
corings at each sampling 
point. The recorded depth 
was based on the results of 
replicate corings. If the 
difference in depth between 
two corings was less than 
50 cm, the recorded depth 
was taken as the deeper 
measurement. However, if 
the difference exceeded 50 
cm, a third coring was 
required. The depth 
recorded was the deepest 
from all the corings. 
 
Peat depletion time 
stratification has been 
made and presented in 
Figure 29. 
The 50-cm resolution 
used in mapping results in 
a conservative 
assessment, by making 
sure that several corings 
are taken in one sampling 
point. The deepest data 
will be kept in record. 
Therefore, it will tend to 
overestimate strata that 
will be depleted. Further 
stratification has been 
made based on their peat 
depletion time. 
No stratification on the basis of peat 
thickness is required if the peat 
thickness in 95% or more of the 
project area exceeds the required 
minimum peat depth for all of the 
above conditions

that less than 5% of the

thinner than the required

majority of the project area 
(over 95%) either exceeds 
the minimum peat depth 
threshold or consists of 
non-peat mineral soils. 
Since 95% or more of the 
project area meets or 
exceeds the minimum 
peat depth threshold 
required under all 
conditions, no further 
stratification of peat 
thickness is required 
according to the X-STR 
module.

Drained and undrained peat area mapping 
Peat condition of the NMPP area at the project start date is determined through the VCS Program 
Definition
18
 of drained peatland and the average annual water level in Indonesia of 40 cm
19
. 
According to the Government Regulation No. 57 of 2016, this threshold represents the optimal 
drainage status for Indonesian peatlands. A water level below (WTB) 40 cm from the peat surface is 
classified as drained peatland, indicating a deviation from the natural hydrological condition due to 
anthropogenic interventions. 
This threshold is not only supported by national regulation, but also commonly applied in cultivation 
areas, including industrial plantations—the selected baseline land use in this project. It has also 
been validated by experts as a widely accepted standard, even within protected peat ecosystem 
areas (FEG - Fungsi Ekosistem Gambut Lindung), underscoring its relevance in both policy and 
field-level implementation.

This interpretation is consistent with the VCS Program definition, which characterizes drained 
peatland as having a lower-than-natural average annual water level due to water loss or reduced 
water supply resulting from on- and off-site human activities and constructions. This alignment 
supports the validity of applying the 40 cm threshold as the reference for determining drained versus 
undrained peatland conditions in the NMPP area. 
Within the NMPP area, peatland occurs only in BHK II, while BHK I consists entirely of mineral soils. 
In BHK II, 70% of the total area is classified as peatland, all of which was in an undrained condition 
at the project start date. The extent of peatland within BHK II is illustrated in Figure 25 above.  
Digital terrain model (DTM) and drainability elevation limit  
Digital Terrain Model (DTM) data presents terrain information in the form of bare earth without being 
influenced by the elevation of vegetation, buildings, or other solid objects, thus only presenting the 
pure elevation of the earth. One of the derivatives of DTM data can be used to analyze the height 
limit of drainage, commonly referred to as Drainage Elevation Limit (DEL). 
 
Figure 26. Map of digital terrain model (DTM) of the BHK II area 
DEL determines the maximum thickness of peat that can be lost. DEL is the maximum elevation 
threshold above the drainage channel where water in the soil/peat can still flow out without the aid of 
pumps. When subsidence causes the ground surface to reach the DEL, the remaining peat layer will 
remain waterlogged, and further drainage is no longer possible. In the context of carbon projects, the 
DEL is used to determine how deeply the peat layer can be dried and the volume of peat that 
149 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
remains potentially oxidizable, which is important for subsequent analyses such as Peat Availability 
for Microbial Decomposition & Burning. 
 
Figure 27. Map of drainability elevation limit (DEL) of the BHK II area

burning chosen from both rules are mentioned as the conservative peat thickness that will be used 
on the peat depletion time (PDT) analysis.

Area of peatland eligible for crediting: Stock Loss Approach 
We determined the area eligible for crediting by following the VCS rules as set out in VMD0016 
Module X-STR, Section 4.4, using the Stock Loss Approach. The assessment is based on 
cumulative peat subsidence or soil organic carbon (SOC) loss over a 100-year period (t = 100). A 
significance test is then applied to compare stock loss between the baseline and project scenarios at 
t = 100, which determines the area eligible for crediting for WRC peatland activities. 
The crediting period, during which the project generates GHG emission reductions or removals, is 
aligned with the delineated area eligible for crediting within the project boundary. The delineation of 
this area was carried out using the equation provided in VM0007 Module X-STR, Section 4.4, as 
presented in the equation below.

The rate of peat loss applied in the assessment accounts for subsidence and fire in both the 
baseline and project scenarios, using conservative values that are assumed constant over time. In 
the project scenario, subsidence is limited to natural peat decomposition, whereas in the baseline 
scenario it is primarily caused by drainage following conversion to industrial plantations. 
Based on this analysis, 100% of the project area qualifies as eligible for crediting under WRC 
activities, as the difference in stock loss between the baseline and project scenarios exceeds the 5% 
threshold at t = 100. This demonstrates a significant difference and confirms full eligibility in 
accordance with the methodological requirements.

3.1.3.2.3. Tidal Wetland Stratification 
In accordance with VCS Module X-STR and VM0033, the project area is stratified into the following 
categories: 
1. Tidal wetland geographical boundary 
2. Stratification of tidal wetland within project boundary 
3. Sea level rise 
4. Ineligible wetland areas 
 
3.1.3.2.3.1. Tidal wetland geographical boundary   
The tidal wetland area of the NMPP is located exclusively within the BHK I area. In line with the 
VM0033 requirements, the project activity will focus exclusively on the restoration of mangrove 
habitats located within non-forest areas, and the project boundary is therefore defined accordingly. 
To delineate the area available for mangrove restoration (ARR-RWE project activities), an 
assessment of the BHK I area between 2014–2024 was conducted (refer to Section 3.2.1.2.3). This 
assessment identified degraded mangrove habitats with minimal or no vegetation cover and without 
significant evidence of natural recovery, which were considered suitable for restoration. 
Building on this assessment, the project planting zone map was developed to delineate the overall 
area designated for RWE planting activities within the project zone (refer to Section 3.2.2.2.3.1). 
Based on this mapping, the ARR-RWE project activities will cover approximately 143.41 hectares in 
total, including areas projected to become new mangrove habitat as a result of sea level rise, as 
further explained in Section 3.1.3.2.3.3. 
 
3.1.3.2.3.2. Stratification of tidal wetland within project boundary   
In accordance with VM0033, stratification was applied to delineate tidal wetland areas within the 
project boundary. For the NMPP, stratification was conducted based on the expected impacts of sea 
level rise, as identified through prior spatial analysis. Areas projected to be inundated or otherwise 
affected by SLR were separated from those expected to remain stable throughout the project 
crediting period. This approach ensures that baseline conditions and associated risks are 
conservatively captured for each stratum. 
 
As a result of this analysis, the tidal wetland was divided into two strata: 
● Loss stratum, representing areas projected to experience shoreline retreat and erosion under 
sea level rise scenarios; and 
● Persistent stratum, representing areas projected to remain stable and unaffected by erosion 
within the project timeframe. 
The delineation of these strata provides a conservative basis for subsequent carbon accounting and 
risk assessment. A summary of the stratified tidal wetland area is presented in Table 28 below.
