Above-ground Biomass Carbon Stock 
During the pre-assessment phase, a stratified systematic sampling with random start approach was 
applied to conduct preliminary measurements of woody biomass. The pre-assessment data were not 
used to estimate carbon stocks or carbon stock changes, as the planting areas consist of degraded 
mangrove habitats with minimal or no vegetation cover and without significant evidence of natural 
recovery, which were identified as suitable for restoration. 
All baseline carbon stock estimates and future monitoring activities will adhere to the sampling 
requirements of VM0033, as further detailed in Section 3.3.3.3, which specifies the methodological 
provisions to be applied. 
Areas with organic soil 
The project area designated for restoration does not include organic soils. This is primarily due to the 
absence of vegetation and the consistently bare condition of the ground, which indicates that the 
area has not undergone significant natural recovery. As further described in Section 3.2.1.2.3, the 
areas selected for planting correspond to degraded mangrove habitat with little to no vegetation 
cover and no evidence of substantial natural regeneration. 
Native ecosystems 
In line with the requirements of VM0033, the project proponent must demonstrate that the project 
area has not been cleared of native ecosystems for the purpose of generating GHG credits, except 
in cases where such clearing occurred more than ten years prior to the project start date. Within the 
project area, two general land categories can be distinguished. The first consists of stable non-forest 
areas that have long remained in a non-forest condition and are designated as planting sites. For 
these areas, no further evidence is required, as the conversion clearly occurred more than ten years 
before the start of the project. The second category consists of areas that were once forested but 
subsequently converted to non-forest. For these areas, evidence must be provided to demonstrate 
that the conversion was not carried out by the project proponent for the purpose of creating GHG 
credits. 
In this regard, it is documented that BHK only began managing the project area in 2023, when the 
Government of Indonesia issued the Business Licensing Commitment Approval for Forest Utilization 
156 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
(Persetujuan Komitmen Perizinan Berusaha Pemanfaatan Hutan – PBPH) for the Production Forest 
under the name of PT Bumi Hijau Konservasi in Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan Province. 
Prior to this date, specifically as shown in satellite imagery from 2014 (i.e., more than ten years prior 
to the project start date), the area was not under BHK’s management. 
Supporting evidence further confirms that forest-to-non-forest conversion in the project area 
occurred before BHK’s management period. Spatial analysis using land cover maps identified 
significant pond expansion in 2017 and 2018, whereas land cover between 2013 and 2023 remained 
relatively stable in other years (Figure 30). In addition, interviews with the Head of Nunukan Barat 
Subdistrict revealed that the fish ponds located within the PT BHK concession area (referred to as 
BHK I) were unauthorized, and the exact ownership remains uncertain. Local community members 
further explained that these ponds were initially developed by residents from outside Nunukan Barat 
Regency, specifically from Tarakan. According to the same sources, the land was originally forested, 
and the earliest clearing occurred only along riverbanks, where communities also harvested timber. 
Over time, portions of this land were converted into fish ponds by local residents, before being sold 
to residents from Tarakan who expanded aquaculture activities. 
 
Figure 30. Pond expansion map in BHK I project area 
 
Together, these lines of evidence confirm that the deforestation observed in the project area was 
caused by aquaculture operators rather than by BHK. Therefore, any forest-to-non-forest conversion 
that occurred before 2023 cannot be attributed to the project proponent. In conclusion, the project 
157 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
area complies with the VM0033 requirement regarding native ecosystems. Building on this 
distinction, the areas designated for planting can be further described as follows. 
The areas designated for planting include both sites that have been converted to non-mangrove land 
uses, primarily through pond development and prolonged disturbance, as well as stable non-forest 
areas that have long remained devoid of mangrove cover. Active planting is therefore required to 
initiate mangrove recovery and re-establish ecological functions, while also creating conditions that 
facilitate natural regeneration in adjacent areas.  
Understanding the ecological characteristics of the project zone is critical to justify the selection of 
these planting areas. In the project area and zone, the mangrove ecosystem is predominantly of the 
riverine type. Riverine mangrove forests develop along tidal rivers and creeks within floodplains, 
where they are submerged during most high tides and exposed during low tides. These mangroves 
can extend several kilometers inland, as seen along the Sebuku riverbank, tens of kilometers 
upstream. Salinity levels fluctuate seasonally between dry and wet periods. The conditions in these 
areas are ideal for mangrove growth due to the steady freshwater runoff from upland areas, the 
substantial influx of terrestrial and fluvial nutrients, regular tidal flushing, and sheltered locations 
shielded from strong sea waves and storms. This combination creates an environment that supports 
lush and extensive mangrove growth, often reaching heights of up to 20-30 meters. Riverine 
mangrove forests harbor most of the mangrove species. 
Some mangroves in the project zone fall under the fringe ecosystem category. Fringe mangrove 
forests lie along coastlines exposed directly to tides, sea waves, storms, and strong winds with high 
energy. Unlike riverine mangroves, these forests experience daily tidal flushing but receive fewer 
nutrients, which generally limits their height to 10-12 meters. They are dominated by species like 
Avicennia, Rhizophora, and Sonneratia. However, since the fringe mangroves in the project zone 
border a strait rather than open sea, wave action is less intense, allowing these mangroves to grow 
nearly as tall as riverine mangroves. 
Despite this natural potential, mangrove ecosystems in the project area face significant threats of 
conversion, most notably to aquaculture ponds. Based on the analysis of the most credible 
alternative land use, as presented in Section 3.1.4.1, aquaculture expansion represents the 
predominant driver of deforestation and ecosystem degradation in the project zone. Without 
intervention, the persistence and recovery of mangrove ecosystems would remain severely 
constrained under these pressures. 
To address this, the project has designed a restoration strategy through active planting. Current and 
planned planting activities will focus on non-forest areas, including both stable non-forest sites and 
areas previously converted to aquaculture ponds (refer to Section 3.2.2.2.3.1). By targeting 
degraded zones with little to no natural regeneration, the project ensures that planting efforts directly 
contribute to ecosystem recovery, complementing natural successional processes and securing 
long-term carbon and biodiversity benefits. 
158 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Stratification of vegetation cover for adoption of the default SOC accumulation rate 
In line with the requirements of VM0033, the default SOC accumulation rate may only be applied to 
non-seagrass tidal wetland systems with a crown or vegetation cover of at least 15%, with a linearly 
discounted factor applied for areas where cover is between 15% and 50%. For the baseline 
scenario, the restoration sites are located in areas with no significant vegetation cover (i.e., less than 
15% crown or vegetation cover), as indicated by their classification as non-forest land cover. 
Therefore, SOC accumulation under the default factor is not applied in the baseline (refer to Section 
3.2.1.2.4
). 
For the project scenario, vegetation cover is expected to increase as a result of mangrove planting 
activities. In the NMPP area, mangrove plantations are expected to achieve a minimum crown cover 
of 50% within approximately 15 years following planting. To conservatively account for canopy 
development dynamics, a phased trajectory is applied: 15% crown cover is assumed at Year 5, 
increasing to 50% crown cover at Year 10. For the period between Year 5 and Year 10, linear 
interpolation is applied to derive intermediate values. SOC accumulation estimates under the project 
scenario are thus based on this stratification of vegetation cover (refer to Section 3.2.2.2.4.1). 
3.1.3.2.3.3. Sea level rise 
Loss of tidal wetlands due to sea level rise 
In accordance with VM0033 requirements, stratification by salinity is only necessary when CH₄ 
emissions are accounted for. As detailed in Section 3.2.1.2.4 and 3.2.2.2.4.3, CH₄ emissions are not 
included in the GHG accounting for this project. Consequently, stratification of salinity is not required. 
In the baseline scenario, existing pond areas have been excluded from the planting area. Since the 
project activity consists exclusively of planting, no new ponds will be established during project 
implementation. Therefore, stratification of water bodies lacking tidal exchange is not relevant for the 
project. 
The BHK I coastal concession lies along the Celebes Sea, roughly between 3.82°–4.15° N latitude 
and 117.07°–117.69° E longitude, encompassing low-elevation mangrove forests, tidal flats and 
associated wetlands. The terrain is extremely flat (coastal slopes mostly <1%), with tidal range 
averaging about 1.77 m (from the EOT20 global tide model). The climate is equatorial (wet tropical 
monsoon) with little topographic relief. The area includes existing land uses (villages, shrimp ponds, 
farmland) interspersed with natural tidal marsh. Coastal vegetation is dominated by mangroves and 
tidal grasses. Subsurface conditions include soft mudflats and shallow peat in parts of the foreland. 
Overall, the region’s combination of high productivity and low elevation makes its wetlands both 
carbon-rich and vulnerable to rising sea levels. 
A spatial inundation model was implemented at 5-year time steps to produce a 100-year model 
(2025–2125) of tidal wetland gain and loss in the BHK I area. Each step incorporated projected 
sea-level rise (SLR), tidal flooding range, and shoreline erosion to classify areas as wetland or 
159 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
non-wetland. SLR scenarios were based on the latest IPCC AR6 (2023) projections and related data 
(Garner et al. 2022), giving total local rise of roughly +0.45 m (low-emissions) to +1.03 m 
(high-emissions) by 2125. For each year, we raised the effective water surface by the projected rise.  
The EOT20 global tide model (Hart-Davis et al., 2021) was used to represent tidal conditions for the 
BHK I project area. EOT20 was developed using multi-mission satellite altimetry data from 1992 to 
2019, incorporating observations from eleven satellite altimetry missions. The model extends across 
the global ocean from 66° S to 66° N, with the FES2014b tide model applied to fill in the higher 
latitudes. Tidal ranges for the period 2025–2125 were predicted using the EOT20 global tide model. 
The predicted average maximum tidal range amplitude for the site was approximately 1.77 m. At 
each time step, any land lying below the combined high-tide level (sea-level rise + tidal range) was 
classified as inundated. 
In terms of coastal plains slope zones, we treated the BHK I area as very gentle slopes (<~1%), 
which are most susceptible to inundation consistent with USGS coastal vulnerability guidelines. 
Coastal plain slope zones can be classified into very gentle (0.1–1% or ~0.06°–0.57°), moderate 
(1–5%), steep (5–20%), and very steep (>20%). Coastal erosion was applied uniformly along the 
seaward edge: following Nunukan’s BPBD 2023 report, we retreated the shoreline by 5–6 m per 
year. In practice, this means each 5-year step removed ~25–30 m of wetland at the seaward front, 
reducing landward area over time. 
The modeling shows both losses and gains accelerating over time. Cumulative loss increases from 
about 19 ha in 2025 to 602 ha in 2125, while cumulative gain grows from ~3 ha in 2025 to 298 ha in 
2125. Annualized rates rise markedly: losses accelerate from ~3.8 ha/year (2025–2050) to ~9.2 
ha/year (2075–2125), while gains increase from ~0.6 ha/year in the first 50 years to ~8.3 ha/year in 
the final decades. In the early century, wetland expansion was minimal compared to loss, but after 
~2075 both curves steepen, with losses consistently exceeding gains. By 2125, the net change is 
approximately –304 ha, meaning the loss area is roughly double the gain area (Figure 31). These 
trends indicate that wetlands are initially more resistant to inundation but become increasingly 
vulnerable under prolonged sea-level rise. 
160 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 31. Projected erosion and wetland creation over a 100-year period in the project area 
 
In addition, projections indicate that the BHK I tidal wetlands will undergo both substantial loss and 
gain under rising seas, with total loss expected to be roughly twice the gain by 2125. Loss rates 
accelerate in the later decades, underscoring increasing vulnerability. These findings highlight the 
importance of integrating climate-driven dynamics into carbon project design. 
It is further assumed that areas transitioning to intertidal conditions as a result of sea-level rise (i.e., 
potential new mangrove habitat) will be progressively colonized by mangrove species, supported by 
the proximity of existing seed and propagule sources. At the same time, a portion of existing 
wetlands is expected to be lost, consistent with the baseline scenario (see Section 3.1.4). 
Accordingly, additional factors for ex-post stratification include: 
1. Loss of planted area due to sea-level rise, creating zones where active reforestation with 
mangroves was undertaken but subsequently submerged, eroded, and lost (“LOSS”) (Table 
57). 
2. Gain of new mangrove habitat formed through sea-level rise (“GAIN”). 
3. Canopy cover conditions in subsequent monitoring years.

For this project, the Stock Loss Approach was applied to quantify the difference between the 
cumulative SOC loss in the baseline and project scenarios over a 100-year period. In line with 
methodological guidance, the rate of organic carbon loss in mineral soil due to oxidation was 
assessed for both scenarios. In the baseline scenario, this rate reflects the carbon losses resulting 
from conversion of wetlands into aquaculture ponds. In the project scenario, the rate of SOC loss 
was conservatively set to zero, acknowledging that losses are expected to be negligible or even 
negative. The volumetric organic carbon content in mineral soils was determined through field 
sampling of soil cores. These values were then applied in the stock loss equations to estimate 
162 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
cumulative SOC losses over the 100-year period. This parameter will be reassessed at each 
baseline reassessment, as required by the methodology. 
Based on this analysis, the results demonstrate that the difference in SOC loss between the baseline 
and project scenarios exceeds the required 5% threshold at t = 100. Consequently, 100% of the 
project area qualifies as eligible for crediting under the RWE activities, thereby confirming full 
eligibility in accordance with the methodological requirements. 
3.1.3.2.4. ARR Stratification 
The VM0047 ARR methodology provides two options for defining the project boundary: an 
area-based approach or a census-based approach. For the NMPP, the area-based approach is 
applied, as the project activities generate continuous tree cover exceeding one hectare and 
encompass both direct and indirect planting activities. The project boundary area is presented in 
Figure 9. 
This stratification reflects the ecological characteristics of the land, ensures the appropriate 
application of growth models and emission factors, and allows for accurate monitoring of carbon 
dynamics across strata. The spatial distribution of mineral and peatland planting areas is further 
illustrated in the project planting zone map (refer to Section 3.2.2.3.2). 
The carbon pools selected within the project boundary under the area-based baseline scenario are 
detailed in Section 3.1.3.5. 
3.1.3.3 Temporal Boundary 
3.1.3.3.1 REDD-APWD 
The project is defined by the following temporal boundaries: 
● Project Crediting Period: The crediting period is established from February 2026 to February 
2066, covering a 40-year period. 
● Historical Reference Period: The historical reference period spans from 1
st
 of January 2014 
to 31
st
 of December 2023. This historical reference period is used for determining the 
deforestation rate as per BL-PL which is in turn used for LK-ASP. 
● Baseline Update Interval: The baseline shall be updated at 10-year intervals. 
 
3.1.3.3.2 ARR-RWE 
The project activity is scheduled to commence in 2028 and will continue throughout the crediting 
period.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
The baseline scenario represents the most plausible land-use and management practices that would 
occur in the absence of the NMPP. In line with VCS requirements, the baseline is determined 
separately for each project activity type. These baseline conditions provide the reference case 
against which project impacts on GHG emissions and removals are assessed. 
3.1.4.1 REDD-APWD and ARR/RWE Baseline Scenario 
The VT0001 and VM0033 baseline scenario determination follow three similar sub-steps of Step 1, 
as outlined below: 
169 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Step 1. Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project 
activity 
This step aims to determine alternative land use scenarios that could serve as the baseline scenario 
for the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity. The identification process is carried out through the 
following sub-steps: 
Sub-step 1a. Identify credible alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU 
project activity 
Five plausible and credible alternative land-use scenarios were identified that could have occurred 
within the project boundary of BHK I and BHK II in the absence of the project. These scenarios were 
determined based on the legal designation of the project area. BHK I is located entirely within areas 
designated as Production Forest (Hutan Produksi – HP), whereas BHK II includes areas classified 
as both Production Forest (HP) and Convertible Production Forest (Hutan Produksi Konversi – 
HPK).

Trends in Land-Use Change 
The expansion of aquaculture, 
particularly shrimp and fish ponds, is a 
significant driver of forest and 
mangrove conversion around the 
project area. The expansion of ponds 
around the BHK I project area is often 
driven by non-local business actors 
and characterized by shifting ponds 
(tambak berpindah). Declining 
productivity in previously established 
ponds frequently lead these external 
operators to clear new forested areas 
for pond development. As a result, 
abandoned or poorly managed ponds 
are left behind, further degrading the 
landscape and contributing to land 
degradation and the spread of barren 
areas.  
Existing ponds area 
Recent surveys identified 306 hectares 
of ponds within the PT BHK I 
concession area. These ponds, 
established without proper permits, 
exemplify the broader trend expansion 
in the region.  
 
Uncontrolled expansion of ponds 
The lack of governance and oversight 
in the forest management permitting 
system has further exacerbated the 
issue, allowing the uncontrolled 
expansion of aquaculture activities and 
Aquaculture ponds, particularly for 
shrimp and fish farming, are a prevalent 
form of non-forest land use in coastal 
and lowland areas of North Kalimantan. 
While community oil palm development 
is currently more dominant in the vicinity 
of the BHK II project area, the risk of 
pond expansion remains significant due 
to the project area’s proximity to major 
river systems. These river boundaries 
provide direct access and logistical 
advantages for pond establishment. In 
North Kalimantan, the proliferation of 
aquaculture activities has been widely 
observed in state forest areas, often 
leading to the clearing of lowland forests. 
The presence of ponds in nearby regions 
and the ease of access via waterways 
underscore the plausible risk of 
continued encroachment into the BHK II 
area in the absence of the project.

Unexploited 
Forest  
Unexploited forests in Indonesia do exist, but they are rare and typically only 
persist temporarily as a transitional phase before being exploited again. Without 
formal protection or management, these forests quickly degrade due to external 
pressures such as land claims for other land uses. This degradation causes the 
forests to lose their commercial value as timber resources or aquaculture 
production, making them prime targets for conversion into industrial agriculture, 
forest plantations, or illegal ponds.

Land use

Illegal logging activities by local communities within the BHK II project area exist

Edi Kancil. These activities were linked to communities from outside the project 
area, although these operations are still conducted manually. 
Historical analysis across Indonesia shows that forests without protection will not 
remain intact over the long term. This increases the potential for encroachment by 
local communities, leading to illegal utilization of the land in various forms. 
Protected 
forest 
The conversion of the project area’s status from production forest to protected 
forest (Hutan Lindung, HL) represents a plausible, though uncommon, scenario. 
This consideration is supported by the ecological characteristics of the project 
area, which include: 
● Mangrove ecosystems in BHK I that function as coastal and marine 
protection zones (Kawasan Perlindungan Pantai dan Laut), and 
● Peatlands in BHK II with a thickness of three meters or more.  
A precedent for such a status change can be found in the establishment of 
Sebangau National Park, where the land status was converted from Production 
Forest to a protected area through Minister of Forestry Decree No. 
423/Menhut-II/2004. This demonstrates that the legal reclassification of forest 
areas for conservation purposes is not only feasible but has also been successfully 
implemented in other regions with similar conditions. 
The five identified scenarios will be analyzed for their potential feasibility based on their legality 
through sub-step 1b. A detailed explanation of this analysis is presented below. 
Sub-step 1b. Consistency of credible land use scenarios with enforced mandatory applicable 
laws and regulations 
The credible land-use scenarios identified under sub-step 1a were assessed from a legal 
perspective based on Indonesian laws and regulations. Five land-use scenarios were identified, 
considering the legal designation of the project area as a combination of ‘Production Forest’ and 
‘Convertible Production Forest’. The analysis focused on what is legally permissible within these 
land classifications, as detailed in Indonesian law, and the results are summarized in Table 32.

released.

for changing the status and function of

development.

Continuation 
of pre-project 
activity - 
Smallholder 
Oil Palm 
Cultivation 
The NMPP area is designated as state-owned forest land, consisting of HP and 
HPK. According to Government Regulation No. 23 of 2021 and Ministry of 
Environment and Forestry Regulation No. 7 of 2021, smallholder oil palm 
cultivation is not legally permitted within forest areas. However, in practice, 
abandoned or unmanaged forest lands—those not actively under concession or 
commercial use—are often targeted by smallholder farmers. In such cases, these 
individuals may attempt to informally claim and cultivate the land, despite lacking 
legal entitlement. This practice introduces additional legal complexity and 
undermines regulatory oversight of forest land use. 
As a result, smallholder oil palm cultivation faces substantial legal and regulatory 
barriers in the project area, making it an unviable and non-compliant land-use 
option under current conditions. 
Unexploited 
Forest  
There are several legal options that allow an area to remain an unexploited forest. 
One option is to maintain the area as a production forest without granting permits 
for logging or land conversion for industrial plantation. Another option is to apply for 
a concession for ecosystem restoration management, which allows the 
management of production forests for ecosystem restoration purposes. 
According to Government Regulation No. 23 of 2021, Article 141, paragraph (2), 
the utilization of production forest areas can be carried out through ecosystem 
restoration schemes or environmental service utilization. Additionally, Ministry of 
Environment and Forestry Regulation No. 7 of 2021 states that the optimization of 
production forest area utilization can be achieved through environmental service 
utilization schemes. 
Protected 
forest 
 
The forest areas within the project site can be legally reclassified as protected 
forests or conservation forests. This process involves legal procedures governed 
by Article 331 of the Ministry of Environment and Forestry Regulation No. 7 of 2021 
and Articles 77 and 80 of Government Regulation No. 23 of 2021. These 
regulations stipulate that the conversion of production forests into conservation 
forests or protected forests can be carried out, provided that the areas meet the 
criteria for conservation or protected forest classification as specified in applicable 
laws and regulations. 
In accordance with the Minister of 
Forestry Regulation No. 
P.35/Menhut-II/2010, mangrove 
coastal zones (Kawasan Pantai 
Berhutan Bakau) are defined as 
coastal areas with natural mangrove 
habitats that serve vital protective 
functions for coastal and marine 
ecosystems. Additionally, Minister of

peatland with a depth of 3 meters or

the criteria outlined in Article 10 of 
Presidential Decree No. 32 of 1990. This 
is further supported by Article 10,

Sub-step 1c.  Selection of the baseline scenario.  
Based on the analysis conducted in Sub-step 1a and Sub-step 1b, the most credible alternative 
land use scenarios in the project area are: (i) aquaculture pond expansion for the BHK I area; and (ii) 
industrial plantation development for the BHK II area. 
The implementation of project activities, namely the protection of intact forest and the 
rehabilitation/restoration of peatlands (REDD-APWD activity), is intended to prevent forest loss due 
to land conversion into industrial plantations. Such conversion typically involves land clearing and 
the construction of drainage canals, resulting in the loss of natural vegetation cover and leading to 
drained or partially drained peat soils. This condition subsequently triggers oxidation of organic 
matter, peat subsidence, and increased greenhouse gas emissions.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Furthermore, the rehabilitation, restoration, and improvement of vegetation cover in non-forest areas 
and mangrove ecosystems (ARR/RWE activity) are crucial, as the baseline scenario does not 
involve any efforts to enhance vegetation cover. This is because the baseline scenario prioritizes 
business-oriented land use, focusing on industrial plantation expansion in BHK II and aquaculture 
pond development in BHK I, which tend to degrade natural ecosystems without any restoration 
intervention. 
3.1.4.2 ARR Baseline Scenario 
A performance benchmark analysis is used to quantify the baseline scenario or the 
business-as-usual changes in vegetation cover. This is equal to the ratio of average change in 
stocking index (SI) of control plots to project plots. The performance benchmark analysis is derived 
from 10-year period data from 2015 up to 2025. Detailed analysis on the performance benchmark is 
provided in Section 3.2.1.3.  
Figure 38 below is shown the matched project plots with the control plots. Stocking index (SI) 
analysis through Radar Vegetation Index (RVI) modeling is selected. The RVI data is gathered from 
Sentinel-1 GRD. RVI is a radar data-based vegetation index used to measure vegetation density and 
health, with values ranging from 0 to 1. Higher values indicate denser and healthier vegetation. 
Detailed analysis of the performance benchmark result is provided in Section 3.2.1.3. 
3.1.5 Additionality (VCS, 3.14) 
3.1.5.1 Additionality of REDD-APWD (VT001) 
The most plausible baseline scenario was determined using the VT0001 Tool for the Demonstration 
and Assessment of Additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forest and Other Land Uses (AFOLU) Project 
activities, version 3.0. This tool was applied in the NMPP activity to assess and demonstrate 
additionality, as required by the VCS program rules and requirements for AFOLU project activities. 
The following steps are followed to identify the most plausible baseline scenario in the project. 
As outlined in the additionality tool, four steps were applied in this process: 
Step 1: Identification of alternative scenarios to the project activity 
Step 2: Barrier analysis 
Step 3: Investment analysis (this step was not applicable) 
Step 4: Common practice analysis 
Step 1 is already completed in the Section 3.1.4 with the most plausible alternative land use 
scenario for REDD-APWD project activity is industrial plantation. For this additionality analysis, Step 
3 barrier analysis as it is allowed to undertake either Step 2 or 3 as the next step. Detailed 
explanations for the next steps are provided in the following text.

Step 3. Barrier analysis 
Following the VT001 document, if this step is used, determine whether the proposed project activity 
faces barriers that: 
a) Prevent the implementation of this type of proposed project activity without the revenue from 
the sale of GHG credits; and 
b) Do not prevent the implementation of at least one of the alternative land use scenarios.  
Sub-step 3a. Identify barriers that would prevent the implementation of the type of proposed 
project activity 
The possible barrier that prevents the implementation of the proposed REDD-APWD project activity 
without the revenue from the sale of GHG credits is the investment barrier. Around the region (North 
Kalimantan Province), there is evidence that similar conservation practices are not feasible unless 
being funded by grants provided by NGOs. For example, there is an initiative called “Development 
by Design (DbD)” spearheaded by Yayasan Konservasi Alam Indonesia (YKAN, the Indonesian 
branch of The Nature Conservancy) in collaboration with Mulawarman University. This initiative aims 
to preserve North Kalimantan’s natural environment amid development pressures, employing 
landscape-based approach Menjaga Hutan Kalimantan Utara. 
YKAN has also been collaborating with the Bulungan Regency Government for the Integrated Area 
Development (IAD) program. YKAN has been assisting 18 villages around the Kayan Landscape. In 
this facilitation process, YKAN works in partnership with other NGOs as strategic partners: Yayasan 
Pioner, Yayasan Institute for Research and Empowerment (IRE), Yayasan Kalimajari, Perkumpulan 
Peduli Lingkungan (Perdu), and Perkumpulan Lingkar Hutan Lestari (PLHL) Menjalin Kemitraan 
Mendukung Pengelolaan Lanskap Kayan. 
Sub-step 3b. Show that the identified barriers would not prevent the implementation of at 
least one of the alternative land use scenarios (except the proposed project activity) 
The identified barrier in sub-step 3a is the investment barrier. The selected alternative land use 
scenario (industrial plantation) faces no investment barrier based on the following reasons.  In North 
Kalimantan, both industrial-scale and community-owned Acacia Plantation offer strong economic 
incentives, with no significant financial barriers to establishment.  
Industrial estates of several thousand hectares benefit from established supply chains to pulp & 
paper mills and strong market demand for pulp, both domestically and for export. Although specific 
IRR or payback period figures for North Kalimantan are scarce, in analogous large estates (2000-ha) 
in Malaysia, a 15-year Acacia plantation showed an IRR of ~14.5%
25
. Meanwhile in Indonesia, the 
Acacia Plantation business shows an increasing trend due to: (1) Rapid expansion in certain region 
(mainly Kalimantan) and (2) short rotation period and high productivity resulting a fast returns.

1. Recent mapping studies and NGO reports show substantial plantation area increases in 
Kalimantan (e.g., ~74,000 ha growth in 2023 in Kalimantan; significant clearing inside HTI 
concessions like PT Adindo Hutani Lestari
26
), which is located adjacent with the BHK II. This 
demonstrates active on-the-ground expansion of pulpwood (mostly Acacia/Eucalyptus) 
plantations. 
2. Industrial Acacia (A. mangium / hybrids) are managed on short rotations (commonly 4–8 
years for pulpwood; many industrial plantations use ~7 years), producing high mean annual 
increments (MAI). Studies and industry trials show MAI and successive-rotation productivity 
that enable quicker harvest cycles and earlier cashflow compared with many timber 
species
27
. Short harvest cycles materially shorten payback periods. 
Step 4. Common practice analysis 
Following the VT001, Step 4 proceeds directly as both Step 3a and 3b are satisfied. Similar activity 
to the proposed REDD-APWD project must be identified and assessed.   
The effective protection of intact forests on land designated as production forest is not common 
practice in Indonesia, including in North Kalimantan. The more prevalent practice in this region is the 
conversion of production forests for commercial purposes, such as logging or land-use changes for 
plantations or other industries.  
Outside of legally designated protected areas, there are almost no examples of intact forest 
protection without financial incentives from carbon funding. Most conservation and restoration 
projects in North Kalimantan rely on philanthropic funding from donors or carbon finance 
mechanisms such as VCS AFOLU, yet access to these funding sources remains highly limited. Even 
for donation-based projects, conflicts with local governments or communities frequently arise, as 
land designated for production is perceived as failing to contribute economically. Additionally, some 
offset projects exist where large corporations finance site management as compensation for 
environmental damage caused elsewhere. However, such projects are rare and typically limited in 
scale. 
Based on this analysis, the project does not qualify as common practice, as the protection of forests 
within production forest areas is not a widely implemented approach. Furthermore, there are no 
sufficient financial incentives beyond carbon funding to sustain such initiatives. Therefore, this 
project meets the additionality criterion, as without carbon finance, the large-scale protection of intact 
forests within production forest areas would not occur.

Step 1: Regulatory Surplus 
Is the project located in an UNFCCC Annex 1 or Non-Annex 1 country? 
☐   Annex 1 country  ☒  Non-Annex 1 country 
Are the project activities mandated by any law, statute, or other regulatory framework? 
☐  Yes   ☒  No 
If the project is located inside a Non-Annex 1 country and the project activities are mandated by a 
law, statute, or other regulatory framework, are such laws, statutes, or regulatory frameworks 
systematically enforced?  
☐  Yes   ☒  No 
One of the project activities of the NMPP is mangrove plantation. However, these activities are not 
mandated by any specific law, statute, or regulatory framework related to environmental protection or 
forest conservation in Indonesia. Under the Indonesian legal framework, restoration activities within 
the forest and other land areas are mandated by government regulations, particularly in regions 
classified as degraded ecosystems. According to the Ministry of Environment and Forestry (MoEF) 
Regulation No. P.23/MENLHK/SETJEN/KUM.1/4/2021 concerning the Implementation of Forest and 
Land Rehabilitation, restoration is required when degradation is identified through specific spatial 
analyses. These analyses utilize three key spatial maps: 
(1) Critical land map (peta lahan kritis), 
(2) Watershed classification map (peta klasifikasi DAS dan tangkapan air), and 
(3) Natural disaster risk area map (peta daerah rawan dan pasca bencana). 
Among these, the Critical Land Map identifies areas classified as Kritis (Critical) and Sangat Kritis 
(Very Critical)—lands considered to have lost their ecological functions, such as water retention, 
vegetation support, or erosion control capacity. These maps are publicly available and managed 
through the SIGAP KLHK platform. 
As shown in Figure 32 and Figure 33, which presents the overlay of the Critical Land Map with the 
NMPP boundary, most of the NMPP area is not classified as Kritis or Sangat Kritis. While a small 
portion of the project area does fall within the critical category, this area is currently covered by 
184 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
existing forest and will not be subject to any planting activities. Therefore, restoration in NMPP areas 
is not mandated under current legal requirements. 
 
Figure 32. Map of Critical Land of BHK I area.

In addition, Government Regulation (Peraturan Pemerintah) No. 27 of 2025 on the Protection and 
Management of Mangrove Ecosystems mandates restoration or rehabilitation only in mangrove 
ecosystems that have been officially recognized as degraded or damaged. 
 
To further support the demonstration of additionality, field assessments have confirmed that no 
restoration or reforestation efforts are being implemented in the NMPP area, including in zones that 
may fall within the legally mandated categories. Hence, mangrove restoration activities implemented 
under the NMPP are not required by any existing law or regulation. These activities are carried out 
voluntarily and are not the result of regulatory obligations. Therefore, the project activities meet the 
regulatory surplus requirement and can be considered additional, as they go beyond what is legally 
required and would not occur in the absence of the project. 
Step 2: Positive list 
The VM0033 Methodology for Tidal Wetland and Seagrass Restoration establishes a positive list for 
tidal wetland restoration activities using the Activity Penetration approach (Option A). Under this 
approach, the level of tidal wetland restoration in the United States was determined to be 2.74 
percent of its maximum potential, which falls below the 5 percent threshold defined under the VCS 
Methodology Requirements to qualify as a positive list activity. 
186 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Although no global datasets are currently available to assess tidal wetland restoration activity levels 
outside the United States, the U.S. is considered to have the most developed national and 
state-level programs for tidal wetland restoration. As such, it is recognized as having the highest 
level of activity penetration worldwide. In light of this, and to ensure a conservative determination, 
the global activity level for tidal wetland restoration is assumed to be equal to or lower than the U.S. 
level of 2.74 percent. 
Given that the ARR/RWE project activity is implemented outside of the United States, and 
considering that it satisfies the applicability conditions outlined in Section 3.1.2 as well as the 
regulatory surplus requirement discussed in Step 1, the project activity is deemed to meet the criteria 
of the positive list under the methodology. 
3.1.5.3 Additionality of ARR (VM0047) 
In accordance with the requirements of Module VM0047, the ARR project demonstrates additionality 
using the performance method, as it adopts an area-based approach. The project must therefore 
follow the steps described below, as outlined in the methodology. 
Step 1: Regulatory surplus 
The analysis and justification for regulatory surplus provided in section 3.1.5.2 for additionality of 
ARR/RWE activities also apply to the ARR activities, as both fall under the same area boundary. As 
demonstrated in the previous section, the project activities are not mandated by any specific law or 
regulation, and no systematic enforcement exists requiring their implementation. Therefore, the ARR 
activities are considered to meet the regulatory surplus requirement. 
Step 2: Performance benchmark 
The performance benchmark was established in accordance with the procedures outlined in 
Appendix 1 of the VM0047 methodology, with detailed results provided in Section 3.2.1.3.7.2. to this 
document. To demonstrate additionality at validation, the project conducted an ex-ante analysis to 
assess the expected difference in performance between the project scenario and the baseline 
scenario. 
Specifically, an ex-ante calculation was performed comparing the estimated change in the project 
stocking index ΔSIwp,t against the forecasted change in the control plot stocking index ΔSIcontrol,t. 
A significance test (Z-test) confirmed that the slope of the ex-ante stocking index in the project area 
is statistically different from that of the control plots. This analysis resulted in a benchmark value of 
zero, indicating that vegetation growth performance in the reference area is lower than in the project 
plots under the project scenario, thereby supporting the demonstration of additionality. 
187 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Performance will be reassessed during each verification period. For future verifications, the 
difference between ΔSIcontrol,t and ΔSIwp,t must remain statistically significant to confirm continued 
additionality under the performance benchmark requirement. 
Step 3: Investment barrier 
The project activity faces a significant investment barrier, as its implementation is not financially 
feasible without revenue from carbon credits. Under the alternative-land-use scenario of BHK I and 
BHK II, the concession area lacks any viable source of income to support reforestation activities. 
There are no existing or planned commercial operations that could generate sufficient cash flow to 
finance restoration or tree-planting efforts. Consequently, the project’s economic viability (based on 
the financial model that would be available to the auditor) depends entirely on carbon credit revenue 
to cover the high upfront and ongoing costs of reforestation, maintenance, and monitoring. Without 
this carbon-related income, the project would not proceed, as it would offer no financial return or 
incentive to invest in ecosystem restoration.  
3.1.5.4 Additionality Methods (VCS, 3.14)
 
The barrier analysis for REDD methodology was conducted based on the investment barrier. A 
literature study was cited to support the investment barrier analysis of the chosen ALUS and the 
REDD project activity. See Section 3.1.5.1 Step 3 for detailed explanation. 
ARR/RWE project activity: Step 2 (Positive List) 
Conformance with the positive list is demonstrated through the additionality assessment provided in 
Section 3.1.5.2 above. 
 
ARR project activity: Area-based Approach (Performance Benchmark) 
The performance benchmark was developed in accordance with the procedures outlined in 
Appendix 1 of the VM0047 methodology.  
The selection of the specific stocking index to be used for biomass quantification is described in 
Section 3.2.1.3.2.2. Once the stocking index was determined, the detailed steps for establishing the 
performance benchmark—consistent with the VM0047 Appendix 1 procedures—are presented in 
Section 3.2.1.3.  
To demonstrate additionality at validation, the project applied an ex-ante calculation to illustrate the 
expected difference between the modelled performance of the project and the forecasted 
performance benchmark. In this section, a test of statistical significance was conducted to compare 
the ex-ante estimated rate of change in the stocking index (slope) between the project plots and the 
control plots. The analysis demonstrated that the ex-ante slope of the stocking index in the control 
plots is significantly different from that in the project plots.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

Additionality will be reassessed during each verification period, where the parameters ΔSIcontrol,t 
and ΔSIwp,t must remain significantly different, as determined by a Z-test, to confirm continued 
compliance with the performance benchmark. 
3.1.6 Methodology Deviations (VCS, 3.20) 
VMD0016 (X-STR module) provides recommended default sampling densities for peat-depth 
measurements, including narrower observation spacing (≤100 m) and tighter transect spacing (≤200 
m). The project, however, implemented peat-depth sampling following the  Indonesian Ministry of 
Environment and Forestry Regulation No. 14/2017 (PermenLHK 14/2017), which prescribes a 
coarser systematic grid- and transect-based survey design (500-m sampling intervals and 2–3 km 
transect spacing). This constitutes a deviation from the default sampling density guidance in 
VMD0016. 
The peat depth sampling approach adopted by the project is based on Peraturan Menteri LHK 
Nomor P.14 Tahun 2017, the official national standard for peatland inventory and peat hydrological 
unit (PHU) assessment. This regulation provides a structured, government-endorsed framework for 
determining sampling point locations, transect orientation, and peat ecosystem characterization. The 
P.14/2017 sampling methodology is widely used across Indonesia and has been demonstrated to be 
technically suitable for delineating peat extent, identifying peat domes, and stratifying peatland 
conditions. The resulting strata provide the foundation for the project’s carbon stock assessment. 
Although the sampling density under P.14/2017 is broader than the default spacing recommended 
within VMD0016, its application remains scientifically robust and appropriate for this project based 
on the following: 
1. Methodological consistency with VMD0016: 
The P.14/2017 framework employs a systematic, transect-based approach consistent with 
the methodological intent of VMD0016. It ensures structured spatial coverage and repeatable 
placement of observation points, enabling effective stratification and characterization of peat 
variability across the project area. 
2. Integration of secondary datasets for improved stratification: 
The P.14/2017 methodology incorporates multiple secondary data sources—including 
national peat depth maps, digital elevation models, and geomorphological information—to 
guide the placement and orientation of transects. These datasets support the identification of 
peat domes and areas with expected variability, ensuring that the sampling design remains 
representative within each defined stratum. 
3. Independent expert review and validation of sampling design: 
Prior to field deployment, the project’s peat sampling plan was reviewed by an independent 
peatland expert. The expert assessed whether the sampling density, transect spacing, and 
stratification approach were adequate to produce a representative and defensible peat depth 
189 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
map. The expert confirmed that the methodology—when paired with conservative 
interpolation and interpretation procedures—provides a sufficiently robust basis for 
estimating peat depth variability across the project area. 
4. Accessibility constraints and risk-based substitution approach: 
Certain areas within the project boundary were inaccessible due to documented safety 
hazards, including the presence of unexploded ordnance (“pig bombs”). In accordance with 
the project’s Health, Safety, and Security protocols, field teams were not permitted to enter 
these high-risk areas. For these limited locations, validated secondary peat depth datasets 
were employed as substitutes. This substitution was conducted conservatively to avoid 
potential underestimation of peat depth while ensuring full compliance with safety 
requirements. 
The combination of: (i) implementation of a nationally recognized peat inventory standard, (ii) 
integration of multiple secondary datasets, (iii) independent expert validation, and (iv) conservative 
treatment of inaccessible areas, ensures that the resulting peat-depth dataset is representative, 
scientifically defensible, and conservative in accordance with the intent of VMD0016. This deviation 
does not increase the risk of overestimation of carbon stocks or emission reductions and therefore 
remains fully aligned with Verra’s principles of accuracy, completeness, and conservativeness.

1. Changes in aboveground biomass stocks (characterized by conversion to plantations); 
2. Emission from peat drainage;  
3. Emission from ditches and other open water bodies; and 
4. Emission from peat fire.

3.2.1.1.2.1 Baseline carbon stock changes (∆퐶퐵푆퐿,푖,푡) 
Carbon stock changes in the aboveground tree and non-tree biomass are assumed to be released 
immediately at the time of deforestation. In contrast, emissions from belowground biomass are 
allocated over a 10-year period, with 1/10 of the stock change emitted annually following 
deforestation. Carbon stocks potentially entering the harvested wood products pool are typically 
allocated over a 100-year period at an annual rate of 1/20 of the stock for 20 years. However, in this 
project, no carbon stocks are expected to enter the wood products pool; therefore, this component is 
conservatively set to zero. Similarly, the litter and deadwood pools are excluded from the baseline 
calculation, and their contribution is also considered zero. 
For a given year t, emissions are summed across areas deforested from time t-10 up to time t (for 
belowground biomass). The contribution from belowground biomass is only accounted for areas on 
mineral soils and shallow peat, as defined under the VCS methodology (Equation 13). Consequently, 
the baseline calculation in this project is limited to aboveground and belowground tree biomass 
pools, consistent with the justification provided in Section 3.1.3.5.

Rate of deforestation (퐷%푝푙푎푛푛푒푑,푖,푡)  
As there is no verifiable plan for conversion or deforestation within the project area, the requirements 
of Module BL-PL were applied. Accordingly, a minimum of six proxy areas were selected to establish 
the baseline rate of conversion/deforestation specifically for the establishment of industrial 
plantations. These proxy areas were further analyzed to demonstrate that no risk of abandonment is 
present. The analytical procedures described below were followed to determine a conservative 
baseline rate of industrial plantation development in line with the BL-PL module. 
We selected suitable proxy areas as reference regions through an iterative process of data 
collection, compilation, review and processing of national-level datasets. Our description of the 
nationally available datasets used for performing iterative analyses to select appropriate proxy 
areas. 
Suitable reference regions were identified through an iterative process involving the collection, 
compilation, review, and processing of nationally available datasets. The following criteria were then 
applied to determine the applicability of proxy areas for establishing the deforestation rate: 
1. Land conversion practices must be the same as those used by the baseline agent or class 
of agent  
2. The post-deforestation land use must be the same in the proxy areas as expected in the 
project area under business as usual  
3. The proxy areas must have the same management and land use rights type as the 
proposed project area under business as usual  
4. If suitable sites exist they must be in the immediate area of the project; if an insufficient 
number of sites exists in the immediate area of the project, sites must be identified 
elsewhere in the same country as the project; if an insufficient number of sites exists in the 
country, sites must be identified in neighboring countries  
5. Agents of deforestation in proxy areas must have deforested their land under the same 
criteria that the project lands must follow (legally permissible and suitable for conversion).  
6. Deforestation in the proxy area must have occurred within the 10 years prior to the baseline 
period.  
7. The three following conditions must be met:  
● The forest types surrounding the proxy area or in the proxy area prior to 
deforestation must be in the same proportion as in the project area (±20%). 
● Soil types that are suitable for the land-use practice used by the agent of 
deforestation in the project area must be present in the proxy area in the same 
proportion as the project area (±20%). The ratio of slope classes “gentle” (slope 
<15%) to “steep” (slope ≥ 15%) in the reference regions shall be (± 20%) the same 
of the ratio in the project area; and 
● Elevation classes (500-m classes) in the reference region shall be in the same 
proportion as in the project area (± 20%). 
198 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

Following application of the above analytical framework, eight proxy areas were identified that fulfill 
all required criteria. The conversion practices and post-deforestation land use in these areas closely 
align with those expected in the project area baseline scenario. Furthermore, the analysis confirmed 
that the selected proxy areas demonstrate ongoing conversion to industrial plantations, ensuring that 
they are representative of business-as-usual conditions and not subject to the risk of abandonment.

Figure 36. Location of the project area and the eight reference regions selected for proxy areas.   
 
Based on this assessment, we estimate the average annual conversion/deforestation rate across the 
eight proxy areas was determined to be 5.08%. This rate is conservative, as it is lower than the 
verified rates applied in other Verra-registered projects such as the Katingan Peatland Restoration 
and Conservation Project (7.82%) and the Sumatra Merang Peatland Project/SMPP (7.85%), and 
also below the 12-13% annual conversion/deforestation rate reported in the SMPP PDD as the 
typical rate of Industrial Plantation (HTI) concession practices in Indonesia.  
 
Furthermore, as shown in Table 33, the annual baseline planned deforestation remains within the 
limit defined by the 5.08% deforestation rate, equivalent to approximately 568 ha/year. The projected 
average deforestation over the 20-year is even lower, at 558 ha/year (see Table 33 above), which 
demonstrates that the applied rate is conservative both in comparison with registered project and 
common practices, and also with the internal project allocation of annual deforestation. Therefore, 
the baseline rate of conversion/deforestation uncertainty was set to zero. 
 
Likelihood of deforestation (퐿 − 퐷푖 ) and risk of abandonment 
In accordance with Module BL-PL, when forest areas subject to planned deforestation are not under 
government control and/or have been designated for conversion, the value of 퐿−퐷푖 shall be set at 
201 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
100%. For the project area, although no concession has yet been allocated, the land is under 
imminent threat of conversion to industrial plantations through concession licenses expected to be 
granted in the future. Given this context, the likelihood of deforestation is conservatively set at 100%. 
 
The risk of abandonment was assessed using the eight proxy areas previously selected for 
determining the average annual deforestation rate. After confirming the applicability criteria in the 
proxy area analysis, we conducted visual interpretation of Landsat satellite imagery to assess 
whether any of these proxy areas showed evidence of abandonment or forest regrowth. The 
analysis confirmed that all proxy areas experienced ongoing conversion and industrial plantation 
establishment over the period 2001–2023. Consequently, the risk of abandonment is considered 
negligible, thereby supporting the continued applicability of the BL-PL module. 
Baseline carbon stock change in aboveground tree biomass (∆퐶퐴퐵푡푟푒푒,푖) 
The baseline carbon stock in aboveground tree biomass was determined for both pre-deforestation 
and post-deforestation conditions. Pre-deforestation values reflect the existing land cover at the 
project start date and were derived from field-based measurements of aboveground biomass (AGB) 
within the project area. Post-deforestation values represent industrial plantations and were obtained 
from published literature. 
 
Carbon stock changes in the AGB pool were calculated by subtracting post-deforestation carbon 
stocks from pre-deforestation forest carbon stocks, as presented in Equation 16. The average 
baseline carbon stock change in aboveground tree biomass for each stratum is summarized in Table 
37 below.

3.2.1.1.3 Net GHG emission in the WRC baseline scenario on peatland (GHG BSL-PEAT) 
To assess the net GHG emissions from peat soil in the baseline scenario, equation 18 above is 
applied across all strata where the project duration exceeds the peat depletion time. The 
quantification of the baseline emissions in the wetland SOC pools is done through the following three 
steps: 
1. GHG emissions from peat soil due to microbial decomposition.  
2. GHG emissions from open water.  
3. GHG emissions from peat burning.

Baseline GHG emissions from Ditches and Other Open Water Bodies 
(GHGpeatditch-BSL,i,t) 
Under baseline conditions, the only open water features expected to occur consist of rivers and 
drainage canals. Other types of open water bodies, such as ponds or artificial lakes, are excluded, 
as their development is not associated with the baseline land-use agents. It was further established 
that no drainage canals existed prior to the project start date, and that no such canals will be 
constructed under the project scenario. 
The estimated extent of drainage canals and ditches in the baseline was derived based on the 
Ministry of Environment and Forestry (KLHK) standards for road construction, combined with remote 
sensing analysis. The spatial and temporal stratification of these water bodies is illustrated 
inFigure35. 
As the IPCC does not provide default emission factors for CO₂ and CH₄ from open water bodies, 
emissions were derived using default values for dissolved organic carbon (ΔDOC), as specified in 
Table 39. Accordingly, GHG emissions from ditches and open water bodies in the baseline scenario 
derived on the basis of ditched area and area of open water combined with an emission factor, 
following Equation 22 below.

Assessing Baseline GHG Emissions due to Peat Fire (GHGpeatburn-BSL,I,t) 
According to module E-BPB, GHG emissions from biomass burning can result from the following: 
1. Conversion of forest land to non-forest land using fire.  
2. Periodical burning of grassland or agricultural land after deforestation.  
3. Controlled burning in forest land remaining forest land.  
4. Uncontrolled fire in drained peat swamp forest.  
5. Uncontrolled peat burning in (abandoned) drained peat sites. 
 
For the baseline scenario, emissions from peat burning were calculated using the estimated mass of 
peat carbon lost through combustion combined with emission factors reported in scientific literature 
(Equation 23 below).

In the project area, none of these conditions are applicable. Burning for land clearing is prohibited by 
law, and there is no evidence of controlled or uncontrolled peat fires within the project boundary. 
Therefore, baseline GHG emissions from peat burning are considered negligible and were set equal 
to zero.

3.2.1.2 ARR-RWE 
3.2.1.2.1 Projection of Future Conditions 
In the baseline scenario, the ex-ante projection of greenhouse gas (GHG) pools and emissions is 
derived from an assessment of future conditions. These future conditions and their relevant variables 
are estimated in accordance with the procedures set out in VCS module VMD0019 (Methods to 
Project Future Conditions).  
3.2.1.2.2 Emissions and removals in the baseline scenario  
Baseline emissions are derived from three primary sources: (i) carbon stock change in biomass, (ii) 
emissions from soil, and (iii) emissions from fuel. The net CO₂e emissions under the baseline 
scenario are calculated using the equations presented below.

3.2.1.2.3 Net carbon stock change in biomass carbon pools in the baseline scenario

The baseline scenario represents degraded mangrove habitats with negligible vegetation cover and 
no evidence of significant natural recovery. Accordingly, baseline biomass is assumed to be zero. 
However, in line with methodological requirements, it is necessary to demonstrate that greenhouse 
gas (GHG) removals from natural recovery in the baseline scenario are insignificant and can 
therefore be considered de minimis. 
To assess whether pre-project vegetation would contribute significant carbon stocks in the baseline 
scenario, remote sensing imagery was analyzed for the presence of mangrove trees. Landsat 
imagery from 2014, 2019, and 2024 was used to examine historical land cover trends. The analysis 
shows that areas within the ARR-RWE project area transitioned from mangrove forest cover to 
deforested areas, largely due to conversion to aquaculture ponds. The comparison is presented in 
Figure 37 and Table 45. 
Consistent with the Tool for testing significance of GHG emissions in A/R CDM project activities 
(CDM AR Tool 4), the proportion of natural recovery areas is below 5% of the project area, and 
therefore can be considered de minimis. 
218 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Consequently, GHG removals from pre-project vegetation in the baseline scenario are deemed 
insignificant and do not require deduction from the project scenario. Since the baseline removals are 
effectively zero, no correction is necessary, and no part of the project area is excluded from the 
calculation of GHG removals, as the share of potential natural recovery remains below the permitted 
de minimis threshold.

baseline scenario are estimated using the following equation:

CO2 emissions from soil – in situ 
In the baseline scenario, the planting areas are classified as non-forest, where natural recovery of 
mangroves is not significant (refer to Section 3.2.1.2.3). Accordingly, soil organic carbon (SOC) 
levels are assumed to have reached a steady state prior to the initiation of restoration activities. 
Following the guidance in VM0033, a default factor for GHG
BSL-insitu-CO2,i,t

restricted to areas with a crown or vegetation cover of at least 50% (equation 32). For areas with 
less than 15% cover, SOC accumulation is deemed insignificant and conservatively accounted for as 
zero, while areas with cover between 15% and 50% may be assessed through linear interpolation.

For the NMPP, the baseline scenario consists of non-forest land, with no accumulation of soil organic

3.2.1.2.4.3 CH
4
 and N
2
O emissions from soil   
According to VM0033, CH₄ and N₂O emissions from soil may be conservatively excluded if 
conditions for such emissions are not expected to differ between the baseline and project scenarios, 
or if conditions in the project scenario will decline. Furthermore, the methodology specifies that for 
strata where soil erosion occurs, or soils are exposed to an aerobic environment through excavation 
or drainage, both CH₄ and N₂O emissions are conservatively set to zero. 
223 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
In the baseline scenario, aquaculture pond construction involves soil excavation that exposes soils 
to aerobic conditions; under such circumstances, and consistent with VM0033, both CH₄ and N₂O 
emissions from soil are conservatively set to zero.

For this project, fossil fuel emissions are conservatively excluded in both the baseline and project

scenario, these emissions are not accounted for within the project boundary and are therefore 
conservatively set to zero. 
3.2.1.2.6 Estimation of the sum of baseline emission for ARR-RWE project activity 
The total baseline emissions for the ARR/RWE project activity are estimated as the sum of (i) carbon 
stock change in biomass, (ii) soil emissions, and (iii) fossil fuel emissions. However, since no 
emissions are expected to occur in the absence of the project activity, the baseline emission for the 
ARR/RWE project activity is considered to be zero. 
 
3.2.1.3 ARR  
3.2.1.3.1 Selection of project plots 
Stratification of the project area was carried out to ensure that sampling adequately represents the 
biophysical conditions within the project boundary. Project plots were selected using a stratified 
random sampling approach in accordance with VM0047 requirements. As stipulated by the 
methodology, a minimum of 30 project plots must be established. Each project plot corresponds to a 
224 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
single pixel from the satellite imagery used in the project, with a spatial resolution of 30 m × 30 m. 
This resolution defines each project plot as an area of 0.09 hectares. 
From the pool of potential project plots, a total of 30 plots were randomly selected to represent all 
strata within the project area. Each project plot is subject to periodic monitoring of the annual 
stocking index, which forms the basis for performance benchmark assessment. The spatial 
distribution and exact locations of the selected project plots within the project boundary are 
presented in Figure 38 below. 
 
Figure 38. Project plot across the project area. 
 
3.2.1.3.2 Selection of control plots for each project plot 
3.2.1.3.2.1 Define donor pool area 
The donor pool area is defined as the geographic region that meets all spatial criteria listed in Table 
46 below. The purpose of delineating this area is to establish an eligible pool from which control plots 
can be selected to ensure comparability with project area conditions. Identification of the donor pool 
was conducted through a series of overlay analyses using Geographic Information Systems (GIS). 
225 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
The factors considered in this analysis follow the spatial criteria for donor plots as outlined in 
VM0047, Appendix Performance Benchmark. While no specific data sources are prescribed by 
Verra, all datasets applied in this project were obtained from official government agencies and 
represent the most up-to-date information available. 
To ensure precision in donor pool identification and mapping, each geospatial dataset employed had 
a resolution no coarser than 30 × 30 meters. The areas ultimately selected as eligible control plots 
include permanent production forest (HP) and convertible production forest (HPK) that are not 
currently under concession, and that are located within the same ecoregion as the project area. This 
approach minimizes potential bias in the control plot selection process.

Remote Sensing Analysis

3.2.1.3.2.2 Evaluate project plots 
Historic and initial conditions of the stocking index were quantified using a time series of 
observations from 2015 to 2025 with kernel Normalized Difference Vegetation Index (kNDVI). Prior 
to selecting kNDVI as the definitive Spectral Index (SI), a comparative analysis was conducted on 
several other indices. This evaluation included the Normalized Difference Vegetation Index (NDVI), 
Enhanced Vegetation Index (EVI), Normalized Difference Moisture Index (NDMI), and Normalized 
Burn Ratio (NBR), all derived from Landsat 8 & 9 imagery, as well as the Radar Vegetation Index 
(RVI) from Sentinel-1 data. All optical indices were analyzed at a 30 m grid size, consistent with the 
native resolution of the Landsat data. The selection was based on two criteria: the strength of the 
correlation (R
2
) between index values and biomass data, and the visual consistency of multitemporal 
trends with observable vegetation growth in historical imagery. Among all candidates, kNDVI was 
chosen because it demonstrated the highest correlation value and the most consistent performance 
over time.

To define the donor pool, a 100 km buffer was applied to the project

3.2.1.3.2.3 Select control plots 
From the identified donor pool area, a total of 100,000 candidate control plots were systematically 
generated, as seen in Figure 39 below. Each candidate control plot was used to extract the 
Normalized Difference Vegetation Index (NDVI), which served as the stocking index for the years 
corresponding to the project plots, namely 2015, 2016, 2017, 2018, 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022, 2023, 
and 2024. 
227 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 39. Project and candidate control plot of the project area 
 
Project plots and control plots were then paired for benchmarking purposes. Each project plot was 
matched with five control plots to minimize potential sampling bias. Pairing with multiple control plots 
ensures robustness in capturing variability across the donor pool area. The location of each project 
plot remains fixed for the entire project lifetime. In contrast, control plots may change over time if 
regulatory updates or changes in land use status render certain plots no longer eligible. In such 
cases, a re-selection of control plots is conducted to ensure compliance with the matching criteria, 
specifically alignment with the land use status of the corresponding project plot. 
Historical evaluation of the stocking index was conducted for three benchmark years: t-10 (2015), t-8 
(2017), and t0 (2024). Only the NDVI-derived stocking index values from these benchmark years 
were used as covariates in the matching process, given their direct correlation with aboveground 
biomass (expressed in tons per hectare). 
For each control plot, a multivariate Euclidean distance matrix was computed based on the covariate 
vector relative to the project plots. To match control plots with project plots, a k-nearest neighbor 
optimal matching approach without replacement was applied, meaning that each control plot may 
only be matched to one project plot. The number of matched control plots per project plot (k) was 
fixed at five, consistent across the project lifetime. The k control plots with the lowest multivariate 
distance metric values were selected, and relative weights were derived proportional to the inverse 
of the distance metric values, such that the weights sum to one, as specified in Equation 39 below. 
228 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

The spatial distribution of the control plots and donor pool area used in the matching process is

3.2.1.3.4 Monitoring control and project plot 
During each monitoring cycle, the stocking index of both project and control plots is recalculated 
using the most recent imagery. Prior to this step, the validity of all control plots is assessed. Any 
control plot found to fall within an ongoing subnational program that provides tree-planting incentives 
(to which the project area does not belong), or within the boundaries of an AFOLU project officially 
registered under a carbon offset scheme, may be declared invalid (this condition is optional in the 
latter case). If such invalid plots are identified, they are excluded and substituted with replacements 
drawn from the previously established donor pool. Following replacement, the weights assigned to 
all control plots are adjusted to ensure they sum to one. 
3.2.1.3.5 Derive and evaluate slopes for time series of stocking indices 
In this step, the time series of stocking index (SI) values is used to derive and evaluate the slopes for 
both project plots and their matched control plots. The analysis requires the construction of 
accumulated time series of ΔSI
control,t
 and ΔSI
wp,t
 values from time t=0 up to time t across the full set of 
230 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
sample populations. To ensure methodological consistency, at least three temporal observations 
must be included in the regression: the baseline year (t=0), the monitoring year (t), and one or more 
intermediate time points between these two. 
For a matched set to be included, SI data must be consistently available for the project plot and all of 
its associated control plots at the monitoring year t. In cases where imagery gaps occur (e.g., cloud 
cover or sensor malfunction) and SI values are missing for one or more plots, the entire matched set 
is excluded from the slope estimation at that time step. Despite these exclusions, the analysis must 
retain a minimum of 30 project plots to ensure a representative sample size. 
The slopes of ΔSI
control,t
 and ΔSI
wp,t

accumulated time series of SI values for control and project plots, respectively.

3.2.1.3.7 Ex-Ante Estimation 
The methodology requires that, at the time t of validation, estimates must be made for the 10-year 
period from t to t+10. In this project, however, estimates were extended to cover the entire 40-year 
crediting period. 
3.2.1.3.7.1 Estimate of clearing of pre-existing biomass   
232 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
The assessment of clearing was conducted using stocking index values calculated for each project 
plot over the period 2014–2023. To evaluate potential clearing, an ordinary least squares (OLS) 
regression was applied to the stocking index time series from 2015 to 2025. The regression slope 
was found to be statistically non-significant, indicating that no detectable clearing of pre-existing 
biomass occurred within the project site during this period.

3.2.1.3.7.2 Ex-ante performance benchmark 
The ex-ante performance benchmark was derived following the procedures outlined in Section 
3.2.1.3, with two key adjustments: (i) the stocking index for the project plots was projected using 
ex-ante growth estimates, and (ii) the stocking index for the control plots was based on the historical 
record from the past ten years, rather than on the monitoring period. 
For the project plots, stocking index values were projected from expected growth and yield 
estimates, as described in Section 3.2.2.2.3.1. These projections were converted into stocking index 
values using regression parameters that capture the correlation between biomass and stocking 
index. The resulting modeled time series was analyzed through ordinary least squares regression, 
with the slope of the fitted line representing ΔSIwp. The spatial distribution of ARR planting activities 
across mineral and peatland areas, including areas of overlap, is illustrated in Figure 42. The 
quantitative results of the projections are then presented in two separate tables: one summarizing 
the predicted values for mineral planting areas (Table 50) and another presenting the corresponding 
results for peatland planting areas (Table 51).

A statistical comparison was then conducted between the ex-ante slope for the project plots and that 
of the control plots, as described in Section 3.2.1.3. For mineral planting areas, the Z-score was 
calculated as 9.08, while for peatland planting areas, the Z-score was 6.57. Both values exceed the 
threshold of 1.96, confirming that the differences in slopes between project and control plots are 
statistically significant. Consequently, the performance benchmark could not be assigned the default 
value of 1. Given that the slope for the control plots was negative, the benchmark value was 
conservatively set to 0 for both mineral and peatland cases.
