3.2.2.1.4 Estimation of the sum of project emissions for REDD-APWD project activity 
The project carbon stock changes per stratum from the planned deforestation are summed to obtain 
the sum of the project carbon stock changes due to deforestation and forest degradation plus total 
GHG emissions minus any increase in eligible forest carbon stock. As there are no deforestation, 
degradation, or natural disturbance activities within the project scenario, the total project emissions 
from the planned deforestation parts of the REDD-APWD project are equal to zero. 
Project GHG emissions from wetland are estimated based on GHG emissions from peat soils, water 
bodies, and peat burning. A summary of the total project emissions from the wetland parts of the 
REDD-APWD project can be found in Table 55 below.

3.2.2.2 ARR-RWE 
3.2.2.2.1 Projection of Future Conditions 
In the project scenario, the ex-ante projection of greenhouse gas (GHG) pools and emissions is 
derived from an assessment of future conditions. These future conditions and their relevant variables 
are estimated in accordance with the procedures set out in VCS module VMD0019 (Methods to 
Project Future Conditions).

Emissions from prescribed burning are not accounted for, as no project activities are expected to

baseline and project scenarios, as they are conservatively set to zero. Further details are provided in

3.2.2.2.3 Net carbon stock change in biomass carbon pools in the project scenario  
3.2.2.2.3.1 Planting areas under ARR-RWE Activity 
In the project scenario, the primary intervention consists of reforestation through mangrove planting 
on degraded land. Planting activities will commence in 2029 in accordance with the project schedule. 
Approximately 143 hectares are targeted for restoration, as shown in Figure 41. The coloured 
polygons delineate areas planted and to be planted under the ARR-RWE activity. A summary of 
executed and targeted planting areas is presented in Table 56. 
Table 56. ARR-RWE planting area

246 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 41. ARR-RWE planting area 
In addition to active planting, natural colonisation is expected to occur in areas that gradually 
become intertidal due to sea level rise. These new habitats are anticipated to be naturally 
regenerated by mangrove propagules from nearby stands. Conversely, some restored areas are 
projected to be lost due to submergence and erosion caused by sea level rise (see Section 
3.1.3.2.3). In accordance with VM0033, the submergence of a given stratum results in the loss of 
aboveground biomass carbon stocks through oxidation, and it is assumed that this carbon is 
immediately and entirely released to the atmosphere. 
Ex-ante CO₂ emissions and removals are estimated to provide the initial projection of project 
performance. These estimates account for: 
● Mangrove areas that are actively reforested under the ARR-RWE project activity  
● The loss of planted area due to sea level rise, giving rise to a zone actively reforested with 
mangrove trees but then submerged/eroded and lost (‘LOSS’)  
● The gain of new mangrove habitat due to sea level rise (‘GAIN’) 
These same factors, together with canopy cover development over time, will also be incorporated 
into the ex-post stratification applied during monitoring, ensuring that the actual distribution and 
condition of mangrove stands are represented. Based on the ex-ante estimates, forecasts are 
developed to show the expected annual changes in CO₂ removals over the crediting period, using

3.2.2.2.3.3 Ex-ante project removals as a result of ARR-RWE reforestation activities   
The methodology requires that, at the time t of validation, estimates must be made for the 10-year 
period from t to t+10. In this project, however, estimates were extended to cover the entire 40-year 
crediting period. 
Woody biomass 
The accumulation of carbon in woody biomass was estimated ex-ante by applying a stand-level 
growth model. Tree growth within the project area was represented using the Chapman-Richards 
(CR) growth function
28
, a widely recognized sigmoid-shaped model of biological growth. This 
function was selected as it effectively reflects the natural progression of forest development, 
beginning with a slow establishment phase, followed by a period of accelerated growth, and 
eventually stabilizing as the stand approaches its maximum biomass capacity. The CR model 
parameters applied in this project include: 
● MAX: the potential maximum biomass per hectare (t CO₂e ha⁻¹); 
● k: a dimensionless growth rate; and 
● m: a dimensionless curve-shape parameter. 
 
The CR growth curve applied in this project follows the standard three-parameter formulation:  
 
        (66) 
∆퐶

To ensure the parameters were appropriate for the project context, ecosystem type was determined 
based on rainfall regime, following the classification of rainforest, moist forest, or dry forest. This 
classification provided the basis for assigning representative values of k and m.

 AFOLU Carbon Initiative. (2014). AFOLU-C calculator series: Afforestation/reforestation.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

The potential biomass (MAX) defines the upper boundary of carbon accumulation and corresponds 
to the greatest biomass density that the site can support under mature forest conditions. This value 
is largely determined by climatic and edaphic factors and is assumed to be independent of planting 
density, species composition, or silvicultural interventions, provided that forest regeneration is 
successful. While such factors do not alter the potential biomass, they may influence the trajectory of 
growth and the timeframe over which maximum biomass is reached. 
For biomass estimation, the project applied allometric equations developed by Chave et al. (2005)
29
 
for moist forest stands (equation 67). Species-specific allometric models for the specific planted 
species were not available; therefore, the project adopted the generalized Chave equations, which 
are widely accepted for tropical forest conditions. These equations incorporate tree diameter, height, 
and wood density to reliably estimate aboveground biomass across diverse forest conditions. The 
resulting biomass values were then converted from dry matter (t dm ha⁻¹) to carbon dioxide 
equivalent (t CO₂e ha⁻¹) using the established conversion factors. 
         (67)

The fitted CR curve, combined with the Chave allometric approach, provided annual estimates of 
biomass accumulation per hectare. These values were then multiplied by the annual planting areas 
specified in the project’s planting schedule. The results represent the ex-ante projected GHG 
removals from woody biomass attributable to the reforestation activities. A detailed dataset, including 
references for all allometric equations applied, is provided in the supporting documentation. 
3.2.2.2.4 Net GHG emissions from soil in the project scenario 
In the NMPP area, soils are expected to accumulate SOC as a result of the ARR/RWE activity. 
Greenhouse gas emissions from soil are estimated by accounting for multiple sources. These 
include CO₂ emissions released from the soil organic carbon (SOC) pool, deductions applied to 
exclude the proportion of carbon originating from allochthonous sources, and emissions of CH₄ and 
N₂O from the SOC pool. Accordingly, the net GHG emissions from soil in the project scenario are 
estimated using the following equation:

CO2 emissions from soil – in situ 
SOC levels are closely correlated with crown or vegetation cover. At the start of the project, the 
default value—adjusted to exclude allochthonous carbon—is conservatively applied. This factor is 
restricted to areas with a crown or vegetation cover of at least 50% (Equation 70). For areas with 
less than 15% cover, SOC accumulation is considered insignificant and conservatively set to zero, 
while areas with crown cover between 15% and 50% are estimated through linear interpolation. 
      (70) 
퐺퐻퐺

= −1.46 푡 퐶 ℎ푎

×44/12

In the NMPP area, mangrove plantations are expected to achieve a minimum crown cover of 50% 
after 15 years. To account for variability, a conservative canopy development trajectory is applied, 
assuming 15% cover at Year 5 and 50% cover at Year 15. Linear interpolation is applied for the 
period between Year 5 and Year 15. 
The resulting GHG emissions from the SOC pool, based on vegetation cover development in the 
project scenario, are presented in Table 58.

CO2 emissions from eroded soil 
Sea level rise over a 100-year period is expected to result in erosion and loss of wetland area. The 
projected wetland loss has been assessed in 5-year intervals, where each interval reflects the 
associated carbon release and CO₂ emissions from eroded soils. Within the erosion analysis 
described in Section 3.1.3.2.3.2, the areas identified as being at risk have been grouped into strata 
referred to as strata prone to erosion. The potential emissions from these erosion-prone strata have 
been quantified using the equations presented below.

For the NMPP, since the project scenario involves the accumulation of SOC due to mangrove 
reforestation, a deduction for allochthonous carbon is required. To account for this, field data were 
used to determine the percentage of soil organic carbon that is allochthonous (%C
alloch
), applying the 
generalized deduction estimate proposed by Needelman et al. (2018): 
 
%C
alloch
 = 213.17 × %C
soil
-1.184
         (76) 
 
where %C
alloch
 represents the proportion of allochthonous carbon deducted from the total SOC, and 
%C is the percentage of soil carbon. Based on field data and applying this approach, the proportion 
of allochthonous carbon was estimated at 26%, which was then used in the calculation of 
Deductionalloch with GHGWPS-insitu-CO2,i,t referenced from Section 3.2.2.2.4.1. 
 
3.2.2.2.4.3 CH
4
 and N
2
O emissions from soil   
In the project scenario, emissions of CH₄ and N₂O from soil are conservatively set to zero in 
accordance with the guidance provided in VM0033. No project activities, such as soil disturbance 
from erosion or excavation, are expected to occur that would alter anaerobic conditions or enhance 
methane-producing processes, and therefore no significant CH₄ emissions are anticipated. Similarly, 
N₂O emissions are not expected, as the project does not involve aquaculture practices, the addition 
of feed or fertilizers, or the lowering of water levels—all of which typically promote nitrification and 
denitrification processes. Since none of these conditions apply to the project scenario, the exclusion 
of both CH₄ and N₂O emissions is considered appropriate and conservative. 
3.2.2.2.5 Net GHG emissions from prescribed burning in the project scenario

3.2.2.3.2 Ex-ante project removals as a result of ARR reforestation activities   
The methodology requires that, at the time t of validation, estimates must be made for the 10-year 
period from t to t+10. In this project, however, estimates were extended to cover the entire 40-year 
crediting period. 
Woody biomass 
The primary intervention of the project consists of reforestation through active tree planting on 
degraded land. According to the project’s planting plan, approximately 934 hectares of land will be 
restored through planting activities. Planting activities will commence in 2028, and a summary of 
executed and targeted planting areas is presented in Table 60. 
Table 60. ARR planting area 
Planting Strata 
Planting Area 
(Ha) 
Mineral 407 
Peatland 527 
Total 934 
 
Planting will be conducted progressively over a period of 10 years, in line with the project schedule. 
The procedure for estimating GHG removals from these planting activities, based on the fitted CR

curve combined with the Chave allometric approach, is described in Section 3.2.2.2.3.1 above 
(equation 66). 
 
The designated ARR planting areas in BHK II are further divided into two categories: mineral 
planting areas and peatland planting areas. This stratification ensures that project interventions 
reflect the ecological characteristics of each land type. The spatial distribution of these planting 
areas, including the overlap of ARR activities on mineral soils and peatlands, is illustrated in the 
project planting map provided below (Figure 42).

3.2.3.1 Leakage for REDD-APWD project activities 
Application of the activity-shifting leakage (LK-ASP) module requires aligning the methodological 
procedures with the specific conditions of the project area. An assessment of baseline conditions, as 
outlined in Section 3.1.4, indicates that the predominant deforestation driver is the conversion of 
forested land to industrial plantations. The LK-ASP Module (Section 5.2) specifies three 
methodological approaches for estimating emissions attributable to such land-use changes. For this 
analysis, Approach 1 was identified as the most applicable and is therefore adopted. The procedural 
steps implemented are consistent with the framework set forth in Section 5.1 of the module, while 
evaluation of potential leakage in peatland zones is undertaken in accordance with the guidance 
provided in Section 5.3 of the module.

3.2.3.1.1 Steps to estimate activity shifting leakage for avoiding planned deforestation 
 
STEP 1: Determination of the Baseline Rate of Forest Clearance by the Deforestation Agent 
 
Of the three options provided in the LK-ASP Module for estimating the baseline forest clearance rate 
attributable to the identified deforestation agent, Option 1.1 (Historical Deforestation Trend) was 
selected for this assessment. Using this approach, the baseline annual deforestation attributable to 
the identified agent is determined by extrapolating historical annual trends through linear regression 
analysis. To establish the trend, information must be collected from surveys of the deforestation 
agent and from official records in order to quantify the total area cleared annually by the agent within 
the country. Data covering at least five years, and up to a maximum of ten years, are required for the 
regression analysis. The resulting regression must meet the statistical requirements of p ≤ 0.05 and 
adjusted R² ≥ 0.75. If these criteria are not satisfied, Option 1.2 shall be applied. 
          (84)

STEP 5: Estimate of Peat Carbon in All of the Agent’s Concessions 
The first step of the peatland leakage estimation process begins with identifying and calculating the 
peat carbon stock across all concessions owned by the deforestation agent, in this case, industrial 
plantation concessions. The estimation is based on the following assumptions: 
● Drained peatland is considered equivalent to deforested peatland. 
● Licensed HPK and HP areas will eventually be drained. 
● All peatlands in unlicensed areas are assumed to be forested and undrained. 
265 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
At the project start date, the following locations are identified: 
● Licensed HPK and HP areas. 
● Unlicensed HPK and HP areas. 
● Combined licensed and unlicensed HPK/HP areas. 
Based on the estimated area planned for conversion or expected to generate peat emissions, the 
most likely scenario is conversion occurring in unlicensed HPK and HP areas. These areas serve as 
the basis for calculating peat carbon stock loss. 
Emission factors for activity-shifting leakage are determined based on carbon loss at PDT 
(C
peatloss,t,PDT
) in the undrained peatland of alternative areas. PDTs are estimated in accordance with 
equations (1) to (12) in Module X-STR, applying the same drainability limit restrictions as used for 
the project area. 
The land-use designation of HP and HPK immediately prior to the project start date is used to 
identify areas potentially generating emissions from shifting leakage. It is assumed that in peatlands, 
deforestation is always accompanied by drainage, and conservatively, drained peatland area is 
considered equal to deforested peatland area. Carbon loss is calculated based on peat thickness 
loss at tPDT, bulk density similar to that of the project area, and carbon content as defined in the 
prescribed equations.

STEP 6: Estimate of the CO2 Emission Factor from Leakage to Peatland per ha 
The assumption applied is that all undrained peatland within unlicensed HP and HPK areas will 
eventually be drained across the entire area. Based on this assumption, a projection of undrained 
peatland is carried out for both licensed and unlicensed HP and HPK areas. The purpose of this 
projection is to estimate the potential additional carbon loss that could occur if the peatlands in these 
areas are converted and drained. 
The emission factor for leakage to peatlands is determined as the average carbon loss from peat 
soils per hectare across all concessions owned by the class of deforestation agents at PDT, 
expressed in tonnes of CO₂.

In addition, the project’s NMPP implements extensive leakage mitigation activities, as detailed in 
Section 2.6.1, to minimize the potential for leakage. Actual leakage will be monitored throughout the 
project crediting period and will be reported at each monitoring event. 
3.2.3.2 Leakage for ARR-RWE project activities 
3.2.3.2.1 Activity-shifting leakage and market leakage 
The project meets all applicability conditions of the VM0033 methodology (Section 3.1.2) and 
implements leakage management activities as described in Section 2.6.1. In accordance with the 
applicability conditions, activity-shifting leakage and market leakage are not expected to occur. 
Therefore, both activity-shifting leakage and market leakage are conservatively assumed to be zero. 
3.2.3.2.2 Ecological leakage 
In line with VM0033, ecological leakage may be assumed not to occur in projects that meet the 
applicability conditions of the methodology, provided that project activities are designed to avoid 
significant alterations to hydrological connectivity with adjacent areas that could lead to increased 
GHG emissions outside the project boundary. The ARR-RWE project activity involves mangrove 
planting and is designed to ensure that existing hydrological conditions within the planting areas 
remain unaltered. Specifically, the project excludes planting in ponds or abandoned aquaculture 
areas, as such interventions would require the creation of tidal channels and partial drainage of 
ponded water. These actions could lower the water table and increase soil carbon oxidation, 
resulting in unintended GHG emissions. By restricting planting activities to suitable sites where 
hydrology is preserved, the project ensures that no ecological leakage occurs. This conclusion is 
consistent with the avoidance criteria outlined in Table 63, which detail processes that may arise 
outside the project area due to inappropriate project design and demonstrate how the project 
safeguards against them. 
 
268 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Table 63. Processes associated with ecological leakage outside project boundary, related criteria for 
their avoidance and project justification. 
Ecological leakage process 
outside project boundary 
Avoidance criterion and project justification 
Lowering water table that 
causes increased soil carbon 
oxidation 
Maintain wetland conditions (e.g., converting from impounded 
water to a wetland does not cause soil oxidation)  
 
Justification: The project does not alter the hydrological 
conditions of the project area or adjacent lands. The project 
avoids planting in ponds or abandoned aquaculture areas to 
prevent the need for tidal channel creation or drainage, which 
could otherwise lower the water table and increase soil carbon 
oxidation. 
Lowering water table that 
causes increased N2O 
emissions 
No conversion of non-seagrass wetland to open water  
 
Justification:  
The project does not involve the conversion of open water 
areas. There is no intervention planned in open water bodies, 
and no water drawdown or land conversion will take place that 
could increase N₂O emissions. 
Raising water table that causes 
increased CH4 emissions 
No conversion of non-wetland to wetland. 
 
Justification: 
There is no artificial water input or manipulation to raise the 
water table beyond natural tidal dynamics, and thus, no 
increased risk of CH₄ emissions due to waterlogging or wetland 
expansion. Where such a conversion occurs, it is due to sea 
level rise. 
Raising water table that causes 
decreased vegetation 
production that causes 
decreased new soil carbon 
sequestration 
No causation of vegetated to non-vegetated (or poorly 
vegetated) conditions  
 
Justification:  
The project does not induce any hydrological changes that 
would cause vegetated areas outside the project boundary to 
become non-vegetated or poorly vegetated. Mangrove planting 
activities are specifically designed to enhance vegetation cover 
within degraded intertidal areas, without disrupting the 
hydrology or vegetation of surrounding zones. The project does 
not obstruct or divert water movement that could negatively 
impact plant productivity beyond the project area. On the 
contrary, the presence of mangrove trees within the project 
area is expected to slow tidal flows and increase sediment 
deposition, thereby reducing sediment export to the open sea. 
This natural process supports rather than diminishes carbon 
sequestration potential outside the project area.

3.2.3.3 Leakage for ARR project activities  
In accordance with methodological requirements, leakage emissions for ARR project activities are 
estimated using the current version of VMD0054 for Estimating Leakage from ARR Activities, under 
the area-based approach. 
3.2.3.3.1 Step 1: Determine forgone production in project area 
The baseline commodity production in the project area must be demonstrated using historical 
production records for each commodity during the selected reference period. As the project area 
does not contain fuelwood production, only agricultural commodities are considered under this step.

Within the vicinity of the NMPP area, community livelihoods are diverse, with two dominant activities: 
fishing (extending from the sea into river systems and including seaweed farming) and community oil 
palm cultivation. The latter consists of two forms: (i) household-managed cultivation, where most 
270 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
households own between two and five hectares of land, and (ii) employment in company-owned oil 
palm plantations operating around the project area. These primary livelihood activities are described 
in detail in Section 4.1.1.1. 
However, the lands utilized by communities for household oil palm cultivation overlap with the BHK 
concession area that had been encroached upon prior to project implementation. These areas have 
been excluded from the project area and will instead be designated as community partnership areas. 
Consequently, no pre-project agricultural activities are present within the project area, and therefore 
no commodities are being displaced. As a result, the foregone production in the project area is equal 
to zero. 
3.2.3.3.2 Step 2: Determine the impact of leakage mitigation activities 
The project does not plan to implement activities outside the project area to reduce potential 
leakage; therefore, no leakage mitigation area is established. Instead, the NMPP implements 
extensive leakage mitigation measures within the project boundary, as detailed in Section 2.6.1, to 
minimize the risk of leakage.  
Given the absence of a designated leakage mitigation area, the value of leakage mitigation for 
commodity (LM
j,t
) is set to zero. At the same time, as described in Section 3.2.3.3.1, the foregone 
production in the project area (FP
j,t
) is also equal to zero. Taken together, this means that the amount 
of foregone production subject to leakage (I
j,t
) is zero under current project conditions. 
However, if any leakage mitigation activities are implemented during the project lifetime, those will be 
considered for the leakage calculation. The amount of foregone production that may results in 
leakage is calculated as follow:

land brought into production is zero.

Although no leakage is expected in the initial instance, leakage will be calculated if future project 
instances result in the displacement of agricultural activities. Where required, estimations of leakage 
emissions will follow the procedures outlined in the most recent version of the VMD0054 Module for 
Estimating Leakage from ARR Activities. This ensures that any potential leakage in subsequent 
project instances is accurately quantified and managed. All relevant equations and procedures will 
be applied to maintain transparency and reproducibility in the estimation process.

In the REDD+ MF methodology, the allowable level of uncertainty is set at 15% of NER_
REDD+
 at the 
95% confidence interval. If this level of precision is achieved, no deduction for uncertainty is 
required. If the uncertainty is greater than 15% of NER_
REDD+
 at the 95% confidence interval, a 
deduction must be applied equal to the amount by which the uncertainty exceeds the allowable limit. 
3.2.4.1.2.1 Uncertainty in REDD Baseline Estimates 
Step 1: Uncertainty in Projection of Baseline Rate of Deforestation or Degradation 
According to Module VMD0017 (X-UNC), when the baseline rate of deforestation is derived from 
measurements of proxy areas (as described in Module BL-PL), the uncertainty shall be calculated as 
the 95% confidence interval, expressed as a percentage of the mean area deforested in each proxy 
(D%
pn
), divided by the number of years over which deforestation occurred in each proxy (Yrs
pn
).

3.2.4.1.2.4 Uncertainty in WRC Project Scenario Estimates 
To assess the uncertainty in the WRC project scenario, the parameters Eproxy-CO2,i,t and 
Eproxy,CH4,i,t must be evaluated. The only sources of uncertainty are the emission factors. For 
each parameter i, the uncertainty is defined as the half-width of the 95% confidence interval (Table 
67). These values are derived from the 2006 IPCC Guidelines for National Greenhouse Gas 
Inventories. The total uncertainty for the WRC project scenario is calculated similarly to the WRC 
baseline scenario.

3.2.4.1.2.5 Total error in REDD+ project activity 
As the calculation of leakage is conservative in all cases, uncertainty related to leakage is not 
considered here. Total project uncertainty is therefore equal to the combined uncertainty from the 
baseline and project estimates for both REDD and WRC activities. The total annual uncertainty, 
NER_

, and the corresponding annual uncertainty deduction are presented in Table 68.

3.2.4.1.3 REDD-APWD Calculation of AFOLU pooled buffer account contribution 
Following VM0007, the number of credits to be held in the AFOLU pooled buffer account is 
determined as a percentage of the total carbon stock benefits by the REDD project activity. The total 
carbon stock benefits are calculated as the difference between net emissions in the baseline 
scenario and net emissions in the project scenario, with adjustments for fossil fuel combustion and 
direct N₂O emissions from fertilizer use. Consistent with the methodology, leakage emissions are not 
factored into the buffer calculations.

3.2.4.2.3 ARR-RWE Calculation of AFOLU pooled buffer account contribution 
The number of credits to be held in the AFOLU pooled buffer account is determined as a percentage 
of the total carbon stock benefits. The project has assessed the non-permanence risk in accordance 
with the VCS Non-Permanence Risk Tool. Based on the VCS Risk Report Calculation, the overall 
non-permanence risk for this project is determined to be 12%. Accordingly, the number of credits to 
be held annually in the AFOLU pooled buffer account is presented in Table 72.

are presented on Table 72.

VM0047 - ARR 
Data / parameter C
WP-woody-AB,t 
Data unit t C/ha 
Description Average carbon stock in aboveground woody biomass in the project 
scenario in year t 
Source of data Field measurement 
Description of 
measurement 
methods and 
procedures to be 
applied 
Aboveground woody biomass must be measured via plot-based 
sampling. Stratification may be employed to improve precision but 
is not required. Sample design need not be held constant across all 
monitoring and verification events. 
 Plot-based sampling approaches may be augmented using double 
or two phase sampling approaches (e.g., sampling probability 
proportional to prediction (3P) or ratio sampling). These 
approaches must include: 
1)  A complete census of an auxiliary variable (e.g., stocking index, 
see Appendix 1). 
2)  A sample of direct field-based measurements used to determine 
the relationship (i.e., a ratio or regression) between aboveground 
woody biomass and the auxiliary variable. 
All sample measurements must: 
a) Be demonstrated to be unbiased and derived from 
representative sampling. 
b) Ensure accuracy through adherence to best practices and 
QA/QC procedures (to be determined by the project proponent 
and outlined in standard operating procedures governing field 
data collection). 
c) Apply fixed size thresholds to independent variables used in 
biomass estimation (e.g., diameter at breast height, diameter 
at root collar, height), to be maintained through the crediting 
period. 
Aboveground woody biomass of each sampled woody plant (e.g., 
tree, shrub) is estimated using published allometric equations 
applied to one or more measured attributes. 
For project activities involving facilitated natural regeneration or 
with more than two species in a single stand, equation(s) must be 
chosen from the following as available, listed in descending order of 
preference: 
- Equations specific to the forest type within the same ecoregion 
(defined at the biome level following Olson et al. 2001) or 
328 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Holdridge (1967) life-zone as the region in which the project is 
located. 
- Global equations specific to the forest type 
Otherwise (e.g., in the case of monoculture plantations), 
equation(s) must be chosen from the following as available, listed in 
descending order of preference: 
- Equations specific to the species, genus, or family within the 
same ecoregion or Holdridge life-zone as the region in which 
the project is located. 
- Global equations specific to the species, genus, or family 
Where global allometric equations are used, equations must have 
been developed from or validated with datasets including direct 
measurements collected via destructive sampling from within the 
same ecoregion or Holdridge life-zone as that in which the project 
is located. 
Attributes (e.g., diameter at breast height, total height) incorporated 
as independent variables in allometric equations must be directly 
measured in the field applying established best practices, such as 
those found in: 
Kershaw Jr, J. A., M. J. Ducey, T. W. Beers, and B. Husch. 2016. 
Forest Mensuration. John Wiley & Sons. Avery, T. E., and H. E. 
Burkhart. 2015. Forest Measurements. Waveland Press. 
Measurement and sampling protocols must be detailed in standard 
operating procedures. Parameter tables for all attributes (e.g., 
diameter at breast height, total height) incorporated as independent 
variables in allometric equations must be included in the project 
description under “Data and Parameters Monitored.” 
Frequency of 
monitoring/recording 
At each monitoring period 
Value applied 
(not available in 
methodology) 
Estimates not possible without measurement  
Monitoring 
equipment 
(not available in 
methodology) 
N/A

                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
QA/QC procedures to 
be applied 
To be determined by the project proponent and outlined in standard 
operating procedures governing field data collection

Calculation method Calculated as the average of sample measurements

Data / parameter U
p,t 
Data unit % 
Description Percentage uncertainty (expressed as 90 percent confidence 
interval as a percentage of the mean) in carbon stock estimate of 
pool p in the project scenario in year t 
Source of data Calculation from sampled field measurements 
Description of 
measurement 
methods and 
procedures to be 
applied 
Uncertainty in pools derived from field measurements with 90 
percent confidence interval calculated as the standard error of the 
averaged plot measurement multiplied by the t value for the 90 
percent confidence level. Where double or two-phase sampling 
approaches are employed for aboveground woody biomass, 
parameter Up=woody,t is represented by error in the relationship 
(ratio or regression) between the auxiliary variable and woody 
biomass, referencing the 90 percent confidence interval of the ratio 
or 1.645 times the root mean squared error of the regression.  
Sample error in the auxiliary variable is not treated, because it must 
be subject to a complete census (see parameter table for 
CWP-woody-AB,t above). Where double or two-phase sampling 
approaches are employed for nonwoody biomass and/or litter (i.e., 
where subsampling is employed to estimate the dry-to-green weight 
ratio that is then applied to a sample estimate of green weight (see 
parameter tables for DMWP-herb,t and DMWPLI,t), parameters 
Up=non-woody,t and Up=litter,t are calculated by propagating 
sample error of the green weight estimate and sample error of the 
estimate of dry-to-green weight ratio

equipment

data on burnt peat areas must be validated by field observations

common geodetic system, for example using the UTM system 
using best-practice methods in remote sensing (see e.g., 
Congalton 1991; Congalton et al., 2008). Semi-automated image 
classification approaches may be applied. Strata must be 
validated by reference data collected in the field, other official 
documentation or from recent independent higher resolution 
remote sensing imagery.

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 
Climate impacts will be monitored as outlined in Appendix 9: Monitoring Plan. The monitoring plan 
outlines the indicators to be tracked, targets to be conducted, the frequency of monitoring, and the 
data collection methods. This climate monitoring plan covers peat water levels, subsidence rates, 
fire monitoring (such as burned areas, hotspots, and fire scar depth), and volumetric carbon and bulk 
density, in compliance with the VCS VM0007 methodology. For ARR and RWE project activities, 
each plantation site will be uniquely identified, and a comprehensive database will store all relevant 
information included: hectares restored in mineral, mangrove, and peat ecosystems; seedlings 
planted within each ecosystem; seedling survival rates and growth data; and evidence of planting to 
demonstrate increased canopy cover in the planting areas. The NMPP will employ both field data 
measurements and remote sensing techniques to monitor climate activities impacts. An overview 
description of the principal monitoring approaches is provided below. 
349 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
3.3.3.1 Remote Sensing 
The application of remote sensing technology, including satellite imagery and drone surveys, offers 
detailed insights into land dynamics, encompassing changes in vegetation, AGB stratification, forest 
fire detection, and peat drainability analysis. The overview below provides details of the processes 
and data sources employed for land cover classification and assessment. These processes are 
designed to analyze changes in land cover over a specific period, contributing to the calculation of 
carbon credits and greenhouse gas reduction potentials, and providing insights into patterns of 
urbanization, deforestation, agricultural expansion, and other land use changes that impact 
biodiversity and climate in the project area. 
Data Source 
The primary data sources for this land cover mapping stage are 8 and Landsat 9. These dataset 
provide 30 meters spatial resolution for land cover analysis over the decade. The overall process for 
land cover classification is illustrated in Figure 43. 
 
Figure 43. Land cover classification workflow. 
In addition to these satellite datasets, other data sources are used to calculate Verified Carbon Units 
(VCU) and Indonesian Greenhouse Gas Emissions Reduction Certification/Sertifikasi Penurunan 
Emisi Indonesia Gas Rumah Kaca (SPE-GRK) potential, as detailed in Table 74 below. 
Table 74. Other Dataset Used to Calculate the VCU/SPE-GRK Potential 
Type of Data Data Source Justification 
Peatland of 
Indonesia  
Center for Land 
Resources and 
Agricultural 
Development/Balai 
Besar Sumber Daya 
Lahan dan 
Pengembangan 
Pertanian (BBSLDP) 
Ministry of Agriculture 
(2011) 
This dataset is needed in order to know a project 
area’s peat extent and depth information. 
 
Depth information: 
1. D0: 0-50cm 
2. D1: 50-100cm 
3. D2: 100-200cm 
4. D3: 200-300cm 
5. D3: >300cm

                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Type of Data Data Source Justification 
It is noteworthy that the 2011 version 
corresponds to the data utilized in the FREL 
document submitted to the UNFCCC by the GoI. 
Moratorium 
Map/Peta Indikatif 
Penghentian 
Pemberian Izin 
Baru (PIPPIB) 
MoEF of Indonesia 
(PIPPIB 2023 Periode 
2). The map is 
periodically updated, 
and we are currently 
using  PIPPIB 2023 
Periode 2 from Peta 
Interaktif (menlhk.go.id). 
The PIPPIB data refers to an indicative map for 
the suspension of business licensing for forest 
area use approval or new forest area allocation 
approval in primary natural forest and peatlands. 
The PIPPIB area is protected by the MoEF from 
any kind of activity, including carbon projects. 
The PIPPIB aims to improve forest and peatland 
governance, reduce greenhouse gas emissions, 
and protect natural ecosystems. Therefore, this 
data is needed to identify which areas must be 
excluded from the calculation. 
GMW (Global 
Mangrove Watch) 
Global Forest Watch GMW is used as a reference for mangrove forest 
boundaries to prevent data over estimation. 
Land Cover Data - Landsat 8 
- Landsat 9 
- LC class sample 
collection 
Land cover data is the primary dataset that is 
used for: 
- Creating forest benchmark 
- Estimate carbon stocks for each carbon pool, 
considering their associated areas 
 
Landsat is selected amongst the other satellite 
imagery due to these reasons: 
- Commonly used by any other organizations 
such as MoEF and FAO. 
- Optimal resolution (30m) and spectral bands 
(6 bands) 
- Long-term monitoring: consistently recorded 
since the late 1980s up to recent days allow 
us to track land cover changes over decades 
Methodology 
The methodology involves preprocessing, sample data collection, classification, and data validation, 
all conducted within Google Earth Engine environment. This approach parallels the AGB 
stratification method described in Appendix 12. 
Classification Scheme 
A land cover classification system has been developed that aligns with the MoEF’s standard as well 
as the definitions commonly used by countries in Southeast Asia (SEA), while accommodating the 
capabilities of remote sensing detection capability. 
351 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
The classes included in our classification system are selected based on their significant area and 
their role as drivers of deforestation. Note that not all classes are present in Indonesia, only those 
that correspond to the MoEF’s classification system are included. Table 75 shows the land cover 
classifications we use and their definitions. 
Table 75. Land cover classification (SEA Version 4). 
Class ID Class Name Definition 
41101 Dryland forest (high density) 
Dense natural tropical forest vegetation growing on 
well-drained, non-flooded land. 
41102 Dryland forest (medium density) 
Moderately dense natural tropical forest vegetation 
growing on well-drained, non-flooded land. 
41501 Swamp forest (high density) Dense forest in swampy, waterlogged areas. 
41502 Swamp forest (medium density) 
Moderately dense forest in swampy, waterlogged 
areas. 
41601 Mangrove (high density) Dense mangrove forest in coastal areas. 
41602 Mangrove (medium density) Moderately dense mangrove forest in coastal areas. 
42101 Woodshrubs - old 
Areas are dominated by woody shrubs > 7 to 12 m 
tall 
42102 Woodshrubs - young Areas are dominated by woody shrubs > 3 to 6 m tall 
42200 Rubber Plantations of rubber trees. 
42300 Timber Plantation Plantations of timber for industrial use 
43110 Oil Palm Plantations of oil palm trees. 
43200 Annual crop 
Fields with annually cultivated crops, eg. paddy field, 
sugarcane  
42400 Tree crop 
Plantations of Woody Fruit or Multipurpose Tree 
Species (MPTS) 
44000 Grassland & shrubs Areas dominated by grasses and shrubs < 3 m tall 
45100 Built-up area 
Urban or developed areas with buildings and 
infrastructure. 
45200 Bare land and rock Areas with exposed soil, rock, or minimal vegetation. 
46100 Water bodies Lakes, rivers, and other natural water bodies. 
46200 Ponds Man-made ponds in coastal area 
46300 Ice Areas covered by perineal ice or snow. 
Each type of land cover has a distinct carbon stock value, with forested or vegetated areas 
possessing higher values compared to non-vegetated areas. Therefore, the estimation of carbon 
values is derived from secondary data obtained from reputable national or international entities and 
scientific journals. The carbon stock and Forest Reference Emission Level (FREL) data are sourced 
from the following references :  
1. Indonesia Forest Reference Emission Level submitted to UNFCCC (United Nations 
Framework Convention on Climate Change),

2. Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO), 
3. Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), 
4. The Center for International Forestry Research and World Agroforestry (CIFOR-ICRAF), and 
5. Scientific Journals. 
 
To effectively support data on land cover changes, it is also crucial to monitor fire incidents. This will 
be accomplished through the Fire Information for Resource Management System (FIRMS). 
Developed by NASA, FIRMS utilizes Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer (MODIS) fire 
data to deliver near real-time updates on fire activities within the project area. The system is 
designed to alert teams within 24 hours of detecting a fire, thereby enabling timely responses and 
informed resource management. Such monitoring is essential for understanding the impact of fires 
on land cover dynamics and for implementing effective fire management strategies. 
3.3.3.2 Field peat monitoring 
To ensure robust and continuous peatland hydrological monitoring, the project will install 130 
dipwells in combination with subsidence poles across the project area. These installations form the 
core of the monitoring system used to track peat water table dynamics and peat surface subsidence 
over time, both of which are critical parameters for understanding peat stability and associated GHG 
emissions. Monitoring points are strategically distributed based on the delineated peat-depth strata, 
with a minimum density of 10 dipwells per stratum, in alignment with the requirements of VMD0046 
(M-PEAT). The placement of each monitoring point is informed by key physical characteristics, 
including peat thickness (prioritizing areas with peat depths greater than 2 m), proximity to drainage 
canals, and field accessibility. A detailed map of the peat monitoring points is provided in Figure 44. 
The number and placement of these monitoring stations can be adjusted if necessary to suit the field 
conditions present, ensuring that the monitoring efforts are thorough and adaptable. Peat water 
levels will be systematically measured through weekly measurements and peat subsidence will be 
checked manually twice a year, once during the dry season and once during the rainy season. This 
biannual schedule is employed because it captures seasonal variations that can significantly affect 
subsidence rates, providing a more comprehensive understanding of peatland dynamics. 
353 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 44. Peat monitoring distribution map 
Subsequent analysis of this data offers a deeper understanding of how these factors influence GHG 
emissions especially within the project area. Variations in water levels within peat soils significantly 
affect the decomposition rate, with lower water levels typically resulting in increased decomposition 
and, consequently, higher GHG emissions. Similarly, data on peat subsidence offer insights into the 
long-term stability of peatlands and their capability to store carbon. Combined with other collected 
data, the project is able to make precise estimates regarding GHG emissions arising from peat 
decomposition in tropical peatlands. The insights gained from these monitoring efforts will be crucial 
in determining how well the project contributes to peatland conservation and carbon storage. 
Lastly it should be noted that one canal has been identified inside the project area, but it was 
concluded that it will not negatively affect the project (for justification, see appendix 14). However, 
during the monitoring period it will be closely and routinely monitored, as detailed above, and it will 
be assessed if further action is needed in the project’s 1st MRV report.  
3.3.3.3 Other field measurements  
 
Monitoring of AGB C stock  
To assess carbon stock within the project area, permanent vegetation plots for AGB measurement 
will be established in the BHK I and BHK II. These plots will undergo annual monitoring to measure 
354 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
standing tree growth, specifically the diameter at breast height (DBH) and plant-species. The same 
allometric equations used during the baseline assessment will be employed to estimate carbon stock 
from DBH measurements, with these equations being sourced from scientific journals. 
 
For activities related to ARR and RWE, other permanent plots will be established and selected to 
represent areas targeted for ARR and RWE, including Assisted Natural Regeneration (ANR) and 
intensive planting activities for mangrove, mineral, and peatland ecosystems. In these permanent 
plots, survivability and DBH will remain to be the primary field measurement parameter. Afterward, 
allometric equations will be used to estimate woody biomass (see section 3.2.2.2.3.3), in accordance 
with the species being planted. These activities will be conducted annually. Additionally, an internal 
report will be maintained to document various aspects of the active planting activities, including the 
number of seedlings planted categorized by species, their survival rates, as well as DBH, height 
data, and the disturbances (e.g. pests and diseases, etc).  
 
Permanent plots will be established within each stratum. Locations for the plots will be randomly 
selected using GIS. The sampling method and number of plots will follow the requirements specified 
in VM0007 (VMD0001), VM0033, and VM0047. To ensure that plots are completely situated within 
the designated areas, a 10-meter spatial buffer will be established around the perimeter of each plot. 
During ground checking, the central coordinates of each plot will be captured using a GPS device. 
Additionally, the administrative location, stratum and stand, and series number of each plot will be 
recorded and archived. Permanent signs, such as buried wood, bamboo, or steel bars, will be 
established to mark the plots in the field. 
 
Monitoring of soil carbon stocks 
 
The RWE project activity includes the collection of soil samples which is performed annually at 
designated planting permanent plots within each stratum. In these plots, a soil core is extracted from 
a sampling point using a specialized auger designed to minimize compaction and maintain sample 
integrity. These sampling points are strategically established alongside plantation plots using GIS. 
The collected soil samples are crucial for determining the percentage of organic carbon, expressed 
as a percentage or fraction of organic matter (%OM) relative to the total dry weight. This is 
determined using the loss on ignition (LOI) method, where the dry soil samples are combusted at 
high temperatures to measure carbon content gravimetrically. 
For the measurement of bulk density, soil samples are collected using a ring cylinder with a fixed 
volume. At each sampling point, three replicates are taken using the ring sampler. The samples are 
then oven-dried until they reach a constant weight; after cooling, they are weighed to determine their 
dry weight. Bulk density is calculated by dividing this dry weight by the fixed volume of the soil 
sample collected with the ring cylinder. This bulk density value is crucial for determining soil carbon 
stock, as it aids in converting the organic carbon content to total carbon per unit area. 
 
355 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
To analyze these soil parameters, the collected samples will be transported to a soil laboratory for 
detailed examination. These parameters play an essential role in evaluating the soil's carbon storage 
capacity and understanding its ecological impact. The data will be essential for calculating CO2 
emissions from the soil, utilizing the formula provided in Section 3.2.2.2.4.1, which supports accurate 
assessments of soil carbon dynamics. 
3.3.3.4 Procedures for internal auditing and QA/QC 
To ensure accuracy, transparency, and traceability of all project data, a structured data quality 
assurance and quality control (QA/QC) and internal audit process will be implemented across all 
stages of data collection, management, and analysis. 
1. Field Data Collection.  
All field data will be collected by trained staff using standardized tally sheets or digital 
devices (e.g. Smartphones) in accordance with the relevant SOPs. Each field team is 
supervised by the designated supervisor/manager who will conduct a review of field data 
prior to submission ensuring: the consistency with sampling design and GPS coordinates, 
completeness of variable measurements, presence of supporting evidence (photos, notes, 
metadata). The data will then be uploaded into the project’s online data management 
system, together with metadata and field photographs. 
2. Data Analysis and QA/QC.  
This is conducted by the GIS & RS Supervisor, Forest Restoration and Conservation 
Manager and/or Forest Manager as deemed appropriate. 
● Field data may undergo a second round of verification to ensure methodological 
consistency and accuracy in data entry. 
● Remote sensing data (for land cover changes monitoring, deforestation monitoring, 
fire scar analysis, etc.) will be analyzed by the GIS-RS team and cross-checked 
against field data. 
● Results of both analyses are uploaded into the online database. 
 
3. Results Compilation and Emissions Calculation.  
The verified datasets will be compiled for calculation of emissions and removals following the 
applicable VCS methodologies (VM0007, VM0033, VM0047). A final QA/QC review will be 
conducted by the General Manager prior to finalization. This includes confirming data 
integrity, adherence to calculation procedures, and documentation completeness. 
4. Internal Auditing and Data Archiving.  
Periodic internal audits will be conducted by the project’s technical oversight team to verify 
that all QA/QC and data management procedures are followed. All data, analysis outputs, 
356

transparency and data security. 
3.3.3.5 Organizational structure of monitoring activities 
The organizational structure for monitoring activities is designed to ensure effective oversight and 
execution of the project’s objectives. Key competencies outlined in sections 2.4.1 to 2.4.4. This

3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (VCS, 3.18; CCB, CL4.2) 
All stakeholders will have access to project document summaries, which include monitoring plans 
and results. This information will be available through regular coordination, printed materials at 
project and community office boards, and online resources via the Verra website. For local 
communities, information will be directly communicated during village/community meetings and 
displayed on community boards. Continuous communication is planned to ensure active community 
involvement in project activities and monitoring. We will conduct stakeholder meetings to share 
important project information (e.g. project document dissemination), and gather their 
input/responses. All NMPP document summaries will be provided in English and Bahasa Indonesia, 
therefore providing local communities and stakeholders the opportunity to offer feedback. 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits 
 
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
North Kalimantan, including Nunukan Regency, is projected to experience substantial warming and 
more variable rainfall this century especially under high-emissions pathways (SSP5-8.5/RCP8.5). 
Country-scale projections synthesized by the World Bank’s Climate Change Knowledge Portal 
indicate Indonesia’s annual mean temperature could rise by around ≈3.4 °C by the 2080–2099 
period under RCP8.5 (slightly below the global ≈3.7 °C), with accompanying increases in the 
intensity of heavy rainfall events
30
. For precipitation, multi-model assessments for Southeast Asia 
project increases in monsoon-season rainfall and greater interannual variability (medium 
confidence), implying more frequent wet-season deluges and dry-season deficits. Regionally on 
Borneo, downscaling studies show notable increases in late-century rainfall in parts of Kalimantan 
(e.g., East Kalimantan +12% to +50% by 2070–2099 in some models), while drought risk remains 
episodically high—consistent with strong El Niño sensitivity across the Indonesian Maritime 
Continent

. Along the coast, relative sea level rise of roughly 0.7–0.8 m by 2081–2100 (SSP5-8.5,

 In a “baseline” land-use pathway, large-scale conversion

landscape of hydrological buffers that modulate drought, fire, surge, and saline intrusion under 
climate stress. Conversely, the project’s interventions (maintaining high peat water tables, restoring 
and protecting mangrove belts, and strengthening community stewardship/MRV) directly counter 
these climate-amplified hazards reducing fire susceptibility during prolonged dry periods, lowering 
coastal risk, and improving both community resilience and biodiversity conservation status under 
warming and sea-level rise. 
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
North Kalimantan (Nunukan) is expected to face rising average temperatures and increasingly 
variable rainfall this century, with higher likelihood of hydrologic extremes (droughts, floods) and 
sea-level rise. In a without-project pathway, conversion and drainage of peatlands and degradation 
of mangroves would strip the landscape of its natural buffering capacity, raising fire risk during dry 
spells and amplifying coastal hazards, whereas the project’s hydrological protection of peat domes 
and maintenance/restoration of mangrove belts directly counter those risks.  
Community well-being 
Pathways of impact (without-project) 
● Livelihood volatility and food security: Degradation of mangroves and peat-swamp 
forests undermines fisheries nurseries, forest products, and soil/water regulation that local 
households rely upon. Pressure from outside investors (illegal logging, unregulated pond 
clearing, wildlife poaching) is expected to intensify absent effective monitoring/enforcement, 
eroding ecosystem services and long-term well-being. 
● Disaster and health risks: Peat drainage elevates fire susceptibility in droughts; mangrove 
loss reduces protection from storm surge and saline intrusion, affecting drinking water 
access and public health, particularly in larger population centers such as Nunukan Barat 
(18,240 people) and in smaller, resource-dependent villages like Pembeliangan and 
Semunad.

With-project moderating effects 
● Ecosystem-based adaptation benefits: By keeping peat water tables high and 
restoring/guarding mangrove fringes, the project reduces the severity of fires and coastal 
hazards under climate stressors. 
● Livelihood resilience: Restored mangroves are expected to improve fisheries nursery 
function and catch stability for fishers/seaweed farmers; project employment and alternative 
livelihood programs diversify income and reduce reliance on harmful, climate-sensitive 
activities.  
Biodiversity conservation status 
Climate-sensitive threats (without-project) 
● Habitat loss & fragmentation under extreme events: Illegal logging, expansion to oil 
palm, and unauthorized aquaculture already fragment habitats; climate stress (drought/flood) 
compounds these pressures by increasing peat oxidation and fire risk, degrading key 
ecosystems that support RTE species. 
● Species at risk: The landscape supports globally threatened fauna e.g., Proboscis monkey 
(Nasalis larvatus), Storm’s stork (Ciconia stormi), Lesser adjutant, and aquatic megafauna, 
whose persistence depends on intact peat-swamp and mangrove mosaics that are 
vulnerable to climate-driven disturbance. 
With-project moderating effects 
● Hydrological recovery and habitat integrity: Rewetting peat and restoring mangroves 
strengthen ecosystem function and reduce climate-amplified risks (fire, subsidence, coastal 
erosion), improving conditions for native flora (e.g., Rhizophora, Avicennia, Bruguiera) and 
associated fauna. 
● Threat abatement & stewardship: Patrolling, law-enforcement support, and community 
awareness reduce unsustainable access and poaching critical during and after climate 
extremes when wildlife is most vulnerable, while ongoing monitoring tracks species presence 
and threat levels. 
In the absence of the project, anticipated climatic changes (hotter, more variable, and 
sea-level-affected conditions) would interact with land-use pressures to degrade peat and mangrove 
systems, heightening fire and flood/coastal risks, diminishing fisheries and forest-based livelihoods, 
and accelerating biodiversity decline. By maintaining peat hydrology, restoring mangrove structure, 
and strengthening community stewardship and enforcement, the project provides ecosystem-based 
adaptation benefits that bolster community well-being and improve the conservation status of 
threatened species under a changing climate.

3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
This section operationalizes the project’s Theory of Change for climate adaptation. It links priority 
climate risks in the project zone (hotter and drier dry seasons, more intense rainfall events, coastal 
surge and saline intrusion, and fire susceptibility on drained peat) to concrete measures that 
strengthen both community well-being and biodiversity resilience. The table that follows summarizes 
each adaptation objective, the climate risk pathway it addresses, the designed interventions, 
responsible partners, and measurable indicators for adaptive management. Together, these actions 
maintain hydrological buffers (peat and mangroves), diversify and stabilize climate-sensitive 
livelihoods, reduce illegal and destructive pressures that intensify under climate stress, and 
institutionalize monitoring and feedback so that strategies can be iteratively improved over the 
project lifetime.

4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
The administrative locations of the villages (Figure 8) surrounding the project area consist of three 
villages and 1 hamlet: Nunukan Barat, Pembeliangan, Semunad, and Sebakis; which are distributed 
across three subdistricts in Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan Province. Table 77 below outlines 
the boundaries of the villages in the project area.

4.1.1.1. Well-being Information 
Well-being can be inferred from information such as the availability of public facilities in the villages 
and the communities’ livelihoods. 
Community’s Perception on Well-being Changes from the Past 
The indigenous Dayak people of Nunukan Regency traditionally lived in close dependence on 
nature, meeting their daily needs through river and sea fishing as well as small-scale shifting 
cultivation of rice, fruits, and other crops. Farming was usually practiced along riverbanks for easy 
access by boat, which was once the only means of transportation. Cultivated plots were used for one 
to three planting cycles before being left fallow or converted into fruit orchards, timber stands, or 
other perennial crops. Under customary law, land ownership was tied to the person who first cleared 
it, which explains why indigenous households often controlled large areas of land, sometimes 
reaching tens of hectares. Over time, however, the arrival of transmigration programs and plantation 
companies, particularly oil palm, reduced available land and encouraged a shift from traditional 
shifting cultivation to rotational farming systems. Farmers now tend to cultivate a plot two or three 
times before moving to a new or previously fallowed area, and many increasingly plant oil palm or 
363 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
other perennial crops, which are seen as more profitable and easier to manage. Rising land prices, 
driven by scarcity, have turned land into a form of long-term investment, accessible mainly through 
purchase or inheritance.  
Today, land remains central to livelihoods not only for farming and agroforestry but also for livestock, 
aquaculture, housing, social and religious infrastructure, and even traditional medicine. While 
companies have contributed to land scarcity and acquisition conflicts, residents acknowledge that 
they have also brought economic opportunities by making land use more productive and 
market-oriented. Land price has also soared dramatically, starting from 1 million Rupiah/hectare in 
2000 when sale of land started and reaching 15 million Rupiah/hectare in 2024. In addition, 
infrastructure and public facilities availability and quality have improved significantly especially in the 
last two decades. Road networks were almost absent, and based on what the locals reported, other 
facilities such as schools, electricity grid, and healthcare centres were very limited back then 
compared to now. 
Education Facilities 
Based on FGDs, interviews and field observations, the availability of education facilities in the 
studied villages varies considerably. Overall (Table 78), the region is provided with 4 TK (Taman 
Kanak-kanak/Kindergartens), 8 PAUD (Pendidikan Anak Usia Dini/Early Childhood Education 
Centers), 10 SD (Sekolah Dasar/Primary School), 1 MI (Madrasah Ibtidaiyah/Islamic Primary 
School), 6 SMP (Sekolah Menengah Pertama/Junior High School), 1 MTs (Madrasah 
Tsanawiyah/Islamic Junior High School) 2 SMA (Sekolah Menengah Atas/Senior High School), 1 MA 
(Madrasah Aliyah/Islamic High School), and 1 SMK (Sekolah Menengah Kejuruan/Vocational High 
School). Although most villages have adequate education facilities, their distribution is still uneven. 
For example, Semunad Village and Sebakis only has education facilities up to primary school level, 
so students have to go to the district capital of Tulin Onsoi or Sebuku to get higher education. On the 
other hand, Pembeliangan village and Nunukan Barat have more complete facilities, including senior 
high schools or equivalent. This gap indicates a challenge in education accessibility, especially in 
villages with limited facilities, which could have an impact on equitable human resource development 
in the region. 
Additionally, some students from these areas rely on river transportation along the Sebuku River to 
reach Nunukan Barat, where higher education facilities are more accessible. River transportation 
adds another layer of complexity, as it requires significant time and resources, further emphasizing 
the need for improved educational infrastructure in remote areas.

Health Facilities 
The healthcare facilities available in the villages surrounding the project area include 1 hospital, 1 
community health center (Puskesmas), 2 pharmacies, 5 community health center branch (Pustu), 
and 23 integrated health service posts (Posyandu) (see Table 79). The medical personnel consist of 
6 nurses, 7 village midwives, 4 pharmacists, 120 Posyandu cadres, and 3 trained traditional 
midwives (dukun bersalin). Among these, Pembeliangan and Nunukan Barat have the most 
comprehensive healthcare facilities. While the majority of the community relies on modern medical 
services, a small portion still uses traditional remedies, primarily for specific types of illnesses. 
Nevertheless, the primary referral point for healthcare remains the nearest modern medical facility, 
ensuring accessibility for more critical needs. This blend of modern and traditional healthcare reflects 
the community's adaptive approach to health services in the area.

Infrastructure 
Rural infrastructure is defined as physical infrastructure that provides access to basic services as 
well as social and economic services for rural communities (Asnudin A, 2005-- Andi Asnudin, 
2005,”Manajemen Proyek Konstruksi”, UNTAD Press-Palu). 
The condition of infrastructure in the villages surrounding the project area varied. Some were in 
relatively good condition, while others were not. Village roads consisted of asphalt, concrete, and 
hardened dirt roads. In addition to land routes, river/sea transportation was still operational, 
especially for connecting Nunukan Barat with other villages around the project area, particularly 
Pembeliangan and Semunad; as well as Sebakis, which is a hamlet under the administration of 
Pembeliangan. Nunukan Barat is separated from the other study villages by being on a different 
island, making water transportation (river/sea) the most feasible option for inter-village access. 
Electricity access in most areas was provided by PLN (Perusahaan Listrik Negara/State Electricity 
Company), as evidenced by the power poles lined along the roads. The arrival of PLN has also 
supported the development of communication infrastructure, particularly mobile phones and service 
providers, making it easier for the study villages to access information from outside. In Sebakis 
however, based on an interview with Mr. Mursalim (a resident of RT 05, Sebakis) conducted on May 
1, 2025, electricity supply in the area is very limited. Most residents rely on electricity provided by 
nearby companies such as PT SIL/SIP, which is only available during certain hours of the day. 
Traditional farming plots still exist and are managed by village residents. The types of agriculture 
practiced include food crops (upland rice) and perennial crops on dry land (such as oil palm, fruits, 
etc.). Access to farming areas is relatively easy via both land and water routes. Paddy (wet rice) 
farming is not yet widely developed. The limited presence of paddy fields results in a near absence 
of infrastructure supporting local food production, such as irrigation, at the time of the study. Most 
local farming relies on rain-fed agriculture. 
Clothing 
Approximately 100% of clothing requirements are fulfilled through acquisitions from Malaysia and 
Java. There are no materials currently being sourced directly from nature. Besides the fact that 
natural materials have become difficult to obtain, buying is seen as much easier and more practical 
due to improved access to goods and services in each village. 
Shelter 
Most residents in each village obtain building materials and household necessities by purchasing 
them from the nearest markets. The number of residents collecting timber from forests is now very 
366 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
limited. Those who do harvest forest timber are typically outsiders who come to the area specifically 
to collect wood for resale, even as far as Tarakan. Residents of the villages prefer to purchase 
materials due to ease, practicality, accessibility to goods and services, and the busy nature of their 
work schedules. 
Food and Clean Water 
Food needs are primarily met through purchases, including carbohydrates, vegetables, fruits, and 
protein sources. The shift in land use from agriculture to plantations and the presence of companies 
has led many residents to change occupations, from farmers to company employees. As a result, 
surrounding lands are no longer maintained and have been converted into plantations (especially oil 
palm), which are seen as easier to manage than food crop farming. Consequently, the supply of 
vegetables, fruits, and carbohydrates is largely fulfilled through market purchases. 
For protein, many residents work as fishers (in rivers and at sea) or raise livestock. The ease of 
access to transportation, markets, communication, and other services has made it simpler for 
residents to obtain essential goods and services. 
To meet the basic needs for clean water and sanitation (bathing, washing, toilet facilities), the 
villages around the project area are already served by PDAM (Perusahaan Daerah Air Minum/local 
water utility). Other sources of clean water include wells, mainly bore wells; rainwater harvesting; 
rivers (especially for residents of Semunad and Sebakis); and bottled/refilled water. 
Spiritual Connections to Land 
Traditional customs still play an important role in some villages, particularly in dispute resolution and 
ceremonies such as marriages and funerals. However, there was little mention of their spiritual 
connection to the land. A customary leader in Pembeliangan stated that there is a sacred forest 
within the BHK II area, but the community has never conducted a survey to properly delineate its 
boundaries. Furthermore, no activities have been carried out around the site by the locals, not even 
those related to traditional rituals or customs. 
Natural Resources Dependency 
Local communities, particularly the indigenous, remain highly dependent on forest resources and 
other natural ecosystems within the project area. These areas serve multiple purposes: smallholder 
gardens provide cash income; forests supply wild game; rivers are vital sources of fish for both 
protein and household income; and forests also provide timber for house construction, furniture, 
work tools, and firewood, as well as other materials that can be sold for cash. However, some of 
these practices are no longer sustainable, such as land clearing through burning, hunting or 
capturing protected wildlife, and illegal logging. The degree of community dependence on forest 
resources is summarized in Table 81 below:

4.1.1.2. Community Characteristics 
Ethnic Group 
The ethnic groups in these villages are categorized into two main groups: indigenous ethnic groups 
and migrant ethnic groups. The indigenous groups in the area are the Dayak Tidung (primarily 
residents of Nunukan Barat and Pembeliangan) and the Dayak Agabag (mainly in Semunad). 
Meanwhile, the migrant ethnic groups originate from other islands such Javanese, Sundanese, 
Bugis, Batak, Flores, Malay, Chinese (Tionghoa), Toraja, and others, though their proportions are 
relatively small.
