Community Institutions 
Community institutions are organizations or groups that have established rules and mechanisms to 
resolve the issues they face. They grow and evolve within society. Generally, there are two types of 
institutions: formal and informal. 
Formal institutions develop based on specific legal-formal regulations that are broadly applied across 
regions. The village government institutions are the most important formal institutions in each village 
and are responsible for managing and implementing governance systems for the local community. 
These village institutions are supported by several other organizations that help guide the 
community, such as youth organizations (Karang Taruna), Community Empowerment Institutions 
(Lembaga Pemberdayaan Masyarakat/LPM), Family Welfare Empowerment groups (PKK), 
Integrated Health Service Posts (Posyandu), and others. 
Informal institutions emerge and grow from community initiatives in response to the challenges they 
face. These informal institutions can receive official recognition from the government depending on 
the needs and local context. Active informal institutions include traditional (customary) institutions 
and religious organizations, such as mosque councils (Dewan Kemakmuran Masjid/DKM), prayer 
groups (kelompok pengajian), and others. Customary institutions are led directly by traditional 
leaders (pemangku adat) and supported by customary officials. 
369 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
In addressing community issues, final decision-making authority lies with the village head. Other 
institutions, particularly customary institutions, generally handle issues within their specific domains 
or address social matters according to customary law, such as land disputes, land governance, and 
other social issues. For social problems, customary leaders and the village head usually work 
together to resolve matters at the village level. If the issue cannot be resolved within the village’s 
customary system, it is typically escalated to the district (kecamatan) or even regency (kabupaten) 
level. 
In religious matters, customary institutions often collaborate with religious institutions, as their 
activities tend to align. Traditional ceremonies are always harmonized with the religious beliefs of the 
community, such as in marriage, death, birth, communal feasts (selamatan), and other events. 
Economic Diversity 
Subsistence systems in the villages surrounding the project area are closely linked to the availability 
of local resources, though livelihood strategies are not determined by natural potential alone. Access 
to markets for selling products is also a major factor, and many households pursue multiple income 
sources. Farming is generally a combination of shifting cultivation and perennial crops such as fruits, 
oil palm, and timber species. Seasonal crops like rice, tubers, and vegetables are primarily 
consumed within the household, while oil palm and fruits are cultivated mainly for sale. These 
practices reflect the community’s strong cultural and economic ties to forests and land, which 
continue to serve both subsistence and commercial functions. 
In Kelurahan Nunukan Barat, livelihood options are relatively diverse due to its location within the 
regency’s capital. Nevertheless, fishing is the dominant occupation, with approximately 70% of 
residents engaged in capture fisheries or aquaculture, including seaweed farming. Fishing activities 
extend beyond the sea to river systems such as the Sebaung, where some residents also capture 
softshell turtles for consumption and trade in Tarakan. Although limited rice farming is present, most 
staple food requirements, particularly rice, are met through purchases in local markets, 
complemented by tubers and government food assistance. Other sources of income include petty 
trade, civil service and contract work, wage labor, swiftlet nest farming, and service-related 
businesses. 
In Desa Pembeliangan, oil palm smallholdings and fishing constitute the main sources of livelihood, 
supplemented by swiftlet nest enterprises, transportation services, petty trade, subsistence livestock, 
company employment, and casual labor. Fishing boats are typically either self-constructed or 
purchased, with prices ranging from IDR 1 million to 7 million depending on size and quality. Timber 
for boat construction is obtained from personal gardens, local suppliers, or in some cases from 
within the project area. Non-timber forest products, particularly wild honey, also play a role in 
household economies, though collection is seasonal and not considered a primary livelihood. Honey 
is harvested from tree species such as lita, mangris, and bengkirai, and is consumed domestically or 
sold at around IDR 150,000 per 600 ml bottle. 
In Desa Semunad, livelihoods are heavily concentrated in oil palm cultivation, with most households 
owning between two and five hectares of land. Many residents are also employed by oil palm 
370 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
companies operating in the area. Fishing is practiced by approximately 30 individuals, primarily in 
rivers such as Sapiul, Tulin, and Sebuluh, while around 20 people continue to hunt in nearby forests. 
Wet-rice agriculture is absent, with limited upland rice farming still practiced, though oil palm remains 
the preferred and more profitable livelihood option. 
Across all surveyed villages, the biggest share of the household income bracket falls under the IDR 
1-3 million per month, ranging from 33.3% in Semunad to  50% in Nunukan Barat. Sebakis is the 
exception here, with the majority (62.5%) earns more than IDR 5 million per month. The rest of the 
population makes between IDR 3 million and IDR 5 million per month. There is a tiny minority 
making less than IDR 1 million per month, 10% of the surveyed respondents in Nunukan Barat, 9,4% 
in Pembeliangan, and 7.4% in Semunad. Please note that these figures are generated from small 
samples and more rigorous survey is required in the future. 
Communities’ expenditures are dominated by education, transportation (particularly fuel), and 
consumer goods that cannot be sourced directly from nature. These include clothing, toiletries, 
instant foods (such as coffee, sugar, and instant noodles), modern medicines, and other daily 
necessities. At the same time, households still rely on natural resources for part of their 
consumption, drawing from forest products, farmland, and gardens. Examples include herbal 
medicines, timber for construction, tubers, upland rice, and other subsistence products. Even 
households with salaried or wage employment often supplement their needs directly from 
surrounding ecosystems. 
4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
CM1.1) 
The villages surrounding PT. BHK is divided into several neighborhood units (RT). There is no further 
division based on hamlets (dusun) or community units (RW) in the study area. The total number of 
RTs in the studied villages reaches 44, with the highest number found in Nunukan Barat (30 RTs) 
and the lowest in Semunad (only 4 RTs). The detailed number of RTs in each village included in the 
SIA study area is presented in the Table 83 below. 
Table 83. List of RT in the villages within the Project Area 
# District Village RT 
1. Nunukan Barat Nunukan Barat 30 
2. Sebuku Pembeliangan 10 
3. Tulin Onsoi Semunad 4 
Total 44 
Source: Kecamatan Nunukan dalam Angka 2023, Kecamatan Sebuku dalam Angka 2023, 
Kecamatan Tulin Onsoi dalam Angka 2023 and 2024 primary data of Fairatmos team 
The locations of each village are quite far apart, resulting in very limited interaction between villages. 
Nunukan Barat is even separated by an island from the villages of Pembeliangan, and Semunad. 
Although Semunad and Pembeliangan are located on the same island, the distances between them 
are still considerable. Furthermore, ongoing issues related to village boundaries have contributed to 
371 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
the weak relationships between the villages, especially between Pembeliangan and its neighboring 
villages. 
Meanwhile, the relationships among smaller community groups at the RT (neighborhood unit) level 
are generally close within each village. As smaller sub-communities within a village, RT-level groups 
are subject to and comply with the regulations of the village government above them. The main 
institution that coordinates RT-level governance is the village government, led directly by the village 
head. Other institutions, except for the Village Consultative Body (BPD), fall under the authority of 
the village head, including traditional (adat) institutions. However, in practice, these institutions work 
side by side and interact complementarily in resolving community issues, especially those related to 
customary matters. 
In terms of religions, the Dayak Tidung and other migrant ethnic groups such as Javanese, Bugis, 
Sundanese, and Malays are mostly Muslim, whereas the Dayak Agabag community predominantly 
adheres to Christianity, both Catholic and Protestant. Despite their different religious beliefs, these 
ethnic groups live side by side in each village. The religions practiced by the communities also 
influence their cultures. Each village has distinct cultural practices that incorporate elements of 
Islamic and Christian traditions, particularly in ceremonies related to birth, death, marriage, 
communal feasts, and so on. Nevertheless, people generally live in harmony, showing mutual 
respect across religious differences. Traditional rituals are also adapted to the ethnicity and religion 
of each community. According to local residents, since the majority now follow Islam and a portion 
follow Christianity (both Protestant and Catholic), traditional rituals or offerings to sacred places or 
objects are now rarely performed. The growing influence of religion has led to the gradual 
abandonment of practices considered to conflict with religious teachings. 
Nonetheless, there is still an ongoing issue of unresolved village boundaries to this day. This is 
caused by several factors; for example, official map sources from the government (such as BIG 
[Badan Informasi Geospasial/Geospatial Information Agency] maps, Podes [Potensi Desa/Village 
Potentials] maps, RBI maps [Rupa Bumi Indonesia/Indonesian Topographic Basemap], and others) 
do not provide consistent data or representations, which hinders the determination of disputed 
village boundaries. In addition, assets located near village boundaries often become points of 
contention in defining those boundaries. One example is the case between Pembeliangan Village 
and Semunad Village, where a facility of an existing industrial plantation company is located. Clear 
village boundaries are essential, as the size of each village’s area within the concession determines 
its share of company profit distribution.ion will determine the amount of profit sharing that the village 
will receive from the company. 
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CCB, CM1.2) 
The NMPP intends to maintain, protect, and improve the condition of high conservation values 
(HCVs) that are essential to the well-being of the community in the project zone. The project has 
identified areas that provide critical ecosystem service (HCV 4), areas that are fundamental for the 
372 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
livelihoods of communities (HCV 5), and areas that are critical for the traditional cultural identity of 
communities or sacred areas (HCV 6). 
4.1.3.1 HCV 4: Areas with Critical Ecosystem Services 
HCV 4 revolves around ecosystem services, which are benefits obtained by humans from 
ecosystems, including provisioning services such as food and water; regulating services such as 
flood control, drought mitigation, soil degradation prevention, and disease regulation; cultural 
services such as recreational benefits and other non-material benefits; as well as supporting 
services such as soil formation and nutrient cycling. HCV 4 areas found in the project area are 
detailed in the table below: 
Table 84. HCV 4 Areas in the Project Area 
High conservation value HCV 4: Areas with Critical Ecosystem Services 
1. Areas or ecosystems that are crucial as water sources 
and for flood control for downstream communities, 
2. Areas essential for erosion and sedimentation control, 
3. Areas that function as natural barriers to prevent the 
spread of forest or land fires, 
4. Areas that serve as habitats for pollination agents. 
Qualifying attribute 1. Ecosystems like peatland, riparian, and backswamp forest 
can store and hold more water than typical dryland forest. 
These ecosystems reduce peak flood discharge by 
extending the concentration time and decreasing the flow 
energy. Additionally, backswamps serve as reservoirs for 
surface runoff from the land. These functions help slow 
down the accumulation of water flow downstream, thereby 
reducing the risk of flooding. Mangroves in particular play 
a crucial role in protecting coastlines from waves, winds, 
and storms. Mangrove stands can safeguard settlements, 
structures, and agricultural areas from strong winds or 
seawater intrusion. Mangroves have also been proven to 
play a significant role in shielding coastal areas from 
storm surges. 
2. One of the main factors causing erosion is slope 
steepness, along with rainfall and land cover. The study 
area, with mostly flat or gently sloping terrain, has a low 
risk of soil erosion. Spatial analysis using the Revised 
Universal Soil Loss Equation (RUSLE) shows no areas 
within PT BHK's concession with an erosion hazard level 
(EHL) exceeding 180 tons/ha/year. This is due to limited 
steep slopes (>45%). Yet, there is still risk for riverbank 
erosion. This kind of erosion is controlled by riparian 
zones which are simultaneously filtering sediment too. 
373 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Minimum width for riparian zones is determined by 
Government Regulation No. 38/2011 on River, and 
considers river morphology and water levels. 
3. An area can act as a natural barrier against fires if it has 
characteristics such as high water content, high humidity, 
or natural water bodies like rivers, lakes, ponds, wet 
swamps, and other wetlands. These natural firebreaks 
form barriers within flammable landscapes, helping to 
prevent or limit the spread of fires. 
In PT BHK’s concession area, there is no recorded history 
of significant forest or land fires, except in the 
northeastern part of BHK II, near land currently being 
developed for oil palm plantations. The rest of the area 
consists of intact peat swamp forests, riparian zones, and 
mangroves that have not been affected by fires.  
4. Pollination services depend on habitats for pollinators like 
bees, birds, and bats. In the study area, certain plant 
species, such as Menggeris (Koompassia malaccensis), 
serve as nesting sites for pollinating insects like bees. The 
presence of these trees indicates a suitable habitat. 
Menggeris trees are commonly found along the transition 
between land and peat swamps, especially in the riparian 
zones of the Pembeliangan River. Their smooth bark is 
thought to attract bees, particularly Apis dorsata, which 
often nests in these trees. 
Focal area 1. Ecosystems such as peatland forest; riparian and 
backswamp forest, especially along Pembeliangan, 
Sumbal, Pampang, and Sebaung River, and mangrove 
forest 
2. Riparian forest 
3. Peatland, swamp, riparian, and mangrove forest 
4. Riparian forest of Pembeliangan River on which 
Koompassia malaccensis grow

4.1.3.2 HCV 5: Areas Fundamental for the Provision of Community Basic Needs 
HCV 5 consists of sites and resources essential for meeting the basic needs of local or indigenous 
communities (e.g., sources of livelihood, protein, and water), identified through engagement with the 
communities or indigenous peoples. This applies to whether basic needs are met directly 
(subsistence/self-consumption) or indirectly, through selling products (forest products or other natural 
resources) to earn cash. What classifies as basic needs include food, water, clothing, materials for 
housing and appliances, firewood, medicine, and livestock feed. HCV 5 areas identified in the project 
area are described in more detail below: 
Table 85. HCV 5 Areas in the Project Area

other freshwater species. 
2. Goods exchanged for other essential items or sold for 
cash to purchase medicine, clothing, or to pay school 
fees.

Qualifying attribute 1. Fish is a primary source of protein for over 50% of 
residents in the study villages, with most fish purchased 
and some caught locally. Villagers catch fish from 
nearby rivers, such as the Sumbal, Sebuku, and 
Sebaung Rivers, as well as smaller tributaries within or 
near the project area. Commonly caught species include 
patin (iridescent shark catfish), lais (glass catfish),

2. To earn cash, residents near PT BHK’s concession area 
engage in fishing, palm oil farming, company

and as part-time workers. Palm oil farming and fishing 
are the most common livelihoods. In areas with 
companies such as industrial plantations, mining, or 
estates, many work as company employees. 
Non-timber forest products (NTFPs), though not a primary 
source of income, are still utilized by some residents of 
Pembeliangan Village. These include honey harvested from 
forests during the honey season, usually from trees like lita, 
mangris, and bengkirai. Most honey is collected outside PT 
BHK’s concession area due to distance. It is consumed by 
families or sold locally, with current prices reaching 
Rp150,000 for a 600 ml bottle.

Sebuku River, and Sebaung River).

4.1.3.3 HCV 6: Areas Critical to the Traditional Culture of the Community 
HCV 6 refers to sites, resources, habitats, and landscapes of cultural, archaeological, or historical 
significance at a global or national level and/or critically important for the cultural, ecological, 
economic, or religious/sacred values of the traditional culture of local or indigenous communities, 
identified through engagement with the relevant local or indigenous communities. Based on the 
results of desk studies and initial FGDs and interviews with the community, no sites within the project 
area were found to qualify as HCV 6 area, however, during the FGDs that were conducted on July 
2025, it was mentioned (including by a customary leader) that there are sacred forest areas. 
Therefore, a delineation of these areas will be conducted in the immediate future to establish the 
boundaries of these areas.

4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 
Under the without-project land use scenario, the well-being of local communities, particularly in 
villages such as Pembeliangan and Nunukan Barat, is expected to deteriorate due to ongoing and 
increasingly unsustainable exploitation of natural resources. While many residents currently engage 
in resource use primarily for subsistence, a portion also participates in activities aimed at improving 
their livelihoods. However, based on findings from focus group discussions (FGDs) and interviews, 
most of the exploitative practices (e.g. illegal logging, land clearing for unregulated aquaculture or 
farming, hunting of protected species) are driven by external actors. These individuals, who are 
typically outsiders to the local communities, act as investors, landowners, or product buyers and are 
motivated by profit rather than subsistence. 
378 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Local residents often participate in these harmful activities not by choice, but due to limited 
alternative livelihood opportunities and the ready market provided by wealthier outsiders. As a result, 
environmental degradation is expected to intensify under the without-project scenario, further 
reducing the ecosystem services that communities depend on (e.g. clean water, biodiversity, and 
healthy soils) ultimately deteriorating their long-term well-being. Furthermore, the lack of effective 
monitoring and enforcement allows such activities to continue unchecked, reinforcing a cycle of 
ecological degradation and economic vulnerability. Notably, the SIA study indicates that many local 
actors are willing to stop these activities if viable, sustainable alternatives are made available, thus 
highlighting the need for proactive intervention.

conditions. The impacts were identified and evaluated through participatory methods to ensure that 
the community perspectives informed the outcomes. The key anticipated outcomes include 
increased livelihood opportunities, enhanced access to public services, and reduced environmental

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
The NMPP is committed to delivering net positive outcomes for local communities while recognizing 
that certain risks may arise from project implementation. In line with the precautionary principle, 
proactive measures have been designed to identify and mitigate any potential negative impacts on 
community well-being. 
To address concerns about a reduction in farmland (in regard to the project area that will be 
protected) and its impact on local livelihoods, the project will implement community mapping and an 
inventory of local land uses, including of agricultural zones. Collaborative land-use planning will be 
conducted through stakeholder consultations and in partnership with the Nunukan Food Security and 
381 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Agriculture Agency; these partnerships will support community empowerment initiatives that promote 
sustainable land use, provide targeted training through Agricultural Field Extension Officers (PPL), 
and strengthen Forest Farmer Groups (KTH). 
To prevent and resolve land conflicts, including unclear village boundaries, the project will undertake 
awareness raising sessions and consensus-based negotiation (musyawarah mufakat), as also 
described in activity 2.2 of section 2.1.17.  
To ensure equitable economic benefits, particularly for marginalized groups, local hiring will be 
prioritized. Communication on employment opportunities and community development programs will 
be disseminated widely and transparently. 
Training and safety briefings will be conducted for all workers to ensure safe working conditions, and 
protective equipment will be provided as required. In addition, insurance coverage will be offered 
where applicable, further safeguarding the well-being of individuals involved in project activities. 
While the project activities do not involve the use of hazardous substances, any waste or emissions 
(including noise, if relevant) generated during nursery or restoration operations will be managed 
according to environmental best practices to prevent community exposure, supported by the 
implementation of Pollution control and Health, Safety, Security, and Environment (HSSE) SOPs. 
Special attention will be given to the protection and inclusion of women and children through 
gender-sensitive consultation and grievance redress mechanisms that are accessible, confidential, 
and responsive; this will be ensured through the Gender Equality and Children’s Rights, and 
Grievance Redress SOPs. 
More details on the mitigation measures mentioned above can also be found on Appendix 3. Project 
Risks Table. These combined measures not only mitigate potential negative well-being impacts but 
also contribute to the maintenance and enhancement of High Conservation Value (HCV) attributes 
related to cultural identity, community resilience, and access to ecosystem services.

various aspects of well-being, including natural capital, human capital, social capital, physical capital,

4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, CM2.4) 
The NMPP will incur no negative impacts to the HCVs related to community well-being (HCV 4, 5, & 
6), as described below: 
HCV 4 - Areas with Critical Ecosystem services: The project activities, consisting of protection of 
existing riparian, peatland, and mangrove forest as well as restoration of degraded peatland and 
mangrove ecosystems will maintain and even enhance the HCV 4 values.  
HCV 5 - Areas Fundamental for the Provision of Community Basic Needs: The project activities will 
also include patrol and prohibition of destructive and illegal methods of capturing fish or other fishery 
products, such as electrocution or lye (potash) poisoning, therefore securing the rivers’ sustainability 
in the future. 
HCV 6 - Areas Critical to the Traditional Culture of the Community: The project activities will maintain 
and enhance the sacred forest area which is culturally important for the indigenous Tidung 
communities.

4.3 Other Stakeholder Impacts  
4.3.1 Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.1) 
List of other stakeholders can be found in Appendix 1. The project is not expected to cause harm to 
any parties beyond those noted in Section 4.2.1-4.2.2. Mitigation for these vulnerable populations 
have already been devised. In the planning stages, the project team assessed possible external 
parties who might be affected, including various governmental agencies and non-governmental 
organizations. Based on the assessment, it is concluded that these external parties are not 
anticipated to face significant disruption. Potential benefits for these surrounding stakeholders may 
include: 
● Broader understanding of key topics such as forest stewardship, agricultural development, 
land ownership rights, and governance at the local level, supported by targeted outreach 
and education campaigns. 
● Strengthened ability to collaborate with Project Zone communities on matters such as 
service delivery, dispute mediation, resource management (including forests, wildlife, and 
fisheries), land use planning at the village level, sustainable agriculture, and 
environmental outreach. 
● Greater access to funding streams, particularly from carbon credit revenues along with an 
increased grasp of the underlying principles of these financial mechanisms by these 
stakeholders. 
4.3.2 Mitigation of Negative Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, 
CM3.2)
 
No adverse effects on additional stakeholders have been identified, so no specific measures to 
address such impacts are necessary. As a result, this section does not apply.
 
4.3.3 Net Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.3) 
Since no harm to other stakeholders has been identified, the benefits outlined in Section 4.3.1 
represent the full extent of the project's overall influence on them. 
4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CCB, CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
The project has developed a community monitoring plan to track the social benefits delivered 
through its activities. This plan addresses key areas including improved livelihoods, education, 
health, and access to essential services. It is designed to be participatory, ensuring that local 
communities are directly involved in monitoring and reporting processes. 
385 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Annual monitoring will provide information on both community well-being and ecosystem protection, 
while also creating feedback loops to ensure results are shared with and validated by the 
communities themselves. Further details on the indicators, methods, and responsibilities are 
presented in the Monitoring Plan Table (Appendix 9
). 
4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, CM4.3) 
The project’s community monitoring plan, as well as the results of monitoring activities carried out 
under this plan, will be made publicly available through multiple channels to ensure transparency 
and accessibility including online (e.g. through whatsapp group). Summaries of the monitoring plan 
and its findings will be made available in English and translated into Bahasa Indonesia and 
disseminated directly to local communities and stakeholders through village meetings, and 
information boards located in key public areas within the project zone. As outlined in Sections 
2.3.3-2.3.4, this process is designed to ensure that both project-affected people and other interested 
stakeholders are well-informed of the project’s progress, outcomes, and any adaptive measures 
being taken. 
4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
The project is not seeking to be validated under the Gold Level for Exceptional Community Benefits.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
Location and general description  
The NMPP concession is located in Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan, Indonesia and spans an 
area of ± 15,591 ha, divided into two area units: BHK I (± 4,426 ha) and BHK II (± 11,165 ha) which 
is predominantly covered by peat and mangrove ecosystems. The current forest area stands around 
±8,394 ha. Located on the island of Borneo, biophysiographically this region belongs to the Oriental 
zone.  A biodiversity assessment conducted in 2024 in the two ecosystems of the project area(peat 
and mangrove) found that the characteristics of fauna in this area include: a large number of big 
mammal species, various types of monkeys, the presence of endemic animals, and oriental bird 
species with striking colors. Some families are only found at the boundaries of the Oriental region, 
such as Hylobatidae (gibbons and siamangs), Cercopithecidae (langurs and macaques), and 
Cynocephalidae (colugos). There are clear distinctions between the Oriental region and the 
surrounding regions, as seen at its northern boundary, namely the Isthmus of Kra.  
Biodiversity at the global scale indicates that this area is important and significant. It is part of the 
Important Bird Area and the Key Biodiversity Area of Sebuku-Sembakung (BirdLife International, 
2005). Threatened bird species in the Sebuku-Sembakung area include Ciconia stormi, Spizaetus 
nanus, and Polyplectron schleiermacheri. Regarding the Endemic Bird Area (EBA), the study area is 
located approximately 27 km from the project zone. 
The closest Peat Hydrological Unit/Kesatuan Hidgrologis Gambut (KHG) to the Project Zone is the 
Sebuku-Sembakung River KHG (Figure 48), located approximately 4.4 km southwest of the project 
zone. Within PT BHK II’s location permit, there is a Moratorium/Peta Indikatif Penghentian 
Pemberian Izin Baru (PIPPIB) area covering 10 ha (Figure 49). In the context of the project zone, 
particularly in the southern part, a Peatland Forest Moratorium area is located 5 km from BHK I and 
4 km from BHK II, with a total area of 2,612.36 ha. These moratorium areas are scattered and do not 
form a single contiguous block. In addition to peat, there is also a Forest Area Moratorium situated in 
the middle of Nunukan Island. 
387 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
Figure 48. Peat hydrological unit within the project zone. 
 
Figure 49. Moratorium area within the project zone 
Meanwhile, based on the ecoregion, the island of Kalimantan can be classified into 11 landscape 
types, namely: (1) Kalimantan fluvial plains, (2) Kahayan-Kapuas-Mahakam peatland complexes, (3) 
388 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Kalimantan coastal plains, (4) Meratus complex structural plains, (5) Kalimantan denudational 
mountains, (6) Kalimantan intrusive old igneous rock mountains, (7) Meratus complex structural 
mountains, (8) Kalimantan denudational hills, (9) Kalimantan karst hills, (10) Meratus complex 
structural hills, and (11) lakes (KLHK, 2016). Based on the Kalimantan Ecoregion Map, the 
assessment area falls within the Northern Lowlands Ecoregion. The flora and fauna in Kalimantan’s 
ecoregions show a relatively high level of endemism. Several animal species exist only on this 
island, such as the Bornean orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus), proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus), 
and the Sentarum langur (Presbytis chrysomelas ssp. cruciger Thomas, 1892). 
Biodiversity Description 
Fauna 
During the survey, faunal species that were found within the project area include the proboscis 
monkey (Nasalis larvatus; Endangered/EN), Storm’s stork (Ciconia stormi; Endangered/EN), sun 
bear (Helarctos malayanus; Vulnerable/VU), lesser adjutant (Leptoptilos javanicus; Vulnerable/VU), 
and Asiatic softshell turtle (Amyda cartilaginea; VU) (IUCN, 2024). The orangutan (Pongo 
pygmaeus; Critically Endangered/CR) and Bornean elephant (Elephas maximus ssp. borneensis; 
Endangered/EN) occur outside the study area, but there is a significant likelihood that these species 
may also be recorded within PT BHK’s concession. 
Flora 
The flora of Kalimantan belongs to the Malesian region, which includes Indonesia, Papua New 
Guinea, Sarawak, Sabah, Brunei, Southern Thailand, and the Philippines. Its types and 
characteristics differ from those of the flora found in Asia and Australia. The Isthmus of Kra serves 
as the northern boundary of this region. There are 375 plant genera in the south that do not extend 
northward, and 200 genera in the north that do not extend southward. Around 10,000–15,000 
species of flowering plants occur on the island, including about 3,000 tree species, among which 267 
belong to the Dipterocarpaceae family. In addition, there are about 2,000 species of orchids and 
1,000 species of ferns (MacKinnon et al., 2000
34
).  
During the survey, protected plant species that were found include: Shorea balangeran (VU), Shorea 
teysmanniana (CR), Gonystylus bancanus (VU), and Dyera polyphylla (VU), which are all found in 
peat swamp forests. In mangrove forest ecosystems, the rare species Camptostemon philippinensis 
(EN) is found. Meanwhile, in mineral soil forests, protected species such as Cantleya corniculata 
(VU) and Eusideroxylon zwageri (VU) are present. Table 88 and the paragraphs below put more 
detail on the findings from the biodiversity survey conducted in July 2024. 
34
 MacKinnon J, Philips K, van Balen B. 1998. Seri Panduan Lapangan Burung-Burung di Sumatera Jawa, Bali dan Kalimantan. 
Puslitbang Biologi – LIPI. Bogor.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Table 88. Protected plant species identified during the 2024 Biodiversity Survey 
Species and habitat  
The total biodiversity found in the BHK project area during 
the assessment that was conducted in 2024, includes 437 
species. The fauna composition consists of 21 mammal, 92 
bird, and 9 herpetofauna species; they represent typical 
biodiversity found in North Kalimantan’s lowland ecosystems, 
particularly within the Sebuku Watershed. However, this 
inventory likely reflects only a portion of the total biodiversity 
in the area, as the region is part of a larger ecosystem with 
significant conservation importance. 
The project area primarily comprises peat and mangrove 
ecosystems which are critical for supporting forest-dependent 
species, such as the Storm stork (Ciconia stormi), the 
proboscis monkeys (Nasalis larvatus), and saltwater 
crocodiles (Crocodylus porosus), many of which are sensitive 
to habitat disturbance. The project aims to protect and 
enhance all such areas within its project zone thus it will not 
adversely impact any areas needed for habitat connectivity. 
Areas needed for 
habitat connectivity 
The project area functions as a green corridor and it plays a 
critical role in linking ecosystems (mangroves and peatlands) 
within the Sebuku Watershed thus it will not adversely impact 
any areas needed for habitat connectivity. 
During a 2024 biodiversity survey, in the project area, a total of 437 species were identified as High 
Conservation Value (HCV) species, particularly HCV 1. HCV 1 represents fauna or biodiversity 
species that are included in the Vulnerable (VU), Endangered (EN), and Critically Endangered (CR) 
categories according to the IUCN Red List; Appendix I and II of the Convention on International 
Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES); and species protected by the 
Government of Indonesia (MoEF Regulation No. P106/2018). During 2025 interviews with locals the 
endangered Orcaella brevirostris was also identified; local fishers in the Nunukan area have reported 
sightings to the project team, and opportunistic observations from citizen-science platforms show 
recent records in the wider North Kalimantan region (iNaturalist platform).  The area also hosts 21 
endemic species (Table 90), highlighting its ecological importance, located in Nunukan Regency, 
North Kalimantan.

Threats to biodiversity 
The biodiversity within the NMPP area faces significant threats primarily due to poaching, illegal 
logging, conversion to palm oil plantations, and unauthorized aquaculture ponds. Illegal logging has 
led to habitat fragmentation, reducing the availability of critical habitats for forest-dependent species. 
391 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
The expansion of palm oil plantations has resulted in the loss of peatland ecosystems. Unauthorized 
aquaculture development, in mangrove areas, disrupts aquatic habitats and diminishes the 
ecosystem's natural protective functions. Poaching remains a persistent issue throughout the area, 
threatening endangered and endemic species such as Storm stork (Ciconia stormi), proboscis 
monkeys (Nasalis larvatus), and saltwater crocodiles (Crocodylus porosus). These activities 
collectively endanger the ecological integrity of the Sebuku Watershed and undermine the critical 
ecosystem services it provides. Peatland degradation, driven by drainage and fires, not only 
releases stored carbon but also threatens the survival of species that rely on these unique 
ecosystems. Without intervention, these pressures could severely impact the region's biodiversity 
and ecosystem services. 
5.1.2 High Conservation Values (CCB, B1.2) 
The NMPP area, located in Nunukan Regency, North Kalimantan, Indonesia, is an area of significant 
ecological importance. This region features diverse ecosystems, including tropical rainforests and 
peatlands, which provide habitats for various species, some of which are endemic, threatened, or 
rare. Nunukan is part of the Heart of Borneo initiative, emphasizing its role in maintaining biodiversity 
and supporting the survival of species in their natural patterns of distribution and abundance. The 
region's ecosystems also contribute to climate regulation, water conservation, and cultural values for 
local communities. 
 Table 91. HCV 1 Areas in the Project Area 
High conservation value HCV 1 - Rare, Threatened and Endangered species 
Qualifying attribute 
The project area serves as a habitat for several endangered 
and vulnerable species listed on the IUCN Red List. Among 
the endangered species is the Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis 
larvatus), which relies on the peat and mangrove ecosystems 
for survival. Additionally, the area is home to vulnerable bird 
species such as the Storm’s Stork (Ciconia stormi) and the 
Lesser Adjutant Stork (Leptoptilos javanicus). Several 
hornbill species, including the Black Hornbill (Anthracoceros 
albirostris), Bushy-crested Hornbill (Anorrhinus galeritus), 
and Wrinkled Hornbill (Rhabdotorrhinus corrugatus), are also 
present, indicating the ecological richness of the region. 
Furthermore, the flora in the project area includes critically 
important tree species such as Shorea teysmanniana 
(Meranti Rawa), which plays a vital role in maintaining forest 
integrity. These species underscore the ecological 
significance of BHK and highlight the need for conservation 
measures to protect these vulnerable habitats and their 
biodiversity.

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
The BHK area is largely surrounded by production forests, industrial plantations, and oil palm 
plantations as well as some illegal aquaculture ponds, placing it at high risk of land use conversion. 
In the absence of the project, critical natural ecosystems, particularly peatlands and mangrove 
forests, are likely to face continued degradation and clearing for the expansion of industrial 
plantations and oil palm plantations. These ecosystems are home to a rich diversity of species, 
including the endangered Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus) and several hornbill species such as 
the Black Hornbill (Anthracoceros albirostris), Bushy-crested Hornbill (Anorrhinus galeritus), and 
Wrinkled Hornbill (Rhabdotorrhinus corrugatus). 
Peatlands within the BHK area play a crucial role in carbon storage and water regulation, while 
mangrove ecosystems act as natural coastal buffers, reducing erosion and storm impacts. They also 
both provide vital breeding and feeding grounds for wildlife, including migratory birds. Unchecked 
land conversion would destroy these habitats, leading to significant biodiversity loss and substantial 
greenhouse gas emissions from soil oxidation and degradation. 
These ecological shifts not only endanger species survival but also threaten the provision of 
essential ecosystem services relied upon by local communities, including fisheries, water transport, 
395 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
and various livelihood activities. Without intervention, the BHK area’s unique biodiversity and 
ecological functions are at risk of being permanently lost. 
5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.1) 
Biodiversity element Fauna Diversity 
Estimated change Reduction in threats to fauna and improved habitat 
conditions, supporting the presence and potential population 
recovery of RTE species. 
Justification of change The project area provides critical habitat for several 
threatened and ecologically significant fauna species. Prior to 
the project, unsustainable land use such as mangrove and 
peatland clearing for aquaculture, illegal hunting, and 
disturbance from unregulated access posed direct and 
indirect threats to these species. Through forest restoration, 
reforestation, hydrological rehabilitation, and conservation 
activities (e.g. regular patrolling), the project mitigates these 
pressures and contributes to habitat quality improvement. 
Moreover, community engagement programs and 
awareness-raising further discourage unsustainable 
practices and promote coexistence with wildlife, while 
ongoing monitoring will be established to assess species 
presence, threat levels, and habitat utilization. 
 
Biodiversity element Flora Diversity 
Estimated change Enhanced protection, regeneration, and diversity of native 
plant species in restored and conserved ecosystems. 
Justification of change The NMPP seeks to reverse the ecosystems’ degradation 
through active restoration and protection of native flora, 
especially mangrove species such as Rhizophora spp., 
Avicennia spp., and Bruguiera spp., which are critical for 
stabilizing coastlines, enhancing carbon sequestration, and 
supporting associated biodiversity. Through natural 
regeneration and assisted planting, the project aims to 
increase both the abundance and richness of native plant 
species. In addition, the prevention of further land conversion 
through patrolling, monitoring, and awareness raising will 
396 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
preserve existing vegetation and promote long-term 
resilience of flora communities. 
 
Biodiversity element Peat Ecosystem 
Estimated change 
Improved protection and restoration of peatland hydrology 
and vegetation, leading to recovery of ecosystem function 
and reduction of risks. 
Justification of change 
The NMPP directly addresses the peatlands’ degradation 
drivers by preventing further encroachment through patrols 
and engagement with local communities and authorities. The 
project will restore the peat dome’s natural hydrological 
function, which is critical for preserving below-ground 
biodiversity, stabilizing carbon stores, and supporting 
peat-specific flora such as Shorea spp., Calophyllum spp., 
and Campnosperma spp.. By halting oxidation and reducing 
GHG emissions from peat decomposition, the intervention 
contributes to both biodiversity recovery and climate benefits. 
 
Biodiversity element Mangrove Ecosystem 
Estimated change Improved ecological integrity, species diversity, and stability 
of mangrove ecosystems through their protection and 
restoration. 
Justification of change The state of mangrove ecosystems will be ameliorated by 
implementing active restoration using native mangrove 
species (e.g., Rhizophora, Avicennia, Bruguiera), protecting 
remaining intact stands, and preventing future 
encroachment. Ultimately, the project strengthens the 
structure and function of these coastal ecosystems. In 
addition, the restriction of destructive activities (primarily 
through patrolling and facilitating law enforcement) promote 
natural regeneration and carbon sequestration. Moreover, 
monitoring and awareness programs will foster local 
stewardship and the reduction of anthropogenic pressures. 
These interventions support habitat connectivity, enhance 
nursery grounds for aquatic and terrestrial fauna, and 
increase the resilience of mangrove ecosystems to climate 
change and coastal hazards.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template

5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
The project is committed to conserving biodiversity and enhancing High Conservation Values (HCVs) 
within the project area through a precautionary and proactive approach. Although no negative 
impacts on biodiversity are currently foreseen, the project has designed and implemented specific 
measures to prevent, mitigate, and respond to potential threats. These include the delineation and 
protection of HCV areas via buffer zones, the promotion of sustainable land-use practices, and close 
collaboration with local communities to reduce pressures such as illegal hunting, encroachment, and 
habitat degradation. Regular biodiversity monitoring and HCV assessments will allow for the early 
detection of emerging risks, ensuring timely corrective action. As previously described, the project 
will also undertake ecosystem restoration through the reforestation of degraded areas and the 
rehabilitation of mangrove and peatland ecosystems. Targeted conservation activities will safeguard 
RTE species, while the establishment of ecological corridors will help maintain habitat connectivity 
and support broader landscape-level biodiversity resilience. 
These measures are grounded in the precautionary principle, as they are designed not only to 
prevent harm but also to actively improve ecological integrity. The project will apply adaptive 
management practices, using feedback from monitoring to refine interventions and ensure that 
biodiversity and HCV values are continually safeguarded and strengthened throughout the project’s 
duration. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 
In the without-project scenario, the BHK area is highly vulnerable to land use conversion driven by 
surrounding industrial plantations and oil palm expansion as well as illegal aquaculture ponds. As 
detailed in the baseline, this would result in the progressive loss of peatland and mangrove 
ecosystems, in turn leading to substantial degradation of biodiversity, habitat fragmentation, and the 
disappearance of threatened and endemic species. The degradation of these ecosystems would 
also result in the loss of critical ecosystem services like water regulation, coastal protection, and 
fisheries productivity. 
Under the project scenario, the anticipated net impacts on biodiversity are clearly positive. The 
project takes a proactive, science-based, and community-driven approach to protecting and 
restoring natural habitats through the following key activities: 
● Rehabilitation and restoration of peatlands and mangroves, which will improve habitat 
quality, enhance carbon sequestration, and reestablish natural hydrological regimes. 
● Creation and maintenance of biodiversity monitoring and patrol teams, which will reduce 
illegal hunting, logging, and encroachment while safeguarding RTE species. 
● Establishment of wildlife corridors and buffer zones, reconnecting fragmented habitats and 
increasing ecological resilience 
398 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
● Participatory biodiversity monitoring and awareness-building with local communities, 
enhancing their capacity to engage in sustainable resource management and conservation. 
● Alternative livelihood development, which reduces pressure on ecosystems by shifting 
economic activities away from extractive practices. 
Through these interventions, the project not only halts further biodiversity loss but actively 
contributes to species protection, habitat restoration, and improved ecosystem function, achieving a 
measurable net gain for biodiversity in the project zone. 
The project strengthens the adaptive capacity of ecosystems, biodiversity, and communities by 
restoring hydrology in peatlands and mangroves, protecting climate-vulnerable species, and 
enhancing habitat connectivity. These efforts improve resilience to climate-related disturbances like 
flooding and drought, while community-based strategies (e.g. livelihood diversification and 
ecosystem education) ensure both people and nature are better equipped to respond to long-term 
climate impacts. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
High attention will be given to the protection and conservation of HCV 1, 2, and 3, as identified in 
Section 5.1.2. These values encompass critical areas for biodiversity, ecosystems, and landscapes 
that are crucial for environmental sustainability. The protection and conservation efforts will be 
carried out in alignment with the HCV Resource Network Common Guidance for the Management 
and Monitoring of High Conservation Values. Additionally, the criteria and indicators outlined in the 
CCB Standards, will guide the implementation and monitoring activities. Inherently, the project aims 
to contribute to the improvement of the protection of the ecosystems found within the project area 
including the vast biodiversity they host. Therefore, with activities such as forest patrol, continuous 
monitoring and awareness raising, the project will have a positive impact on the HCVs.  
5.2.5 Species Used (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5, B2.6) 
The project’s restoration and rehabilitation activities will utilize only native species. No non-native 
species will be used in the project area. More details can be seen in the table below:

5.2.8 Inputs Justification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.8) 
Name Organic fertilizer 
Justification of use Organic fertilizers are used for enhancing soil fertility 
and supplying essential nutrients to support optimal 
plant growth in nursery and restoration areas. 
Potential adverse effect Overuse can lead to nutrients leaching into nearby 
water bodies. May impact microbial communities in 
the soil, potentially disrupting natural processes and 
biodiversity. 
Mitigation Strategies The project will employ a strategy of minimal use of 
organic fertilizers, avoiding manure-based options to 
prevent unintended environmental impacts. A 
thorough literature review will inform best practices, 
ensuring effective application that maintains 
ecological balance. 
  
Name Organic pesticide 
Justification of use Organic pesticides provide an environmentally 
friendly solution for pest management in restoration 
projects. By utilizing naturally derived compounds 
(e.g spices, herb, etc) they could control pest 
populations while safeguarding ecological integrity. 
Potential adverse effect If improperly applied, organic pesticides can still 
impact non-target organisms and may contribute to 
pest resistance, undermining restoration efforts.  
Mitigation Strategies Organic pesticides will be used minimally and only in 
cases of outbreaks at nurseries and/or planting 
sites. Conducting a literature review or research is 
essential to guide the effective application of organic 
pesticides. This should be complemented by 
employing targeted application methods, integrating 
multiple pest management strategies, and 
monitoring pest populations. This comprehensive 
approach ensures adaptive management and 
minimizes impacts on the ecosystem. 
5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
404 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Project activities typically generate various types of waste from general operations (domestic waste). 
This waste is produced during activities such as project site monitoring in the field and office 
activities at the basecamp. The waste generated includes items like soap, detergent, oil, and food 
scraps. In the field, waste mainly consists of food packaging during monitoring activities and other 
organic waste. To address this issue, the company will establish regulations for all employees in the 
form of SOPs or related documents regarding waste-generating activities. Waste produced in the 
field must be taken back out, while waste around the office must be properly processed to minimize 
its environmental impact. The process will begin with the identification and classification of all waste 
into categories such as organic, inorganic, and hazardous waste. Regular monitoring and audits will 
be conducted to ensure compliance, with reports documenting the types and quantities of waste 
generated and how they are managed. Employees will receive training on waste management 
procedures, and clear workflows will be established to assign specific responsibilities for waste 
handling and disposal.  
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (CCB, 
B3.2) 
The project is designed in such a way that it does not generate any negative offsite biodiversity 
benefits. 
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
The project acknowledges potential negative impacts on biodiversity outside the project zone, such 
as habitat displacement, ecosystem disturbances, and pressure from poaching. Within the project 
zone, the initiative actively restores degraded habitats and protects HCVs, which serve as critical 
sanctuaries for these species. The project also addresses external threats by implementing regular 
patrolling and monitoring, engaging local communities in awareness programs, and collaborating 
with enforcement authorities to combat illegal activities. By integrating habitat restoration, species 
protection, and anti-poaching efforts, the project not only safeguards biodiversity within its 
boundaries but also reduces pressures on surrounding ecosystems. This comprehensive approach 
ensures a net positive impact on biodiversity, contributing to both local and regional conservation 
goals.
 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
The biodiversity monitoring plan of the NMPP is strategically designed to measure and support the 
conservation impacts of our activities across diversified ecosystems. This comprehensive plan 
405 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
incorporates scientific methodologies tailored to align closely with our biodiversity objectives and the 
anticipated ecological, social, and economic outcomes. 
Our focus encompasses comprehensive direct and indirect monitoring techniques, the detail is given 
in Appendix 9. For indirect monitoring will include monitoring of mammals, birds, vegetation, and 
marine fauna. Initially, permanent monitoring sites and/or observation routes will be established. 
Camera traps are strategically positioned to study and record mammal populations, gathering critical 
data on their presence and behavioral patterns. Bird populations are systematically observed to 
track fluctuations and their trends over time. Vegetation surveys are conducted to evaluate plant 
diversity and health, providing insights into ecosystem dynamics. In marine environments, our 
monitoring efforts target intricate interactions within aquatic biodiversity, crucial for maintaining 
ecological resilience.  
Whereas, indirect monitoring will provide data about land cover change, fire incidence, hydrology, 
illegal natural resource use and associated preventative measures. These indicators are vital for 
understanding broader environmental trends in the project area and informing adaptive management 
strategies. By evaluating these variables, we gain insights into the cumulative impacts of natural 
events and human activities on the health of ecosystems, enabling responsive action. 
5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
The project’s biodiversity monitoring plan, as well as the results of monitoring activities carried out 
under this plan, will be made publicly available through multiple channels to ensure transparency 
and accessibility including online (e.g. through whatsapp group). Summaries of the monitoring plan 
and its findings will be made available in Bahasa Indonesia and disseminated directly to local 
communities and stakeholders through village meetings, and information boards located in key 
public areas within the project zone. As outlined in Sections 2.3.3-2.3.4, this process is designed to 
ensure that both project-affected people and other interested stakeholders are well-informed of the 
project’s progress, outcomes, and any adaptive measures being taken. 
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 
Throughout the BHK project area a variety of threatened species has been identified, including 
mammals, aves, herpetofauna, and plants that are of high biodiversity conservation priority status.

5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 
Trigger species Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus) - 
Endangered 
409 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Population trend at start of project The species is in greater abundance in 
Kalimantan, populations range in size from over 
1000 to less than 100 depending on past and 
current threats (Meijaard and Nijman, 2000)
35
. 
The species is relatively easily hunted with little 
effort; entire populations can be hunted to 
extinction.  
Without-project scenario Without the project, Nasalis larvatus would likely 
face increased hunting for consumption and 
trade, as is currently happening, putting their 
populations at serious risk and possibly leading to 
local extinction. This would disrupt the 
ecosystem, as their role in seed dispersal helps 
maintain forest health. Habitats would also be 
further degraded by unregulated land use and 
deforestation, and while communities might see 
short-term benefits from these activities, it would 
come at the cost of long-term resource loss and 
declining livelihoods. 
With-project scenario The project will focus on monitoring, habitat 
restoration, and community involvement. 
Boat-based surveys will be conducted (Atmoko et 
al., 2020
36
) to examine and monitor the Nasalis 
larvatus in the project area. Regular surveys will 
track their population and habitat conditions, while 
protected zones and patrols will be established to 
prevent illegal hunting and logging. Communities 
will be engaged through awareness programs 
about the importance of conserving Nasalis 
larvatus and will be provided with alternative 
livelihood opportunities to reduce pressure on the 
species. 
36
 Atmoko, T., Mardiastuti, A., Bismark, M., Prasetyo, L. B and Iskandar E. 2020. Habitat suitability of Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis 
larvatus) in Berau Delta, East Kalimantan, Indonesia. Biodiversitas. 21: 5155 - 5163.  
 
35
 Meijaard, E. & Nijman, V. (2000) ‘Distribution and conservation of the proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus) in Borneo’, Biological 
Conservation, 92(1), pp. 15–24

Trigger species Storm’s Stork (Ciconia stormi) - Endangered 
Population trend at start of project The population is estimated at 300 to 1,750 
mature individuals (BirdLife International, 2025)
37
 
Without-project scenario The cause of such rapid declines is the 
widespread and large-scale clearance of the 
lowland forest to make space for industrial 
plantations, oil palm, rubber and timber. These 
losses have disproportionately impacted 
peatswamp forests (Miettinen et al. 2011
38
) which 
host the largest population of this species.  
With-project scenario 
The project will restore and protect critical 
lowland and peat swamp forests within the 
species’ range, reducing habitat loss and 
degradation. By halting further deforestation and 
promoting natural regeneration, it will stabilize 
and potentially improve habitat quality for Storm’s 
Stork. Additionally, awareness raising campaigns 
38
 
Miettinen, J., Shi, C. and Liew, S.C., 2011. Deforestation rates in insular Southeast Asia between 2000 and 2010. Global change 
biology, 17(7), pp.2261-2270.
 
37
 BirdLife International (2025). Species factsheet: Storm's Stork Ciconia stormi. Downloaded from 
https://datazone.birdlife.org/species/factsheet/storms-stork-ciconia-stormi  on 03/12/2025

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
will be initiated for the local communities and 
regular monitoring will be conducted using the 
point count survey method (Sharma et al., 
2024
39
) surrounding the Storm’s Stork habitat 
area. These interventions are expected to help 
slow and even reverse local population decline 
and support the species' long-term viability in 
Borneo.

Trigger species Pesut Mahakam (Orcaella brevirostris) - 
Endangered 
Population trend at start of project Pesut Mahakam occurs in South and Southeast 
Asia, limited to shallow coastal waters and river. 
This species is only found in small numbers 
(<100), fragmented in the Mahakam River (Budi 
et al., 2022
40
), East Kalimantan and currently 
listed as Endangered on the IUCN Red List. 
40
 Budi, T., Piyapattanakorn, S., Kreb, D., Yuda, P., Ninwat, S., Hardwises, P., Prachamkhai, P., Senanan, W., Thongsukdee, S., 
Phavaphutanon, J. and Klinsawat, W., 2022. Mitogenomes provide insight into complex evolutionary history of freshwater and 
coastal Irrawaddy dolphin (Orcaella brevirostris Gray, 1866) in Thailand and Indonesia. Agriculture and Natural Resources, 56(3),

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

   
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                             CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
Without-project scenario Without the project, Pesut Mahakam will continue 
to face significant threats, primarily from habitat 
loss. Their habitat has been highly modified and 
degraded by human activities due to ongoing 
human activities, such as deforestation, pollution, 
and river traffic. 
With-project scenario 
The project will restore degraded mangrove 
areas, thereby enhancing biodiversity by 
providing critical habitat for various species, 
including Orcaella brevirostris. Reforestation 
efforts are expected to help stabilize riverbanks, 
reduce erosion, improve water quality, and have 
potential to mitigate habitat loss.  
Threat-reduction measures will also be 
implemented (e.g., boat-speed controls, 
awareness campaigns) that directly reduce the 
main causes of mortality and habitat loss for the 
species. The project will help to monitor and 
detect the occurrences of Orcaella brevirostris in 
the regency. Local communities will be involved 
in the monitoring effort (through a community 
reporting/citizen-science network to collect 
systematic sighting records), on top of 
boat-based survey methods (Azizul et al., 
2022
41
).

APPENDIX 1: STAKEHOLDER DESCRIPTION TABLE 
 
Stakeholder Rights, interest, and overall relevance to the project 
Stakeholders 
Illegal loggers 
Identifying individuals engaging in illegal logging and estimating their numbers proved to be 
challenging because these people were not forthcoming when being asked during the FGDs. Meeting 
them in person when they are in action was even more difficult due to the sheer size of the project 
area, the unpredictable timing of their activities, and their tendency to hide from outsiders traversing 
the forest. These people left their mark in the forest; such as logged over areas full of stumps, logging 
slash, and residues; wooden huts; and skidding roads that span kilometers. Species commonly 
harvested are meranti and bengkirai (Shorea sp.), ulin (Eusideroxylon zwageri), and kapur 
(Dryobalanops sp.). These timber are mostly sold elsewhere, though some ends up in Pembeliangan 
to supply for building materials, furniture, or other household items. These groups pose a significant 
main threat for forest conservation and will need to be contained by the patrol team. 
Hunters/Poachers 
Similar to the illegal loggers, the actual figures for hunters or poachers are still unknown due to them 
being discreet and the vast size of the project area. Only one data point emerged during the 
stakeholder identification process. A community member in Semunad stated that there are “only” 
around 20 hunters/poachers in their village. However, this figure remains unverified and needs further 
investigation. The hunted species include proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus), Southeast Asian 
softshell turtle (Amyda caltilaginea), Bornean yellow muntjak (Muntiacus atherodes), Sambar deer 
(Rusa unicolor), Bornean bearded pig (Sus barbatus) and Long-tailed macaque (Macaca fascicularis). 
Some of this species are endangered, protected under the national law (Minister of Environment & 
Forestry No. P.106/MENLHK/SETJEN/KUM.1/12/2018 on the Protected Flora & Fauna Species), and 
also endemic, like the proboscis monkey. If poachers are left unchecked, biodiversity within the 
project zone & area will deteriorate further. 
414 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
Smallholder Oil Palm 
Growers 
Smallholder oil palm growers mostly live in Pembeliangan, particularly in Sebakis. On average, each 
smallholder manages between 2 and 5 hectares of oil palm. Survey data indicate that there are 10 
farmer groups in the area, each comprising of 25–40 members with each farmer typically managing 
2.5 to 5 hectares. Their predisposition to expand and encroach the primary forest and undrained peat 
area constitute a major threat to the project area.  
Aquaculture farmers 
Aquaculture farmers operate directly within the NMPP/BHK I project area. This enterprise is wholly 
owned by outsiders from Tarakan; a 2.5 hour journey on a speedboat from Nunukan. The operation is 
illegal, and even local village officials were unaware of its existence. At the moment, the ponds have 
already expanded to nearly 600 hectares. Given the high profitability of this business, further 
expansion is likely if not dealt with. 
Foragers 
Foragers mainly reside in Pembeliangan village. They mainly harvest forest honey. The collection is 
done in various areas and only during the honey season. However, honey collection is not a primary 
source of income due to its seasonal nature. Trees that are commonly used as honeybee nesting 
sites include lita (Alstonia sp.), mangris (Koompassia excelsa), bengkirai, among others. 
Seaweed Farmers Seaweed farmers unite in groups which become the beneficiaries of government (national & regional) 
policies and the Fisheries Agency. These are the people who are actually working on the ground and 
whose income depends on seaweed farming, making them potential partners for the implementation 
of community development programs in the farming sector. 
Capture Fishermen Capture fishermen receive assistance and are involved in the programs initiated by the government 
and Fisheries Agency. These are the people whose income relies heavily on capture fishery and they 
tend to be of low-income population. Thus, these groups make potential and appropriate partners for 
the implementation of community development programs in the farming sector. 
Customary Institutions 
(Lembaga Adat) 
These institutions manage matters related to traditional customs and practices. In the villages with 
high interaction with and impact from and to the project, their role is not as vital as they used to be, as 
the area has become more cosmopolitan with the settling of migrant groups. Yet, their influence is still 
strong, especially among the indigenous people. 
415 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
Village Governments 
(Pemerintah Desa) 
Institution with the highest authority in the village, responsible for governance in its area. The village 
head in particular will be the main policy and decision maker during the implementation of project 
activities/interventions related to the communities. 
Village-owned Enterprises 
(Badan Usaha Milik 
Daerah/BUMDes) 
Village-run enterprises whose profits are utilized for village development, benefiting all residents. 
These enterprises might be the potential “vehicle” for community development programs. 
Village Consultative Body 
(Badan Permusyawaratan 
Desa/BPD) 
Institution overseeing village governance and representing each hamlet/village unit/neighborhood. 
Often referred to as the “parliament” of villages in Indonesia, they consult the village leaders on issues 
pertaining to the welfare of the people. 
Family Welfare 
Empowerment Group 
(Pemberdayaan 
Kesejahteraan 
Keluarga/PKK) 
This group is an organization composed of women who participate in efforts to improve family welfare. 
The activities carried out span several areas, with the primary focus on meeting what is known in 
Indonesia as the "three basic needs": clothing, food, and shelter. Other activities include skills and 
capacity development, health, environmental conservation, and cooperatives. To ensure women's 
representation in project activities, the project developer will engage this group during the 
implementation of community development programs. 
Youth (Karang Taruna) Youth in all villages are generally involved in social and economic activities, but some are also looking 
for work opportunities. 
Other Stakeholders 
Other villagers 
Other villagers refers to residents of the three villages who do not fall under the previously mentioned 
“Stakeholder” categories. Out of ~24.000 people who call the three villages home, this group likely 
represents the largest portion and might become beneficiary for future project activities. 
Nunukan Regency 
Government 
Led by the bupati (regent), the regency government holds the highest authority in Nunukan. All 
permits for the project and its ensuing activities have to be approved by the bupati. More technical 
affairs are managed by the dinas (regency government agencies) answerable to the bupati, which will 
be explained in more detail in the latter half of this table. Maintaining a good relationship with this 
entity is a must for the smooth running of the project. 
North Kalimantan Province 
Government 
In the AFOLU sector, the provincial government holds several key mandates: 1) proposing changes to 
the status & function of forest areas (through revisions on spatial planning document), 2) issuing 
416 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
non-forestry business permits that alter forest cover, 3) managing KPH, 4) recognizing Indigenous 
Peoples through regional regulations, 5) integrating Social Forestry (Perhutanan Sosial) into regional 
development plans & budgets, and protect & manage natural forests within APL as designated in the 
official Regional Spatial Plan (Rencana Tata Ruang dan Wilayah/RTRW). Once administrative 
obligations have been resolved in the early phase of the project, usually project developers have 
limited further engagement with this authority.  
District Governments 
(Nunukan, Sebuku, and Tulin 
Onsoi) 
District governments implement technical government activities at the district level, an administrative 
unit above the villages. It is necessary to notify them and ask their permission first before running the 
interventions or community development programs at the village level. 
Forest Management Unit 
(Kesatuan Pengelolaan 
Hutan/KPH) of Nunukan 
Regency 
KPH is responsible for operational and technical support activities in forest management within its 
jurisdiction (field level). They are the technical and local partner of the project proponent during the 
planning and design phase of the project, and the cooperation will continue in the future during the 
implementation phase. 
Fisheries Agency (Dinas 
Perikanan) of Nunukan 
Regency 
This agency is represented by a "fisheries outreach officer (penyuluh perikanan)” on the ground, 
whose decisions are influenced by the district and village government. These officers are potential 
partners for the project proponents during the project activity implementation in the communities. 
Food Security and 
Agriculture Agency (Dinas 
Ketahanan Pangan dan 
Pertanian) of Nunukan 
Regency 
This agency helps the Regent in formulating policies, carrying out tasks, providing public services, and 
managing food security and agriculture. 
Environmental Agency 
(Dinas Lingkungan Hidup) of 
Nunukan Regency 
This agency has a duty to assist the regent in formulating technical policies, carrying out duties, 
providing public services related to the environmental sector, and also overseeing environmental 
management within Nunukan Regency. 
Community and Village 
Empowerment Agency 
(Dinas Pemberdayaan 
Masyarakat Desa) of 
Nunukan Regency 
This agency deals with rural issues within the regency. Nowadays, it focuses more on mapping 
customary territories in the region. This is being done not only to inventory customary lands but also 
to facilitate the resolution of boundary issues between villages. Through this activity, residents are 
encouraged to jointly map their respective territories, which will then be formally recognized as 
customary land in each village. As a result, there will be mutual recognition of customary lands 
417 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
between villages, and agreements on inter-village boundaries can be reached—issues that have long 
been problematic for several villages in Nunukan Regency. 
NGOs A non-governmental organization intended to assist the community in relevant fields, for example a 
group called TNBA (The North Borneo Adventure) whose activities focus on mangrove nursery and 
planting since 2016.

state of biodiversity and ecosystems within the project area 
and they will be monitored continuously starting from Year 1.
