Soil degradation 
and soil erosion 
No risk identified — There will be no use of heavy equipment within project area 
soils. One of the main goals of the project is to protect and 
enhance soil functions throughout the project area. 
Water consumption 
and stress 
No risk identified — Water use for nurseries will be optimized to ensure there is no 
waste. No other water usage is envisaged within the project 
area apart from water used to fight incidences of fire. 
Habitats (and areas 
needed for habitat 
connectivity) for 
rare, threatened, 
and endangered 
species 
No risk identified — 
A biodiversity baseline assessment was conducted as part of 
the feasibility study and continuous monitoring of biodiversity 
will be occurring throughout the project. Since one of the aims 
of the project is to protect RTE species, identified critical 
zones will be excluded from disturbance and all project 
activities will be conducted with the aim the ensure that such 
habitats remain undisturbed. 
Areas needed for 
habitat connectivity 
No risk identified — The project activities will not affect green corridors. The 
project aims to protect and not disturb biodiversity and 
ecosystems within the project area. Any restoration activities 
will ensure the enhancement of habitat connectivity in areas 
where it was previously lost.  
Invasive species  No risk identified — Only native species will be planted. In areas where Nypa palm 
is found there will be supervision to ensure that it does not 
spread uncontrollably (especially in mangrove areas that are 
currently degraded). Overall, plantations will be highly 
monitored to ensure the ameliorated ecosystem conditions do 
not allow for invasive species to thrive.  
Ecosystem 
conversion 
No risk identified — The natural vegetation will be protected. Additionally, no 
drainage or clearance are allowed within the project area and 
strict patrolling throughout the project area will ensure this.

Note on phasing of activities and indicators: While annual monitoring will be conducted across all activities, it is important to note that not all interventions and associated indicators will 
become effective from Year 1. Certain activities will be initiated gradually or later over the course of the project. As such, the monitoring framework captures both immediate outputs 
and longer-term outcomes. This phased approach ensures that progress is tracked consistently while recognizing that some impacts will only emerge later in implementation.

APPENDIX 10: SEA LEVEL RISE ANALYSIS 
 
Summary 
This study developed a century-scale (2025–2125) tidal wetland gain–loss model. Using IPCC AR6 
(2023) sea-level rise projections, predicted tidal range from EOT20 altimetry data, and local erosion 
rates, the model projects substantial long-term wetland change. Cumulative wetland losses increase 
from 19.1 ha in 2025 to 602.4 ha by 2125, while cumulative gains remain modest—rising from 3.1 ha 
in 2025 to a peak of only 25.5 ha by 2085 before declining to 19.5 ha in 2125. Persistent wetland 
area declines steadily from 2,942.7 ha in 2025 to 2,398.9 ha in 2125. The results indicate that 
wetland loss consistently outpaces gain throughout the century, leading to a net decline of ~543.8 ha 
in persistent coverage. 
 
Methodology and Input Data Exploration 
We implemented a spatial inundation model at 5-year time steps from 2025 through 2125. Each step 
incorporated projected sea-level rise (SLR), tidal flooding range, and shoreline erosion to classify 
areas as wetland or non-wetland, in order to identify loss, gain, and persistent tidal wetland areas 
(Figure 1). 
The general definitions of each category are as follows: 
● Loss area – An area that transitions from its current wetland area to fully inundated condition, 
even during low tide, due to environmental drivers such as sea-level rise, tidal inundation, or 
erosion. 
● Gain area – An area that transitions from a non-wetland to a wetland area because of an 
elevated tidal reference level resulting from sea-level rise and/or erosion. 
● Persistent area – An area that retains its wetland status, without conversion to a non-wetland 
state, and remains within the tidal range regime. 
For operational purposes during the modeling process, each category is defined as summarized in 
Table 1. 
 
Table 1. Operational Definitions for Modeled Tidal Wetland Change Categories (2025–2125) 
Category Operational Definition (2025–2125, 5-year intervals) 
Loss Area All 2024 existing land-use classes that, at each time step, are affected by the 
combination of SLR + tidal range + erosion, resulting in the loss of tidal-wetland 
status. 
Gain Area Pixels classified as non-mangrove in 2024 that, during the modeling process, 
transition into tidal wetland. 
Persistent 
Area 
Pixels classified as mangrove/nypa in 2024 that remain mangrove (never 
classified as Loss). 
 
SLR scenarios were based on the latest IPCC AR6 (2023) projections and related data (Garner et al. 
2022), giving total local rise of roughly +0.45 m (low-emissions) to +1.03 m (high-emissions) by 2125 
(Figure 2). For each year, we raised the effective water surface by the projected rise.  
434 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
The EOT20 global tide model (Hart-Davis et al., 2021) was used to represent tidal conditions for the 
study area. EOT20 was developed using multi-mission satellite altimetry data from 1992 to 2019, 
incorporating observations from eleven satellite altimetry missions. The model extends across the 
global ocean from 66° S to 66° N, with the FES2014b tide model applied to fill in the higher latitudes. 
In this study, tidal ranges for the period 2025–2125 were predicted using the EOT20 global tide 
model. Tidal prediction was performed using MATLAB and Python, specifically the MATLAB Toolbox 
Tide Model Driver (TMD) version 3.0 (Chad et al., 2023; Greene et al., 2024) and the Python-based 
plugin pyTMD (Padman et al., 2018; Sutterley et al., 2019). The predicted average maximum tidal 
range amplitude for the site was approximately 1.77 m (Figure 3). At each time step, any land lying 
below the combined high-tide level (sea-level rise + tidal range) was considered inundated (see 
Figure 1). 
 
Figure 1. Conceptual of spatial inundation model and considering parameters 
 
 
Figure 2. SLR scenarios based on the latest IPCC AR6 (2023) projections in the study area 
 
 
 
435 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4
 

      
CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4
 
 
Figure 3. Prediction of maximum tidal range amplitude 
 
In terms of coastal plains into slope zones, we treated the study area into very gentle slopes (<~1%) 
as most susceptible to inundation consistent with USGS coastal vulnerability guidelines. Coastal 
plain slope zones can be classified into very gentle (0.1–1% or ~0.06°–0.57°), moderate (1–5%), 
steep (5–20%), and very steep (>20%). Coastal erosion was applied uniformly along the seaward 
edge: following Nunukan’s BPBD 2023 report, we retreated the shoreline by 5–6 m per year. In 
practice, this means each 5-year step removed ~25–30 m of wetland at the seaward front, reducing 
landward area over time. 
 
Results 
Model outputs show both wetland losses and gains increasing over time, but with different 
magnitudes and temporal patterns (Table 2). Cumulative loss grows from 19.2 ha in 2025 to 564.7 
ha in 2125, representing an average loss rate of ~3.0 ha yr⁻¹ in the first half of the century and 
accelerating to ~7.5 ha yr⁻¹ in the final 50 years. Gains are minimal in the early decades—averaging 
<0.2 ha yr⁻¹ from 2025–2055—and reach a maximum of 25.6 ha in 2085 before declining to 19.5 ha 
by 2125. 
The magnitude of Gain can fluctuate over time because some areas that initially transition into tidal 
wetlands (classified as Gain) may subsequently be reclassified as either Persistent (if they remain 
wetlands without further change) or Loss (if they become fully inundated or eroded in later time 
steps). 
Persistent wetland area decreases from 2,944.8 ha in 2025 to 2,400.6 ha in 2125, indicating a total 
reduction of ~544 ha. Throughout the modeled period, wetland loss consistently exceeds gain, with 
the loss area roughly 10 times larger than gain in the early decades and remaining more than 20 
times greater by the end of the century. These findings highlight the long-term vulnerability of the 
tidal wetlands to sea-level rise and erosion, despite occasional localized wetland expansion.

APPENDIX 11: ASSESSMENT OF CARBON POOL 
SIGNIFICANCE 
 
VM0047 ARR Carbon Pool 
Aboveground and Belowground Non-Woody Biomass 
Project activities are not expected to result in any significant emissions from aboveground 
non-woody biomass. In the baseline scenario, the ARR project area consists of grassland and 
shrubs, young wood-shrubs, and old wood-shrubs. Only the grassland and shrubs and the young 
wood-shrubs will be affected by site preparation activities for planting. 
It is conservatively assumed that vegetation within these areas will be removed once, at the initiation 
of project activities, and only within the limited areas required to facilitate planting. Although the total 
extent of land associated with these areas is approximately 401 ha, only a small portion of this 
extent will be subject to clearing. 
The emission factors applied for estimating non-woody biomass loss are presented in Table 1 below. 
Based on the analysis and calculations, an estimated 765.43 tCO₂e of aboveground non-woody 
biomass and 180.55 tCO₂e of belowground non-woody biomass loss are expected for the entire 
project area. Together, these emissions represent approximately 0.71% of the total ex-ante GHG 
removals calculated for the ARR reforestation activities. 
As this contribution is below the 5% significance threshold, emissions from aboveground and 
belowground non-woody biomass are considered de minimis and therefore not significant for the 
purposes of this assessment. 
Table 1. Emission Factor for Land Preparation 
Land Cover 
Emission Factor (tC/ha) 
Aboveground Biomass Belowground Biomass 
Grassland & Shrubs 1.91 0.45 
Young woodshrubs 9.09 2.14
